Six Arguments on Divine Obligation and Grace
Sanusi enumerates six arguments and refutes each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six arguments concerning divine obligation and grace and why the author considers them invalid.

Core Idea
Not all possibilities obligate God; divine grace is not compelled by human reasoning.

In this passage, the author presents six arguments that some might use to claim that God is obligated to bestow grace or perform certain acts. Each argument is introduced and then refuted. The author systematically shows the weakness or invalidity of these arguments, emphasizing that divine actions are not bound by human expectations of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the six arguments and the author's refutations.
The passage is corrupted but the structure is clear.
2
Identify the six arguments
Notice each argument is introduced with 'وأما' and a number (first through sixth). List them as you read.
The arguments cover topics like possibility, obligation, sending messengers, will, knowledge, and protection.
3
Understand each refutation
For each argument, see what the author says in response. Often begins with a word like 'ضعيف' (weak) or a reason why it fails.
The refutations are brief; the author deems all arguments invalid.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The closing statement says these aspects are all invalid. Consider the theological implication that God is not obligated by human standards.
This reinforces divine sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اما الاول فضعيف لازم همهم ان دارها امكن في قدرة الله تعالى از فعل لهم من الانفاق فقد وجب عليه ذلك دور الوترك المطالبة الهند ولمار حاملا او شاینا و الشي الي مون في حاجة الى الدعا والتفرح في طلبه و اما الثاني من وفي الصالات النشيد يد و التكليف ان على الله ان له انرا و حمل المكان على الجسم کا ہونہ اللہ جیسی وان على أنه لا انزله في حمل المكلف على محلى الصبح تا به خود و كعدمه الركون العبد مطيعا و نيا نا اناملة في صرف التها اليه واما الذلة وهو ان تكون المرادلو الا موقع اللہ علینا کاسم التاسع معد بعدم جوا ہے و اما الرابع جوایمان لوگان علمه را نه قلص من السنة الامانه موقت ان عنقه في الحمال الكان علمه بأنه بكفر طوال عمره توجب عليهم ان لا تخلفه الله و اما امامی هوما على ابقا العقل فضلت أن هذه الآية متحكمة ما هو مذكور قبل من الاغنية وهو قوله ماما الدين قلوبهم ابل واما السادس هولة عمل ولها على الحراسة من النشطان ومن شرور النفس منقول ذكد ان كان مقدو الله وهذه وجب فعله ولا فايد اف ه طلبه و ان لم یکن مغز و ر ا ولا فاين في الدعا فظهر بادكرنا سقوط جمل هذه الوجوه
As for the first, it is weak and necessary that if its occurrence is possible in the power of God Almighty, then He did for them from the expenditure, then it is obligatory upon Him, turning and the claim of Hind, and it is not a carrier or something, and the thing that is in need of supplication and rejoicing in seeking it. As for the second, from the prayers, the urging, and the obligation, that God has that He sent down and carried the place on the body like His being, and that He does not send it down in carrying the obligated person to the place of the morning, with its own and the lack of the servant's resting obediently, and we intend to complete it in directing towards Him. As for the third, it is that the intended one should not fall upon us, like the name of the ninth, counted by the non-existence of his permission. As for the fourth, the belief of his knowledge, not a decrease from the second year, a temporary trust, his neck in the load, his knowledge that he disbelieves throughout his life obliges them not to fail God. As for the fifth, it is what is upon preserving the intellect, it is preferred that this verse is abrogated by what was mentioned before from the song, which is His saying: 'But those whose hearts are astray.' As for the sixth, it is a work and a guard from the devil and from the evils of the soul, transmitted that if it was possible for God, then it is obligatory to do it, and there is no benefit in seeking it, and if it is not possible, there is no benefit in supplication. So it appears that we have invalidated all these aspects.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents six arguments (وجوه) concerning divine obligation and grace (الالطاف). The author refutes each argument, showing that they are invalid. The first argument is that if an act is possible for God, it becomes obligatory, but the author calls this weak. The second argument concerns the obligation of sending messengers and carrying out obligations. The third argument deals with the will of God and His permission. The fourth argument involves God's knowledge of future disbelief. The fifth argument claims abrogation of a verse. The sixth argument is about protection from evil. The conclusion states that all these arguments are invalid.

Key Terms

الالطاف
التكليف
النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a certain act is possible for God, then He is obligated to perform it.
Correction: Possibility does not entail obligation; God acts according to His wisdom, not human expectations of necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the first argument regarding divine obligation?
A) It is strong and necessary
B) It is weak
C) It is abrogated
D) It is about miracles

Key Takeaway
The six arguments attempting to obligate God to grant grace are all invalid according to the author.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often pray fervently for something, assuming that because God is able, He must grant it. After reading this lesson, how does your sense of entitlement change? Have you ever felt disappointed thinking God was obliged to answer? Write a specific prayer you've held too tightly, and commit to releasing it to His will.
?
Do you sometimes believe your good deeds or knowledge earn you God's grace? This lesson shows that grace is not compelled. Where in your life have you acted as if you deserve God's favor rather than relying on His mercy? What would it look like to live today with no claim on God but total dependence on His grace?
?
If God's foreknowledge does not force Him to act, why does it still feel unsettling that He knows your future sins? How does this truth free you from despair over past failures and from presumption about future obedience? Make a decision now to trust His wisdom over your desire to control outcomes.

The Incomprehensibility of God's Essence
The author argues by analogy that God's essence is beyond human comprehension and only the via eminentiae is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human reason cannot fully comprehend God's essence and what path is available instead.

Core Idea
God's absolute dissimilarity makes comprehensive knowledge of Him impossible for human reason.

In this passage, the author establishes a foundational principle: God's essence is completely different from all created things. He then argues that if even the most obvious things are obscure, then knowledge of God is even more difficult. The only way to speak of God is by attributing to Him the most perfect qualities in a manner that befits His majesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the main premise
The author states that God's essence is different in quiddity and reality from all contingent things. This is the anchor of the argument.
This is a foundational Ash'ari principle.
3
Follow the analogy
The author draws an analogy: if the most apparent things are already obscure, then knowledge of God, who is infinitely dissimilar, must be even more difficult.
This is a fortiori reasoning: if something is true of the less, it is more true of the greater.
4
Understand the conclusion
Human intellects cannot fully encompass God; the only path is the via eminentiae — attributing to God what is worthier and more perfect.
This is not agnosticism but a positive theological method.
5
Address a common misunderstanding
Some think this passage denies all knowledge of God, but it only denies comprehensive knowledge and affirms the via eminentiae.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا فنقول: إن ذات الحق سبحانه مخالفة بالماهية والحقيقة لجميع أقسام الممكنات والمحدثات فإذا كان العلم بأظهر المعلومات قد بلغ في الخفاء والغموض إلى الحد الذي ذكرناه، فالعلم الذي بصفات الموجود الذي لا يشابه شيئاً من الممكنات، ولا يناسب شيئاً مع أنه في غاية البعد عن مناسبة المعقولات ومشابهة ما يصل إليه الفكر والذكر والوهم والخيال، لو كان صعباً عسراً كان ذلك أولى فيثبت أن هذا العلم الشريف أعلى وأجل من أن يحيط به العقل إحاطة تامة فلا سبيل للعقول البشرية فيه إلا الأخذ بالأولى والأخلق والأكمل والأفضل.
Let us say: The Essence of the Truth, exalted be He, is different in quiddity and reality from all classes of contingent beings and originated things. So if knowledge of the most apparent things has reached the limit of concealment and obscurity we have mentioned, then knowledge of the attributes of the Existent who is unlike any contingent being and does not resemble anything, while being at the extreme of distance from resembling rational things and what is reached by thought, remembrance, estimation, and imagination—if it were difficult and hard, that would be more appropriate. So it is established that this noble knowledge is too elevated and majestic for the intellect to encompass it completely, so there is no path for human intellects regarding it except adopting the worthier, the more appropriate, the more perfect, and the more excellent.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy and a fortiori. The author begins by stating a foundational principle: God's essence is absolutely dissimilar (mukhalifah fi al-mahiyyah) from all contingent beings. He then argues by analogy: if the most apparent things are already deeply obscure (as he claims), then knowledge of God, who is infinitely dissimilar from what we can grasp, is even more difficult. He concludes that human reason cannot fully comprehend God; the only path is to attribute to Him what is 'worthier, more appropriate, more perfect, and more excellent' (al-akhdh bil-awla wal-akhlaq wal-akmal wal-afdal). This is a classic Ash'ari principle of negative theology via the via eminentiae.

Key Terms
ذات الحق
المخالفة بالماهية
الأخذ بالأولى والأخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage denies any knowledge of God.
Correction: It denies comprehensive knowledge but affirms the via eminentiae as a valid path.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the only path for human intellects regarding knowledge of God?
A) Complete certainty
B) Adopting the worthier, the more appropriate, the more perfect, and the more excellent
C) Total agnosticism
D) Logical proofs alone

Key Takeaway
Human reason cannot fully comprehend God's essence; the only proper approach is to attribute to God the most perfect qualities in a fitting manner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life are you still trying to fully comprehend God, rather than humbly accepting that His essence is beyond your grasp? How might surrendering to His absolute otherness change your relationship with Him today?
?
This passage says only the path of 'the worthier, the more appropriate, the more perfect, the more excellent' remains for you. Are you truly content with that indirect knowledge, or do you secretly wish for a God that fits neatly into your own categories? What does that desire reveal about your faith?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you tend to imagine them as similar to human qualities only magnified? How can you actively train your mind to affirm God's perfections without falling into the trap of similarity, as this passage warns?

Divine Attributes

The Dilemma of Divine Attributes: Distinct or Identical?
Author presents a dilemma regarding divine attributes and Essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological dilemma regarding whether God's attributes are distinct from His Essence or identical to it, and why both alternatives appear problematic.

Core Idea
Both options—attributes distinct from or identical to the Essence—lead to contradictions.

This passage presents a classic theological puzzle (mushkil) in kalam. The author examines two possibilities regarding the relationship between divine attributes and the Essence. Each possibility, when examined closely, leads to a difficulty. This dilemma highlights the limits of human reason in comprehending the divine nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise and logical style of the argument.
2
Understand the first horn (distinctness)
The author argues that if attributes are distinct from the Essence, then God would be composed of multiple quiddities, making Him composite. Every composite is contingent, so the Necessary Being would be contingent—an impossibility.
This uses the kalam principle that compositeness implies contingency.
3
Understand the second horn (identity) and its two problems
If attributes are identical to the Essence, two problems arise: (1) each attribute can be known while being heedless of the Essence, which would be impossible if they were identical; (2) different attributes have different quiddities, so if all were identical to one Essence, the one thing would have multiple quiddities—impossible.
Both problems show that identity also leads to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الأول : إن تلك الصفات إن كانت مغايرة للذات كانت حقيقة الإله مركبة من ماهيات كثيرة، وكل مركب ممكن فالواجب لذاته ممكن لذاته، وهو محال وإن كانت الصفات عين الذات فهو أيضا مشكل لوجهين : الأول: إن كل واحد من ماهيات هذه الصفات، قد يعرف مع الذهول عن حقيقة الذات المخصوصة ولو كانت الذات عين الصفة، لامتنع ذلك . الثاني : إن لكل واحدة من الصفات ماهية غير ماهية الصفة الأخرى، فلو كانت الصفات عين الذات الواحدة، لكان الشيء الواحد لا يكون واحدا، بل ماهيات مختلفة. وهو محال .
The first station: if those attributes are distinct from the Essence, then the reality of God would be composed of many quiddities, and every composite is possible, so the Necessary in Himself would be possible in Himself, which is impossible. And if the attributes are identical to the Essence, that is also problematic for two reasons: First, each one of the quiddities of these attributes can be known while being heedless of the specific quiddity of the Essence, but if the Essence were identical to the attribute, that would be impossible. Second, each attribute has a quiddity different from the quiddity of another attribute, so if the attributes were identical to the one Essence, then the one thing would not be one, but rather different quiddities. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a theological dilemma (mushkil) regarding the relationship between God's attributes and His Essence. It is a problem statement. The author considers two possibilities: (1) If attributes are distinct from the Essence, then God would be composed of multiple quiddities (essences), making Him composite and therefore contingent, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. (2) If attributes are identical to the Essence, then two problems arise: first, each attribute can be known independently of the Essence, which would be impossible if they were identical; second, different attributes would not be distinct, implying that the one Essence would simultaneously be multiple different quiddities, also impossible. Thus both alternatives are impossible or problematic, highlighting the difficulty of understanding the divine attributes.

Key Terms
صفات
ذات
مغايرة
عين الذات
ماهية
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that either option (distinct or identical) is straightforward and unproblematic.
Correction: The author shows that both options lead to contradictions, highlighting a deep theological mystery.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given against the attributes being identical to the Essence?
A) It would make God composite
B) Each attribute can be known without the Essence
C) It would imply different quiddities
D) It would necessitate God's contingency

Key Takeaway
The relationship between divine attributes and Essence is a profound mystery that cannot be resolved by simple identification or distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just encountered a dilemma that human reason cannot resolve: either attributes are distinct from God’s essence or they are identical, and both lead to absurdity. Does this realization humble you? How do you respond when your mind fails to fully grasp the nature of Allah? Will you trust in His revelation despite this intellectual dead-end?
?
Consider the two problems with identity: you can think of God’s knowledge without thinking of His essence, and knowledge is different from power. If you were to assert that they are the same, would you be comfortable with that logical inconsistency? What does this teach you about the limits of your own conceptual tools when approaching the Divine?
?
After reading this passage, you might be tempted to say, 'So we cannot know anything about God's attributes.' But is that the conclusion? Or is the lesson that our language of distinct vs. identical is inadequate? How will this affect the way you speak about Allah's attributes from now on? Will you be more careful to avoid oversimplification?

The Difficulty of Conceiving God's Infinite Attributes
Author explains the difficulty of conceiving God's infinite knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human intellects find it difficult to conceive of God's infinite knowledge and power, and how this highlights the transcendence of divine attributes.

Core Idea
God's knowledge and power are infinite and all-encompassing, unlike any human experience.

This passage from Al-Matalibul Aliyah introduces a profound challenge in theology: how can we understand an attribute that embraces infinite details without being overwhelmed? The author points out that our own minds operate sequentially and are limited, making it hard to grasp the nature of God's knowledge and power. Let's explore this difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author describes both knowledge and power before stating the difficulty.
2
Identify the core challenge
The author contrasts divine attributes with human experience: a single attribute encompassing infinite details without distraction is unknown in our souls and intellects. Find where this contrast is made.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does this difficulty matter for theology? It reminds us not to measure God by human standards, preserving tawhid and transcendence.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني من المقامين المهيبين : إن علم الله تعالى لا بد وأن يكون محيطا بما لا نهاية له من الكليات والجزئيات، وقدرة الله تعالى لا بد وأن تكون نافذة في كل الممكنات، وإحاطة الصفة الواحدة بأمور لا نهاية لها على سبيل التفصيل مع أنه لا يشغله شان عن شان أمر ما وجدناه من نفوسنا وعقولنا، فكان تصور إدراك هذه المعاني صعبا على العقول البشرية .
The second station of the two awe-inspiring stations: Indeed, the knowledge of God Most High must encompass what has no end of universals and particulars, and the power of God Most High must penetrate all possible things. And the encompassing of a single attribute of things that have no end in detail—while He is not occupied by one thing from another—is something we have not found from our souls and intellects. So conceiving these meanings is difficult for human intellects.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the second knot (knowledge of attributes) by addressing a second difficulty (المقام الثاني). The author states that God's knowledge must encompass infinite universals and particulars, and His power must extend to all possibles. The problem is that a single attribute (like knowledge) encompasses infinite details without being occupied by one thing to the exclusion of another—unlike human experience. Since humans have not observed such a thing in themselves, it is very difficult for human intellects to imagine how this works. This highlights the transcendence of God's attributes beyond human analogy.

Key Terms
علم الله
قدرة الله
كليات
جزئيات
لا نهاية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God's knowledge is like human knowledge but larger.
Correction: The author stresses that God's attributes are utterly different in kind; they encompass infinite details without distraction, something no human experiences.

Checkpoint
What characteristic of divine knowledge and power does the author say is difficult for human intellects to conceive?
A) That they are limited to certain things
B) That they encompass infinite things in detail without distraction
C) That they are only general and not specific
D) That they are identical to the Essence

Key Takeaway
The infinite nature of God's attributes transcends human comprehension, reminding us not to measure divine attributes by our own limited experiences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that makes no sense to you, do you trust that God's all-encompassing knowledge has a purpose you cannot see, or do you let your limited understanding breed resentment? How can you actively surrender your need to comprehend fully today?
?
Have you ever unconsciously imagined God's knowledge as merely bigger than yours, rather than utterly different in kind? What specific thought pattern or habit in your life reveals that you have measured God by human standards? Repent and correct it now.
?
The author says it is difficult for human intellects to conceive of God's infinite attributes. Does this difficulty drive you to humble awe, or to dismiss what you cannot grasp? What step will you take today to train your heart to bow before the incomprehensible majesty of your Lord?

Divine Attributes

The Dilemma of Divine Attributes: Distinct or Identical?
The author presents the dilemma of attributes being either distinct from or identical to the essence, with two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical theological dilemma regarding divine attributes: the problems that arise whether they are considered distinct from or identical to the divine essence.

Core Idea
Every view on divine attributes seems to lead to impossibility.

Before we can affirm anything about Allah's attributes, we must confront a fundamental puzzle. If the attributes are separate from His essence, His reality becomes composite and thus contingent. If they are identical to His essence, problems of knowledge and unity arise. This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents that initial dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and the translation carefully. Notice the structure: first horn, then second horn with two reasons.
This is a classic dilemma.
2
Understand the first horn
The author argues that if attributes are distinct (mughayira) from the essence, then the divine reality becomes composite of many quiddities, leading to contingency. Understand why composite implies contingent.
3
Understand the second horn
If attributes are identical to the essence, two problems arise. First, each attribute quiddity can be known without knowing the essence. Second, each attribute has a distinct quiddity, so identity with one essence would make the one thing have multiple quiddities.
4
Reflect on the dilemma
Both options lead to impossibility. This shows the difficulty of conceptualizing the relationship between Allah's attributes and His essence.
This dilemma is not a denial of attributes but a demonstration of the limitations of human reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الأول : إن تلك الصفات إن كانت مغايرة للذات كانت حقيقة الإله مركبة من ماهيات كثيرة، وكل مركب ممكن فالواجب لذاته ممكن لذاته، وهو محال وإن كانت الصفات عين الذات فهو أيضا مشكل لوجهين : الأول: إن كل واحد من ماهيات هذه الصفات، قد يعرف مع الذهول عن حقيقة الذات المخصوصة ولو كانت الذات عين الصفة، لامتنع ذلك . الثاني : إن لكل واحدة من الصفات ماهية غير ماهية الصفة الأخرى، فلو كانت الصفات عين الذات الواحدة، لكان الشيء الواحد لا يكون واحدا، بل ماهيات مختلفة. وهو محال .
The first station: If those attributes are distinct from the essence, then the reality of the deity would be composed of many quiddities, and every composite is contingent, so the Necessary by itself would be contingent by itself—that is impossible. And if the attributes are identical to the essence, that too is problematic for two reasons: First: Each one of the quiddities of these attributes can be known while being heedless of the specific essence, and if the essence were identical to the attribute, that would be impossible. Second: Each attribute has a quiddity different from the quiddity of another attribute; so if the attributes were identical to the one essence, then the one thing would not be one, but rather different quiddities—that is impossible.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma about the divine attributes. The author states the first station of two. First, if attributes are distinct (mughayira) from the essence, then the reality of God would be composed of multiple quiddities (mahiyyat). Anything composite is contingent (mumkin), so the Necessary Being (wajib li-dhatihi) would become contingent, which is absurd. Second, if attributes are identical to the essence, that too is problematic for two reasons. Reason one: each attribute's quiddity can be known even when one is unaware of the specific essence; if essence were identical to attribute, that recognition wouldn't be possible. Reason two: each attribute has a distinct quiddity; if all were identical to one essence, then one thing would have multiple quiddities, contradicting unity. Thus both options are impossible, leaving a theological puzzle.

Key Terms
صفات
ذات
ماهية
مركب
ممكن
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that God has no attributes or that attributes are impossible.
Correction: The author is not denying attributes; he is showing that human conceptual frameworks cannot fully capture the divine nature. The dilemma is a theological puzzle, not a refutation of attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the first problem if attributes are distinct from the essence?
A) God would be composed of many quiddities, leading to contingency
B) Attributes become eternal separate entities
C) The essence becomes plural
D) Knowledge of attributes becomes impossible

Key Takeaway
The relationship between Allah's attributes and His essence is a profound mystery that human reason cannot fully resolve.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ponder the relationship between Allah's essence and attributes, do you feel the limits of your own intellect? How does that feeling change the way you approach the names and attributes of Allah in your daily worship?
?
This passage shows that human reason alone reaches an impasse. In what areas of your faith have you relied too much on your own understanding, and how can you cultivate more trust in divine revelation?
?
If you were to explain to a non-Muslim friend why Muslims affirm divine attributes despite this philosophical difficulty, what would you say? Would your explanation deepen your own conviction or raise more questions? How will you respond to that tension?

The Challenge of God's Infinite Knowledge and Power
The author raises the difficulty of God's infinite knowledge and power for human minds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human minds struggle to fully comprehend God's infinite knowledge and power as presented in this passage.

Core Idea
God's infinite knowledge challenges human reason, yet is a necessary belief.

In this passage, the author presents a profound difficulty: how can one singular divine attribute, like knowledge, encompass infinite details without distraction? This contrasts sharply with our human experience of limited attention. The author uses this contrast to highlight the transcendence of Allah's attributes, while affirming their reality.

Guided Path
1

These are the foundation of the difficulty.
3

The author says we have not found such encompassing in ourselves. Why does this make comprehension difficult? Consider the contrast between divine and human capacity.
This connects to the learning objective.

Proof That Dependency Is Not a Real Attribute
Proof that dependency is neither identical to the essence nor distinct from it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dependency (al-iftiqār) cannot be a real attribute distinct from the divine essence, using arguments from tautology, non-existence, infinite regress, and impossibility of describing non-existence with an attribute.

Core Idea
Dependency cannot be a real attribute—it would lead to tautology or absurdity.

This passage presents a sophisticated proof that the attribute of dependency (being in need) cannot be a real, distinct attribute of Allah's essence. The argument works by eliminating all possibilities: dependency is neither identical to the essence nor something other. By examining both options, the author shows the only coherent conclusion is that dependency is not a positive attribute at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense argumentation and careful elimination of possibilities.
2
Understand the first part: dependency is not identical to the essence
The author argues that if dependency were identical to the essence, statements about dependency would be tautological, like 'body is body'. But they are informative, so identity is false.
3
Understand the second part: dependency is not something other than the essence
Here the author splits the 'other' into two categories: non-existent concept and existent concept. Both are refuted in detail, with key arguments about infinite regress and impossibility.
Pay close attention to the argument against existent concept using infinite regress.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since dependency can be neither identical nor other, the very notion of dependency as a real attribute is false. This upholds the divine attribute of independence (al-ghinā).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الكلام قضية مفيدة ، وتشهد الفطرة بكون موضوعها مغايراً لمحمولها (١) .
وإن قلنا : الجسم جسم لم يكن كذلك ، وذلك يدل على المطلوب
وأما بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون ذلك الاحتياج والافتقار أمراً مغايراً للذات ، فلان ذلك المغاير ، إما أن يكون مفهوماً عدمياً أو وجودياً . والقسمان باطلان . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع كونه عدمياً . فلانا إذا قلنا هذا الشيء لا يفتقر إلى الغير ، ولا يحتاج إليه ، كان صريح العقل حاكماً بأن هذا المفهوم سلبي ، وإذا كان نفي الافتقار والاحتياج مفهوماً عدمياً ، امتنع أن يكون ثبوت الافتقار والحاجة مفهوماً عدمياً، ضرورة أن أحد طرفي النقيض يجب أن يكون ثبوتـيـاً ويكون الآخر
عدمياً .
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون المفهوم من الافتقار والاحتياج أمراً ثبوتيـاً
فلوجهين :
الأول : إنه لو كان أمراً ثبوتياً : لكان إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته ، أو
،
مكناً لذاته ، والأول باطل ، لأنه صفة للموجود الذي يكون ممكناً لذاته والصفة مفتقرة إلى الموصوف ، والمفتقر إلى الممكن أولى بالإمكان . والثاني أيضاً باطل ، لأن الاحتياج لما كان موجوداً ممكناً [ لذاته)] (٢) كان [ محتاجاً) (٣) إلى

.
والثاني : إن الموصوف بهذا الافتقار والاحتياج إلى الموجد ، إما أن يكون موجوداً ، [ أو لا يكون ، فإن كان موجوداً ] (٤) كان الموجود موصوفاً بالافتقار والاحتياج [ إلى الموجد والمؤثر ، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل وهو محال ، وإن كان معدوماً لزم اتصاف العدم المحض ، والنفي الصرف بالصفة الموجودة ، وهو
محال . فثبت بما ذكرنا ، أن الافتقار والاحتياج (١) ] لو حصل لكان ذلك إما عين تلك الذات وإما أمراً مغايراً لها ، والقسمان باطلان ، فالقول بثبوت الافتقار والاحتياج باطل
This statement is a useful proposition, and natural disposition testifies that its subject is different from its predicate. And if we say: the body is a body, it would not be so, and that indicates what is sought. And as for the demonstration that it is impossible for that need and dependency to be something other than the essence: because that other either is a non-existent concept or an existent concept. Both divisions are false. And we only say: it is impossible for it to be non-existent. Because if we say: this thing does not depend on another, and does not need it, sound reason judges that this concept is negative. And if the negation of dependency and need is a non-existent concept, it is impossible for the affirmation of dependency and need to be a non-existent concept, necessarily because one of the two opposites must be affirmative and the other negative. And we only say: it is impossible for the concept of dependency and need to be an affirmative thing, for two reasons: The first: if it were an affirmative thing, it would either be necessary in itself or possible in itself. The first is false, because it is an attribute of an existent that is possible in itself, and the attribute depends on the described, and what depends on the possible is more deserving of possibility. The second is also false, because if need is an existing possible [in itself], it would be [in need] of the bringer into existence, so the need of need would be additional to it, and infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. The second: the thing described by this dependency and need to the bringer into existence either exists [or does not; if it exists] then the existent is described by dependency and need to the bringer into existence and the effect, so attainment of the attained would follow, which is impossible; and if it is non-existent, then the description of pure non-existence and absolute negation with an existing attribute would follow, which is impossible. So it is established from what we have mentioned that dependency and need, if they occurred, would either be the same as that essence or something other than it, and both divisions are false, so the assertion of the affirmation of dependency and need is false.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a proof that dependency (al-iftiqār) cannot be a real attribute distinct from the essence. The proof proceeds in two main steps: First, the author shows that dependency cannot be identical to the essence. He argues that if it were identical, the statement 'the essence is dependent' would be a tautology like 'the body is a body', which is not a useful proposition. But in fact, the proposition is useful, indicating a real distinction. Therefore, dependency cannot be identical. Second, the author shows that dependency cannot be something other than the essence. He argues that if it were other, it would have to be either a non-existent concept (mafhūm 'adamī) or an existent concept (mafhūm wujūdī). The non-existent possibility is ruled out because the negation of dependency is clearly a non-existent concept, so its opposite, affirmation, must be existent. The existent possibility is then refuted by two lines of reasoning: (a) if dependency is an existent attribute, it must be either necessary or possible in itself. It cannot be necessary because it is an attribute of a possible being, and attributes depend on their subject. It cannot be possible because if it were a possible existent, it would itself need a cause, leading to an infinite regress of needs. (b) if the thing described by dependency exists, then describing an already existent thing with dependency is redundant (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil); if it is non-existent, then describing non-existence with an attribute is impossible. Thus both possibilities are absurd, so dependency cannot be other than the essence. The conclusion is that since dependency can be neither identical nor other, it cannot be a real attribute at all.

Correction: The proof shows that neither identification nor distinction is possible; therefore dependency cannot be a real attribute at all.

Checkpoint

A) Because it would require an infinite regress of needs.
B) Because it would make the essence dependent on itself.
C) Because it would contradict the principle of non-contradiction.
D) Because the essence is necessary whereas dependency is contingent.

Key Takeaway
Dependency cannot be a real attribute because it is neither identical to the essence nor something other, and both alternatives lead to impossibility.

?
The author argued that describing an already existing thing with need is redundant. You exist by Allah's will every moment. Do you live with constant awareness that your existence is perpetually borrowed, or do you act as if you own it? The Fourth Doubt: Priority and Posteriority of Dependency
Fourth doubt: dependency leads to impossible priority and posteriority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth doubt argues that attributing real dependency to a thing forces its essence into contradictory temporal ranks of priority and posteriority, thereby challenging the coherence of such dependency.

Core Idea
True dependency would make an essence both prior and posterior to its own need.

The fourth doubt presents a sophisticated logical problem: if a thing is truly dependent on a cause, then its essence must be prior to its need (as the subject of an attribute) and also posterior to its need (as the effect that follows the need). This contradiction suggests that the very notion of real dependency is incoherent. In this lesson, we unpack the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt statement
Carefully read the opening sentence of the doubt. It states the conclusion: if something is truly dependent, it would be prior and posterior to its need, which is impossible. Then read the translation.
Notice the logical structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the two reasons for priority
The author gives two reasons why dependency implies priority. Find each reason and understand how it argues that the essence must be prior to the need.
First reason: need is an attribute, and the described is prior to the attribute. Second: need is a relation that depends on the relata, so the essence must exist before the relation.
3
Understand the argument for posteriority
Now look at the argument that dependency implies posteriority. It traces a chain: effect after agent's act, act after need, so effect is after need. Therefore the essence (which is the effect) is posterior to its need.
This is a longer argument; note the transitive reasoning: if A after B and B after C, then A after C.
4
Recognize the contradiction and the objection
The author concludes that the same essence would be both prior and posterior to the same need, which is impossible. Then an objection is anticipated: perhaps the 'described' is the essence itself (the mahiyya). Note that the text cuts off before the reply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : لو صدق على شيء كونه مفتقراً إلى الموجد والمؤثر ، لزم كون تلك الماهية متقدمة بالرتبة على حصول هذه الحاجة ، وكونها متأخرة عن هذه الحاجة محال ، فوجب أن يكون القول بثبوت الاحتياج : محال .
إنما قلنا : إنه يقتضي التقدم لوجهين :
الأول : إن افتقاره إلى المؤثر واحتياجه إليه صفة (٢) من صفاته ، وحكم
من أحكامه ، وصريح العقل يقتضي بتقدم الموصوف على الصفة بالرتبة
والثاني : إن افتقار الشيء واحتياجه إلى الغير، نسبة مخصوصة بينه وبين ذلك الغير ، وتحقق النسبة بين الشيئين يتوقف على تحقق الشيئين (٣) ، فوجب أن يكون الافتقار والاحتياج متأخرا بالرتبة عن تحقق الذات المحكوم عليها
·
بالافتقار والاحتياج وإنما قلنا : إنه يقتضي التأخر ، وذلك لأن الافتقار إلى الفاعل إنما يحصل لأجل أن يجعله الفاعل متحققاً وحاصلاً وكائناً وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يكون تحقق الأثر وتكونه متأخراً عن تأثير الفاعل فيه، لكن تأثير الفاعل فيه متأخر عن
•
افتقاره واحتياجه إلى الفاعل ، والمتأخر عن المتأخر عن الشيء متأخر عنه فثبت : أن تحقق تلك الحقيقة المحكوم عليها بالافتقار والاحتياج إلى المؤثر يجب أن يكون متأخراً بالرتبة عن احتياجه إلى المؤثر ، فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو صدق على شيء كونه مفتقراً إلى المؤثر، ومحتاجاً إليه ، فإنه يلزم كون تلك الحقيقة متقدمة بالرتبة ، على حصول تلك الحاجة ، وكونها متأخرة عنه ، ومعلوم أن ذلك محال ، فوجب أن يكون القول بثبوت الاحتياج والافتقار باطلاً ومحالاً . ولا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال الموصوف بالافتقار والاحتياج الى المؤثر ، هو الماهية ،
The fourth doubt: If something is truly described as being dependent on the bringer into existence and the effect, then it would be necessary that that essence is prior in rank to the occurrence of this need, and that it is posterior to this need is impossible, so it is necessary that the statement affirming need is impossible. We only say that it necessitates priority for two reasons: The first: its dependency on the effect and its need for it is an attribute of its attributes and a judgment of its judgments, and sound reason necessitates the priority of the described over the attribute in rank. The second: the dependency of a thing and its need for another is a specific relation between it and that other, and the realization of the relation between two things depends on the realization of the two things, so it is necessary that dependency and need be posterior in rank to the realization of the essence that is judged with dependency and need. And we only say that it necessitates posteriority, because dependency on the agent only occurs so that the agent makes it realized, obtained, and existing. And if that is so, then the realization and coming-to-be of the effect must be posterior to the agent's influence on it, but the agent's influence on it is posterior to its dependency and need for the agent, and what is posterior to what is posterior to a thing is posterior to it. Thus it is established that the realization of that reality judged with dependency and need to the effect must be posterior in rank to its need for the effect. So it is established from what we have mentioned that if something is truly described as being dependent on the effect and in need of it, then it follows that that reality is prior in rank to the occurrence of that need and posterior to it, and that is known to be impossible. Therefore the statement affirming need and dependency is necessarily false and impossible. And it may not be said: why is it not permissible to say that the thing described with dependency and need to the effect is the essence...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the 'fourth doubt' (al-shubha al-rabi'a) which argues that the very notion of dependency (iftiqār) leads to a contradiction involving temporal priority and posteriority. The argument is structured as follows: If something is truly dependent on a cause (muʼaththir), then its essence (māhiyya) must be prior in rank to the need itself (because the need is an attribute that requires a subject), and also posterior in rank to the need (because the need is a relation that requires the cause to act, and the effect comes after the action, which is after the need). Thus the same thing would be both prior and posterior, which is impossible. Therefore, the assertion of dependency is false. The passage then anticipates an objection (wa lā yuqāl) suggesting that perhaps the thing described with dependency is the essence itself (al-māhiyya), implying a possible way to avoid the contradiction, but the text cuts off before the response can be completed.

Key Takeaway
The fourth doubt challenges the coherence of attributing real dependency to an essence by showing it leads to an impossible temporal paradox.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If the latter, does that make your reliance on God feel less real? Take a moment to admit honestly which is true for you.

The author concludes that dependency cannot be a real attribute of anything. Yet the Quran repeatedly commands you to be in need of Allah (e.g., 'O mankind, you are the needy ones toward Allah'). How do you reconcile this command with the logical conclusion of this lesson? Does it force you to rethink what 'need' means in a theological context? Write down one concrete way you will express your need for God today, knowing that it is not a real attribute but still a necessary attitude.
?
This doubt was raised by skeptics to deny the need for a Creator. The argument is subtle. It is easy to feel unsettled. But reflect: does your faith stand on such fine logical points, or on a direct sense of your own contingency? Without dismissing the intellect, ask yourself: 'If I cannot logically prove my dependency, would my trust in God still hold?' If your answer is yes, what is that trust rooted in? If no, what does that reveal about your foundation?

Refuting the Philosophers on Divine Knowledge
The author begins answering the objections, focusing on the seventh objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author rejects the philosophical claim that God is intellect, intelligent, and intelligible, and affirms that God's knowledge is a relation.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is a relation, not identical to His essence.

In this passage, the author begins his response to the seventh objection about infinite regress in God's knowledge. He anticipates two counterarguments: one from philosophers who identify God as intellect, intelligent, and intelligible, and one from mutakallimun who equate knowledge of knowledge with knowledge. He rejects the philosophers' view as absurd and reaffirms that knowledge is a relational state. This lays the groundwork for his full refutation of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author is answering the seventh objection. He first considers potential counterclaims from philosophers and theologians to dismiss them.
The passage is cut off mid-sentence, so the full argument continues on the next page.
2
Analyze the refutation of the philosophers
Notice how he argues that God's knowledge of blackness cannot be identical to His essence due to difference in quiddity (essence).
This refutes the claim that God is intellect, intelligent, and intelligible all one.
3
Identify the relational nature of knowledge
The author reiterates that knowledge is a relative state (hala nisbiyya), as proven earlier, leading to a relational definition of divine knowledge.
This sets up the distinction between God's knowledge of a thing and His knowledge of His own knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال هذا السؤال ، لا يتوجه إلى الحكماء ، لأنهم يقولون : إنه تعالى عقل وعاقل و معقول ، والكل واحد ، ولا يتوجه أيضا عن المتكلمين ، لأنهم يقولون : العلم بالعلم بالشيء نفس العلم بالشيء ، لأنا نقول : أما الكلام الذي حكيتم عن الفلاسفة فهو محض الطامات ، وذلك لأن العلم بالسواد مثلا عندهم صورة متساوية لذات السواد في الماهية ، وذات الله تعالى مخالفة للسواد في الماهية ، فلزم القطع بأن علمه تعالى بالسواد مغاير لذاته المخصوصة ، وكذلك القول في جميع المعلومات ، وأيضاً فقد دللنا بالبراهين القاطعة في أول هذا الكتاب على أن العلم حالة نسبية [ إضافية ] وإذا كان كذلك ، فنقول : علم الله تعالى بالسواد عبارة عن نسبة مخصوصة ، بين ذاته وبين السواد . وعلمه بكونه عالما بالسواد نسبة مخصوصة بين ذاته وبين النسبة الأولى ، والنسبة إلى أحد الشيئين
It cannot be said that this objection does not apply to the philosophers because they say that He is intellect, intelligent, and intelligible, all one; and it also does not apply to the mutakallimun because they say that knowledge of knowledge of a thing is the same as knowledge of the thing. For we say: As for the statement that you have narrated from the philosophers, it is pure nonsense, because knowledge of blackness, for example, according to them is a form identical to the essence of blackness in quiddity, and the essence of God is different from blackness in quiddity, so it necessarily follows that His knowledge of blackness is other than His specific essence. And similarly for all known things. Also, we have proven with decisive proofs in the beginning of this book that knowledge is a relative state [additive]. If that is so, we say: God's knowledge of blackness is an expression of a specific relation between His essence and blackness. And His knowledge that He knows blackness is a specific relation between His essence and the first relation. And the relation to one of two things
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage begins the response to the objections, specifically addressing the seventh objection about infinite regress in God's knowledge. The author anticipates a counterclaim that the objection does not apply to philosophers (who identify God as intellect, intelligent, and intelligible) or to mutakallimun (who say knowledge of knowledge is the same as knowledge). He rejects the philosophers' view as absurd, arguing that God's knowledge of blackness cannot be identical to His essence if the essence is different in quiddity. He then reiterates that knowledge is a relational state, as proven earlier. The passage is cut off mid-sentence as the page ends.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge is identical to His essence in the same way philosophers claim He is intellect, intelligent, and intelligible all one.
Correction: God's knowledge is a relative state, a relation between His essence and the known thing, as proven by decisive proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the philosophers' position that God is intellect, intelligent, and intelligible rejected?
A) Because it is impossible for God to be intelligent;
B) Because God's knowledge of blackness must be different from His essence;
C) Because the philosophers deny that God has knowledge;
D) Because the mutakallimun proved it false.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is a relational attribute, not identical to His essence, and is expressed as a relation between His essence and the known thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your every sin is a relation between His essence and that sin, does that make His knowledge more personal or more distant? How should this shape your repentance today?
?
The philosophers thought God's knowledge was identical to His essence, making it seem abstract. Have you ever felt that God's knowledge of you was impersonal or automatic? What specific situation in your life right now can you remind yourself that His knowledge is a direct relation to your very being?
?
The author says knowledge is 'relative state' — a connection. If you truly believed that God's knowledge of your thoughts is a real relation, not just an attribute, what secret thought would you be most ashamed of right now? What will you do about it before the day ends?

Refuting the Second and Third Divisions on Necessary Existence
Refutes two divisions regarding necessity of existence as part of essence or external attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of existence cannot be a part of the essence nor an external attribute without leading to logical contradiction or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Necessity cannot be composed or dependent, or it ceases to be necessary.

In this passage, the author addresses two misguided attempts to explain the necessity of Allah's existence. He shows that if necessity is part of the essence, the Necessary Existent becomes composed and thus contingent. If necessity is an external attribute, it becomes dependent and leads to an infinite regress. Both conclusions are impossible for the Necessary Existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the parallel structure: 'As for the second division...' and 'As for the third division...'.
2
Understand the refutation of the second division
The second division claims necessity is part of the essence. The author argues that this makes the Necessary Existent composed, and every composite is contingent, which is a contradiction.
The key logical move: composition implies contingency, so the Necessary Existent would be contingent, impossible.
3
Understand the refutation of the third division
The third division claims necessity is an external attribute. The author shows that an external attribute depends on the essence, making necessity itself contingent and requiring another cause, leading to infinite regress.
Infinite regress is unacceptable in proofs of God's existence.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that all three divisions (including the first, refuted earlier) are false. The only sound position is that necessity is neither a part nor an attribute but identical with the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : الوجوب الذاتي جزء من أجزاء تلك الماهية ، فهذا أيضاً باطل، لأن على هذا التقدير يكون واجب الوجود مركبا ، وكل مركب ممكن ، ينتج أن واجب الوجود لذاته ممكن لذاته ، وهو محال. وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : وجوب الوجود صفة خارجة عن الذات فنقول : هذا أيضاً باطل لأن كل ما كان صفة خارجة عن الذات لاحقة لها فهو مفتقر إلى تلك الماهية، وكل ما كان مفتقراً إلى غيره فهو ممكن لذاته ، فالوجوب بالذات ممكن بالذات، وكل ما كان ممكنا بذاته ، فإنه لا يجب إلا بوجوب علته ، فقبل هذا الوجوب وجوب آخر ، وذلك يوجب الذهاب إلى ما لا نهاية له. فثبت أن هذه الأقسام الثلاثة باطلة.
As for the second division: that one says that the essential necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) is a part among the parts of that essence, this is also false, because on this assumption the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) would be composed, and every composite is possible, resulting that the Necessary Existent by essence would be possible by essence, which is impossible. And as for the third division: that one says that the necessity of existence is an attribute external to the essence, we say: this is also false, because everything that is an attribute external to the essence and attaching to it is in need of that essence, and everything that is in need of another is possible by essence, so the necessity by essence would be possible by essence, and everything that is possible by essence does not become necessary except by the necessity of its cause, so before this necessity there would be another necessity, and that leads to an infinite regress. So it is established that these three divisions are false.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage refutes the second and third divisions concerning the nature of necessary existence (wajib al-wujud). The author addresses the second division, which claims that the necessity of existence is a part of the essence. He argues that if that were the case, the Necessary Existent would be composed, and every composite is contingent (possible), leading to a contradiction. Then he addresses the third division, which claims that necessity is an external attribute. He shows that an external attribute depends on the essence, making the necessity itself contingent, and thus requiring another cause for its necessity, leading to an infinite regress. The author concludes that all three divisions (including the first, refuted earlier) are false.

Key Terms
وجوب ذاتي
واجب الوجود
مركب
ممكن
صفة خارجة
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity can be an external attribute without causing contingency.
Correction: If necessity is external, it depends on the essence, making the necessity itself contingent, leading to an infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the outcome of the third division (necessity as external attribute)?
A) It leads to composition.
B) It leads to infinite regress.
C) It makes existence identical to essence.
D) It makes the Necessary Existent dependent on time.

Key Takeaway
All divisions that make necessity a part or attribute of the essence fail logically, leaving the only sound position that necessity is identical with the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself trying to 'add' something to Allah to make Him more perfect in your mind? How does this lesson's insistence on absolute simplicity challenge the way you conceive of His attributes?
?
If everything that depends on another is contingent, and you are dependent on Allah for every breath, how does that realization reshape your sense of self-reliance and need for Him right now?
?
The argument shows that a chain of causes cannot go on forever. Can you identify one area of your life where you are trusting in an endless chain of worldly causes instead of resting in the self-sufficient Necessary Existent?

Contingency and Permanence Are Not Superadded Attributes
Sanusi argues that contingency and permanence are not additional attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why contingency and permanence cannot be superadded attributes of the divine essence due to the infinite regress argument.

Core Idea
If contingency were an attribute, it would be contingent itself, leading to an infinite regress.

In this passage, the author continues his refutation of those who claim that contingency (ḥudūth) and permanence (baqā’) are attributes distinct from the essence. He argues that treating them as superadded leads to an absurd infinite regress, thus proving they are not separate attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the argument flow naturally.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Understand the conditional argument
The author assumes contingency is a superadded attribute and deduces that it would be contingent, leading to an added contingency, which causes an infinite regress.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
See the infinite regress consequence
Infinite regress is impossible; therefore the assumption is false. Contingency cannot be a superadded attribute.
The same reasoning applies to permanence.
4
Apply the same to permanence
The author says 'the same is said concerning permanence,' so the argument holds for both concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحدوث والبقاء فهما ليسا صفتين زائدتين على الذات، ولو كان الحدوث صفة زائدة لكانت حادثة، فيكون حدوثها زائدا عليها. ولزم التسلسل. وكذا القول في البقاء، وأما وجود واجب الوجود ووجود ممكن الوجود. فهما وإن كانا متساويين في مجرد كونه وجودا، إلا أن الاختلاف في اللوازم إنما حصل بسبب الماهية التي هي كالمحل لذلك الوجود، فإن ماهية الحق سبحانه لما اقتضت ذلك الوجود، امتنع زوال ذلك الوجود عن تلك الماهية، فهذا التفاوت إنما حصل بسبب الاختلاف في المحل والقابل. وأما ههنا فقد دللنا على أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون حالا في المحل، فظهر الفرق. قوله: لم قلتم إن التسلسل باطل؟ قلنا: لما سبق في الدليل الأول. قوله: الإشكال الذي أوردتموه في الموجب، قائم بعينه في القادر، فنقول: الكلام المستقصى في الفرق بين الموجب وبين المختار، سيجيء في مسألة القدم والحدوث. وهذا آخر الكلام في تقرير هذا الدليل.
And as for contingency (ḥudūth) and permanence (baqā’), they are not two superadded attributes upon the essence. If contingency were a superadded attribute, it would be contingent, so its contingency would be additional to it, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow. And the same is said concerning permanence. And as for the existence of the Necessary Being (wujūb al-wujūd) and the existence of the possible being (mumkin al-wujūd), they, although equal in mere existence, the difference in the necessary concomitants (lawāzim) occurs only due to the essence (māhiyya) which is like the place (maḥall) for that existence. For when the essence of the Truth—glorified be He—necessitated that existence, it became impossible for that existence to depart from that essence. So this disparity occurs only due to the difference in the place and the recipient (al-qābil). And as for here, we have proven that the body cannot be an accident in the place, so the difference becomes clear. [The objector] says: Why did you say that infinite regress is false? We said: Due to what preceded in the first proof. [The objector] says: The difficulty (ishkāl) that you raised against the necessitator stands exactly against the powerful (al-qādir) [agent]. We say: The detailed discussion of the difference between the necessitator and the free agent will come in the issue of pre-eternity and contingency (qidam wa ḥudūth). And this is the end of the discussion in the exposition of this proof.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage addresses several remaining points. First, the author argues that contingency (ḥudūth) and permanence (baqā’) are not superadded attributes. If contingency were an attribute, it would itself be contingent, leading to an infinite regress. The same applies to permanence. This refutes any claim that such attributes require a cause. Second, the author distinguishes between the existence of the Necessary Being (Wājib al-wujūd) and that of a possible being. Although both are existence, their concomitants differ due to their essences. The essence of God necessitates existence and its non-removal, while possible beings have essences that allow change. The author reiterates that body cannot be an accident in a place (as previously proven), so the disanalogy is clear. Then, two quick objections: one about infinite regress (tasalsul) and one about the argument applying to the powerful agent. The author refers to earlier and future discussions. This concludes the exposition of this particular proof.

Key Terms
حدوث
بقاء
صفة زائدة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contingency is a real attribute that exists as an additional quality of the essence.
Correction: If it were an additional attribute, it would itself be contingent, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore contingency cannot be a superadded attribute.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if contingency were a superadded attribute?
A) It would be eternal.
B) It would be contingent and lead to an infinite regress.
C) It would be necessary.
D) It would be a caused attribute.

Key Takeaway
Contingency and permanence cannot be superadded attributes because that would cause an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you treat your createdness (ḥudūth) as a mere label or as a reality that demands you are always dependent on the Creator? How does the argument against contingency as a separate attribute affect your understanding of your own contingency? Will you let this truth reshape your daily reliance on Allah?
?
If you were to argue that your existence is an 'additional property' added to your essence, you would fall into infinite regress. How does this logical impossibility help you recognize that your very being is dependent directly on Allah, without intermediary attributes? Are there areas in your life where you act as if you have independent existence apart from Him? Commit to identifying one such area today and actively remind yourself of your constant need for Him.
?
The same reasoning applies to permanence: it is not an added attribute. How does this challenge the notion that you can 'stay' or 'continue' in existence by your own power? What would change in your daily reliance on Allah if you truly internalized that your persistence every moment is directly from Him, not from any inherent quality? Decide on one specific moment this day when you will consciously pause and acknowledge that you exist only because He sustains you.

The Difference Between Necessary and Possible Existence
Sanusi distinguishes the existence of the Necessary Being from possible beings and answers objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of the Necessary Being and the possible being are equal in themselves but differ due to the essence (mahiyya) as a substrate, and how the author addresses two objections.

Core Idea
Existence is the same in both, but the essence determines the necessary concomitants.

This passage continues the discussion by contrasting the existence of the Necessary Being (Wājib al-wujūd) and possible beings. Although existence as such is the same, the attributes differ because of the essences that receive that existence. The author also defends his proof against two objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the distinction
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the key terms: wujūd, mahiyya, mahall, qābil.
2
Understand the equality and difference
The author says existence is equal in itself; the difference in necessary concomitants comes from the essence (mahiyya) which acts as a substrate (mahall).
God's essence necessitates permanence; possible essences allow change.
3
Notice the body argument
The author references a previous proof that body cannot be an accident in a place, reinforcing the distinction.
This shows the context is different.
4
Examine the two objections
First objection: why is infinite regress false? Answer: already proven in first proof. Second objection: difficulty applies to powerful agent too. Answer: deferred to future discussion on pre-eternity and contingency.
Recognize typical kalam methodology of postponing detailed answers.
5
Synthesize the overall purpose
This passage concludes the exposition of this particular proof, clarifying that existence differences are due to essence and answering immediate objections.

Seeing God with the Heart
Ali distinguishes between physical and spiritual vision of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ali ibn Abi Talib explains the vision of God as a spiritual vision by the heart, not physical eyesight.

Core Idea
For Ali, seeing God is a matter of heart-knowledge, not eye-sight.

In this exchange, Ali is asked the fundamental question: Have you seen your Lord? His answer is both startling and profound. He affirms that he does see his Lord, but then clarifies what kind of seeing he means. This passage introduces the concept of spiritual vision (ru'ya al-qalb) that lies at the heart of Islamic gnosis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the structure: question, answer, clarification.
2
Identify Ali's three statements
Ali says: 1) I do not worship a Lord I have not seen. 2) The eyes did not see Him. 3) The hearts saw Him by the realities of gnosis. What is the relationship between these statements?
3
Reflect on the meaning of 'seeing'
Ask yourself: Why does Ali equate not seeing with the eyes with a kind of seeing? How does heart-vision differ from physical sight?
This connects to the idea of certainty (yaqin) and spiritual insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع عشر : قيل لعلي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه : هل رأيت ربك؟ فقال : لا أعبد ربا لم أره . فقيل له : كيف رأيته ؟ فقال : ما رأته العيون بمشاهدة العيان ، ولكن رأته القلوب بحقائق العرفان . فقيل له : صف ربك . فقال : إن ربي لطيف الرحمة ، كبير الكبرياء ، جليل الجلالة ، قبل كل شيء ، وليس له قبل ، وبعد كل شيء ، وليس له بعد ، ظاهر لا بتأويل المباشرة ، باطن لا بالمساعدة ، سميع بلا آلة ، بصير بلا حدقة، لا تحده الصفات ، ولا تأخذه السنات ، القدم وجوده ، والأبد أزليته ، الذي أين الأين ، لا يقال له : أين ، والذي كيف الكيف ، لا يقال له : كيف.
The fourteenth: It was said to Ali ibn Abi Talib, may God be pleased with him: Have you seen your Lord? He said: I do not worship a Lord whom I have not seen. It was said to him: How did you see Him? He said: The eyes did not see Him by visual sight, but the hearts saw Him by the realities of gnosis. It was said to him: Describe your Lord. He said: My Lord is gentle in mercy, great in pride, majestic in glory, before everything, and there is nothing before Him, after everything, and there is nothing after Him, apparent not by interpretation of direct contact, hidden not by assistance, Hearing without an instrument, Seeing without a pupil, attributes do not limit Him, and the moments do not seize Him, pre-eternity is His existence, and everlastingness is His pre-eternity, He who is the 'where' of where, it is not said to Him: where, and He who is the 'how' of how, it is not said to Him: how.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage records a famous exchange with Ali ibn Abi Talib about seeing God. When asked if he has seen his Lord, Ali responds that he does not worship a Lord he has not seen. He clarifies that this seeing is not with physical eyes, but with the hearts through the realities of gnosis (haqa'iq al-'irfan). Then he is asked to describe his Lord, and he provides a series of negative and affirmative attributes that emphasize God's transcendence: He is before everything and after everything, yet nothing is before or after Him; He is apparent but not by direct contact, hidden but not by physical assistance; He hears without an instrument, sees without a pupil; attributes do not delimit Him, time does not seize Him; His pre-existence (qidam) is His very existence, and His everlastingness (abad) is His pre-eternity; He is the origin of 'where' and 'how', so one cannot ask 'where' or 'how' of Him. This is a classic statement of tanzih (divine transcendence).

Key Terms
رؤية القلب
حقائق العرفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ali claimed literal physical vision of God.
Correction: Ali explicitly denies physical vision ('the eyes did not see Him') and affirms heart-vision instead.

Checkpoint
How does Ali describe seeing God?
A) With physical eyes in a vision.
B) With the heart through the realities of gnosis.
C) In a dream.
D) Through logical proof alone.

Key Takeaway
Ali taught that God is seen not with the eyes but with the heart, through gnosis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ali said, 'I do not worship a Lord whom I have not seen.' Do you truly worship a God you have seen with your heart, or are you going through motions based on secondhand knowledge? What will you do this week to seek that living vision?
?
If your heart has not 'seen' God by the realities of gnosis, what are you really worshiping? Are you willing to ask God today for the gift of heart-vision, even if it means upending your comfortable religious habits?
?
You know facts about God, but does your heart know Him directly? Name one area of your life where your actions show you doubt His reality, and commit to changing that before tomorrow.

Describing God's Transcendent Attributes
Ali enumerates God's attributes of transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the attributes Ali uses to describe God and how they affirm His transcendence (tanzih).

Core Idea
Ali describes God using attributes that negate all limitations and establish His absolute otherness.

When asked to describe his Lord, Ali responds with a series of attributes that were to become foundational for Islamic theology. Each phrase carefully guards against anthropomorphism while affirming God's perfections. This passage is a classic statement of tanzih (divine transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic description carefully. Then read the translation to see how each attribute is rendered.
Notice the rhythm: each phrase is short and precise.
2
List the attributes mentioned
Go through the text and list each attribute or description Ali gives of God.
Look for phrases like 'لطيف الرحمة', 'ظاهر بلا مباشرة', etc.
3
Analyze the pattern
Notice how many attributes are negations (e.g., 'not by direct contact', 'no instrument') or affirmations that still imply transcendence (e.g., 'before everything, nothing before Him').
Consider the purpose: to prevent limiting God.
4
Understand key terms
Focus on 'القدم وجوده' (pre-eternity is His existence), 'الأبد أزليته' (everlastingness is His pre-eternity), and 'أين الأين', 'كيف الكيف'.
These terms show that God is beyond time, place, and quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع عشر : قيل لعلي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه : هل رأيت ربك؟ فقال : لا أعبد ربا لم أره . فقيل له : كيف رأيته ؟ فقال : ما رأته العيون بمشاهدة العيان ، ولكن رأته القلوب بحقائق العرفان . فقيل له : صف ربك . فقال : إن ربي لطيف الرحمة ، كبير الكبرياء ، جليل الجلالة ، قبل كل شيء ، وليس له قبل ، وبعد كل شيء ، وليس له بعد ، ظاهر لا بتأويل المباشرة ، باطن لا بالمساعدة ، سميع بلا آلة ، بصير بلا حدقة، لا تحده الصفات ، ولا تأخذه السنات ، القدم وجوده ، والأبد أزليته ، الذي أين الأين ، لا يقال له : أين ، والذي كيف الكيف ، لا يقال له : كيف.
The fourteenth: It was said to Ali ibn Abi Talib, may God be pleased with him: Have you seen your Lord? He said: I do not worship a Lord whom I have not seen. It was said to him: How did you see Him? He said: The eyes did not see Him by visual sight, but the hearts saw Him by the realities of gnosis. It was said to him: Describe your Lord. He said: My Lord is gentle in mercy, great in pride, majestic in glory, before everything, and there is nothing before Him, after everything, and there is nothing after Him, apparent not by interpretation of direct contact, hidden not by assistance, Hearing without an instrument, Seeing without a pupil, attributes do not limit Him, and the moments do not seize Him, pre-eternity is His existence, and everlastingness is His pre-eternity, He who is the 'where' of where, it is not said to Him: where, and He who is the 'how' of how, it is not said to Him: how.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage records a famous exchange with Ali ibn Abi Talib about seeing God. When asked if he has seen his Lord, Ali responds that he does not worship a Lord he has not seen. He clarifies that this seeing is not with physical eyes, but with the hearts through the realities of gnosis (haqa'iq al-'irfan). Then he is asked to describe his Lord, and he provides a series of negative and affirmative attributes that emphasize God's transcendence: He is before everything and after everything, yet nothing is before or after Him; He is apparent but not by direct contact, hidden but not by physical assistance; He hears without an instrument, sees without a pupil; attributes do not delimit Him, time does not seize Him; His pre-existence (qidam) is His very existence, and His everlastingness (abad) is His pre-eternity; He is the origin of 'where' and 'how', so one cannot ask 'where' or 'how' of Him. This is a classic statement of tanzih (divine transcendence).

Key Terms
لطيف الرحمة
ظاهر بلا مباشرة
باطن بلا مساعدة
القدم
الأبد
أين الأين
كيف الكيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that describing God with attributes like 'hearing' and 'seeing' implies He has physical organs.
Correction: Ali clarifies that God hears 'without an instrument' and sees 'without a pupil', negating any physicality.

Checkpoint
What does Ali mean when he says 'القدم وجوده' (pre-eternity is His existence)?
A) Pre-eternity is a created thing.
B) Pre-eternity is an attribute separate from God.
C) Pre-eternity is identical with God's existence.
D) Pre-eternity is a measure of time.

Key Takeaway
Ali's description of God establishes the principle of tanzih: God is beyond all human categories and limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever imagine God's 'hearing' as if it were like your own? What would change in your worship if you truly internalized that He hears without any instrument?
?
You believe God is 'before everything' and 'after everything'—but do you live as if He is bound by your time? What would it mean to reorient your life around a God who is not subject to past, present, or future?
?
The phrase 'لا يقال له أين' (it is not said to Him: where) confronts every attempt to locate God. Where in your life have you tried to confine God to a place—a mosque, a moment, a feeling? What would it take to let go of that security?
