God's Creation of Senses: From a Leaf to Sight and Hearing
A man marvels at a green leaf from dry wood, then a dream, and a prophetic prostration prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the growth of a leaf and the gift of senses both point to God as the sole Creator and Giver of life.

Core Idea
God's creative power is seen in both a green leaf and the human senses.

This lesson presents a vivid story of a man who, upon seeing a leaf, wonders about its origin. His dream provides an answer that connects the leaf's growth to the creation of sight. The Prophet's prostration prayer and Ali's praise further reinforce that God is the Creator of hearing, sight, and speech. Through these examples, we see how everyday phenomena and our own bodies testify to divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the story and the dream response
Read the passage from the beginning up to the dream. Notice the man's wonder and the dream's answer: the One who grew the leaf also opened the eye to sight.
The dream is an illustration, not a claim of divine revelation.
2
Examine the Prophet's prostration prayer
Read the prayer quoted from the Prophet. Identify the phrase 'opened its hearing and sight' and understand that prostrating acknowledges God as the Creator of these senses.
3
Reflect on Ali's saying
Read Ali's praise: 'sight with fat, hearing with bone, speech with flesh.' Notice how he credits God for creating senses from physical materials.
This shows God's power in using humble substances for noble faculties.
4
Connect the three examples
Consider how all three point to one truth: God alone creates and gives life, from leaves to senses. Reflect on the unity of divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس عشر : كان رجل مضطجعاً في ظل شجرة فسقطت ورقة من تلك الشجرة على وجهه ، فتعجب من كيفية تولد هذه الورقة الخضراء الناعمة من تلك الخشبة اليابسة الكثيفة ، فقال : من الذي أنبت الورق على الشجر ؟ فنام فرأى في منامه كأن قائلاً يقول : الذي أنبت الورق على الشجر الذي شق على الوجه البصر. ويقرب من هذه الحكاية أن النبي : كان يقرأ في سجدة التلاوة : سجد وجهي للذي خلقه وصوره وشق سمعه وبصره بحوله وقوته ، وكان علي ابن أبي طالب يقول : سبحان من بصرني بشحم ، وأسمعني بعظم ، وأنطقني بلحم
The sixteenth: A man was lying in the shade of a tree when a leaf from that tree fell on his face. He marveled at how this soft green leaf came from that dry thick wood, and said: Who made the leaves grow on the tree? Then he slept and saw in his dream as if a speaker said: The One who made the leaves grow on the tree is the One who opened the eye to sight. Close to this story is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, used to recite in the prostration of recitation: 'My face prostrates to the One who created it, formed it, and opened its hearing and sight by His power and strength.' And Ali ibn Abi Talib used to say: 'Glory be to the One who gave me sight with fat, and hearing with bone, and speech with flesh.'
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a proof of God's creativity and a description of His giving sight, hearing, and speech. It begins with a man witnessing a leaf and wondering who made it. In a dream, the answer is that the same God who made the leaf also gave the eye its ability to see. The author then relates this to the Prophet's prostration prayer, acknowledging God as the creator and giver of senses. Ali's saying similarly praises God for creating sight, hearing, and speech from physical materials.

Key Terms
سجدة التلاوة
شق السمع والبصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dream in the story is a literal divine inspiration or prophetic revelation.
Correction: The dream is a rhetorical device used to illustrate the point that the same God who creates leaves also creates senses. It is not a claim of prophecy but a teaching story.

Checkpoint
What does the Prophet say in the prostration of recitation?
A) My face prostrates to the One who created it, formed it, and opened its hearing and sight.
B) My heart prostrates to the One who guided me.
C) My soul prostrates to the One who forgives.
D) My body prostrates to the One who heals.

Key Takeaway
God is the Creator of all things, from the leaf on a tree to the faculties of sight, hearing, and speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own eyes, do you see them as a mere biological accident, or do you recognize that God 'opened' them for you to see? How does this change your gratitude today?
?
The man marveled at a leaf falling from a tree. When did you last pause to marvel at God's creation in something as simple as a leaf? Will you make a habit of such reflection starting now?
?
The Prophet prostrated saying, 'My face prostrates to the One who created it and opened its hearing and sight.' Does your prostration in prayer come from that same conscious realization, or is it just a habit? What will you do to make your sujud more alive to this truth?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
Knowing Self Through Opposition
Explanation of the tradition by contrasts of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how recognizing your own contingent and deficient attributes leads to knowledge of Allah's perfect attributes through the principle of opposition (mukhālafa).

Core Idea
Recognizing your own limitations reveals the limitless perfection of Allah.

This lesson unpacks a profound tradition: 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord.' But how exactly? The scholars of verification (ahl al-tahqīq) explain it is not by seeing a likeness but by contrast. By examining our own contingent, deficient characteristics, we infer their perfect opposites in Allah. This passage lays out four key contrasts, forming a simple yet powerful argument from opposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the tradition and orientation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the tradition from the Prophet and the Torah verse. Understand that the scholars provide the interpretative key.
The tradition is well-known; the commentary clarifies its meaning.
2
Understand 'mukhālafa'
Focus on the phrase 'not by way of equality but by way of opposition.' This is the core principle: we know God by contrast, not by similarity.
Contrast with pantheistic or incarnationist interpretations.
3
Examine the four pairs of attributes
Go through each pair: temporality vs eternity, contingency vs necessity, composition vs unity, ignorance/incapacity/need vs knowledge/power/self-sufficiency. Notice how each human limitation points to a divine perfection.
The pairs cover key aspects of Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the method of analogizing
The passage ends with 'upon this category, analogize.' Consider how this method can be applied to other attributes, such as our mortality pointing to Allah's immortality.
This shows the argument is extendable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع عشر : روي عن النبي أنه قال : « من عرف نفسه فقد عرف ربه ، وجاء في التوراة : « يا إنسان اعرف نفسك تعرف ربك ) قال أهل التحقيق : من عرف نفسه فقد عرف ربه ، لا بطريق المساواة بــل بطريق المخالفة ، يعني من عرف نفسه بالحدوث، عرف ربه بالقدم ، ومن عرف نفسه بالامكان عرف ربه بالوجوب ومن عرف نفسه بالتركيب [ والتأليف] (۳) عرف ربه بالوحدانية والفردانية ومن عرف [ نفسه ](٤) بالجهل والعجز والحاجة ، عرف ربه بالعلم والقدرة والاستغناء. وعلى هذا الباب فقس
The nineteenth: It is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord.' And it came in the Torah: 'O man, know yourself, you will know your Lord.' The people of verification said: Whoever knows himself knows his Lord, not by way of equality but by way of opposition. That is, whoever knows himself by temporality (huduth) knows his Lord by eternity (qidam); whoever knows himself by contingency (imkan) knows his Lord by necessity (wujub); whoever knows himself by composition (tarkib) [and arrangement] knows his Lord by unity (wahdaniyya) and singularity (fardaniyya); and whoever knows himself by ignorance, incapacity, and neediness knows his Lord by knowledge, power, and self-sufficiency. And upon this category, analogize.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a well-known tradition and a Torah verse about self-knowledge leading to knowledge of God. The 'people of verification' (ahl al-tahqiq) interpret this not as knowing God by likeness (musawât) but by opposition (mukhâlafa). The method is: by recognizing one's own deficient attributes, one infers the perfect opposite attributes of God. Four pairs are given: our temporality implies His eternity; our contingency implies His necessity; our composition implies His unity and singularity; our ignorance, incapacity, and need imply His knowledge, power, and self-sufficiency. The passage concludes by saying 'analogize upon this category', meaning this method can be extended to other attributes. This is a classic argument from contrast (dalil al-tadadd) in kalam.

Key Terms
معرفة النفس
المخالفة
الحدوث
القدم
الإمكان
الوجوب
التركيب
الوحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'knowing self' means achieving unity with God or that the soul is a part of God.
Correction: The passage explicitly says it is 'by way of opposition' (mukhālafa), not equality or identity. Our limitations point to God's perfections, not to any similarity.

Checkpoint
According to the explanation, how do we know God by knowing ourselves?
A) By seeing the image of God within us.
B) By recognizing our own imperfections and inferring God's perfect opposites.
C) By realizing we are part of God.
D) By meditating on the self until we become God.

Key Takeaway
The method of mukhālafa (opposition) allows us to infer Allah's perfect attributes from our own deficiencies, and this principle can be applied to many other attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Of the four pairs of opposites in this passage (temporality vs. eternity, contingency vs. necessity, composition vs. unity, deficiencies vs. perfections), which one strikes you most personally? In what specific way do you avoid confronting that limitation in yourself, and how does that avoidance keep you from truly knowing your Lord? Today, what one concrete step will you take to face that limitation head-on?
?
The passage says 'upon this category, analogize.' That means you are meant to apply this method to every area of your life. Pick an attribute you possess that is not listed—such as your anger, your pride, or your desire for control. What does its deficiency in you reveal about Allah? Are you willing to look at that flaw not with shame but as a window to His perfection? If not, what fear holds you back?

Divine Attributes

Two Categories of Divine Attributes in Scripture
The author classifies divine attributes into two types with scriptural examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between anthropomorphic attributes (صفات الأعضاء) and attributes of meaning (صفات المعاني) in the Torah, Gospel, and Quran, and recognize that both categories appear in all three scriptures.

Core Idea
Scripture contains both anthropomorphic and non-anthropomorphic descriptions of God.

The author begins by observing that a reader of the Torah, Gospel, and Quran encounters two distinct types of verses about God: those that describe Him in bodily terms (like hands or a throne) and those that describe His attributes of meaning (like power, mercy, and knowledge). This lesson walks through the examples he provides from each scripture, setting the stage for understanding how apparent contradictions between these categories are resolved in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage thoroughly
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the author's clear structure: he first states the two categories, then gives examples from each scripture in order.
Pay attention to how he introduces each category before citing verses.
2
Identify the two categories
Spot the terms 'صفات الأعضاء' (attributes of members) and 'صفات المعاني' (attributes of meanings). Understand that the first implies bodily features, the second refers to non-bodily perfections.
The author also notes that verses negating likeness to God (ليس كمثله شيء) negate the anthropomorphic attributes.
3
Examine the Torah examples
Look at the example of 'finger of God' from Exodus 8:19 as an anthropomorphic attribute, and the description of God as merciful, gracious, etc. from Exodus 34:6-7 as attributes of meaning.
The author explains that 'finger of God' literally implies a body, but the context shows it is a metaphor for God's power.
4
Examine the Gospel examples
Read Jesus' words about heaven as God's throne and earth as His footstool (Matthew 5:34-35) as anthropomorphic, and His prayer in John 17:2-4 as attributes of meaning (power, glory).
Notice the author uses Jesus' own words to affirm God's perfection.
5
Examine the Quranic examples
See 'the hand of Allah' (Surah Al-Fath 10) as an anthropomorphic attribute, and the verse about destroying towns (Surah An-Naml 51) as affirming God's power, justice, and knowledge.
The author concludes that these examples show both categories exist in all three scriptures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقارىء للتوراة والإنجيل وللقرآن يجد آيات تدل على صفات أعضاء الله عز وجل ، ويجد آيات تدل على صفات معاني الله عزّ وجل. ويجد آيات تدل على أن الله تعالى ليس كمثله شيء . والآيات التي تنفي المماثلة هي تنفي أيضاً صفات الأعضاء الله عز وجل . 1 - ففي التوراة في قصة موسى - عليه السلام - مع السحرة . لما لم يستطيعوا إخراج البعوض من أرض مصر : و قال العرافون لفرعون : هذا إصبع الله ، [ خروج ۸ : (۱۹] أي البعوض قد أشتد اذاء بقدرة الله تعالى . وظاهر نص و إصبع الله ، يدل على أن الله جسم، وهو شبيه بالبشر . . واصبع الله ، صفة عضو . فإن الأصابع أعضاء من الجسم. وفي التوراة صفات المعاني اللائقة بجلال الله تعالى ، فيها صفات : القدرة والإرادة والعلم والسمع والبصر والحياة والقدم والبقاء ، وهكذا سائر الصفات التي تدل على أنه ذات مقدسة عن كل نقص ، ومبرأة من كل عيب ، مثل قوله : « الرب إله رحيم ، ورؤوف . بطيء الغضب، وكثير الإحسان والوفاء ، حافظ الإحسان إلى ألوف ، غافر الإثم = والمعصية والخطية ، ولكنه لن يبرى، إبراء : [ خروج ٣٤ : ٧٠٦]. ٢ - وفي الإنجيل نجد مثل ما في التوراة عن صفات الأعضاء وصفات المعاني . فعن صفات الأعضاء يقول عيسى - عليه السلام - : ولا تخلفوا البتة . لا بالماء لأنها كرسي الله . ولا بالأرض لأنها موطىء قدميه ، [ متى ٥ : ٣٤ - ٣٥ ] فظاهر النص يدل على أن الله تعالى يجلس على كرسي في السماء ، وان قدميه على الأرض . والجلوس يستلزم الجسمية والقدمان من صفات الأعضاء . وعن صفات المعاني نجد آيات كثيرة نصف الله تعالى كما وصفته التوراة بالقدرة والإرادة .. الخ ومن ذلك قول عيسى - عليه السلام - الله عزّ وجلّ أنه أعطاء سلطاناً على كل جسد ليعطى حياة أبدية لكل من يريد الله إعطاءه : : وهذه هي الحياة الأبدية أن يعرفونك أنت الإله الحقيقي وحدك ويسوع المسيح الذي أرسلته أنا مجدتك على الأرض ) [ يوحنا ١٧ : ٢ - ٤] فاعتراف عيسى - عليه السلام ـ أن الله تعالى قد اعطاه سلطاناً يدل على أنه قادر واعترافه بأنه مجد الله وعظمه يدل على أن الله متصف بكل كمال ومنزه عن كل نقص . - وفي القرآن الكريم عن صفات الأعضاء : و يد الله فوق أيديهم ، [ الفتح ١٠] وعن صفات المعاني : أنا بمرناهم وقومهم أجمعين . فتلك بيوتهم خاوية بما ظلموا . إن في ذلك لآية لقوم يعلمون) [النمل ٥١] ففي هذا النص إثبات كامل القدرة وأنه عادل وأنه يعلم ذلك ويشهد على ما حدث منه العالمون بحقائق التاريخ .
And the reader of the Torah, the Gospel, and the Quran finds verses that indicate attributes of members [limbs] of God - Mighty and Majestic - and finds verses that indicate attributes of meanings of God - Mighty and Majestic. And he finds verses that indicate that God - Exalted is He - is not like anything. And the verses that negate likeness also negate the attributes of members of God - Mighty and Majestic. 1 - In the Torah, in the story of Moses - peace be upon him - with the sorcerers. When they could not bring out the gnats from the land of Egypt: 'And the magicians said to Pharaoh, "This is the finger of God."' [Exodus 8:19], meaning the gnats had become severely harmful by the power of God. And the apparent text 'the finger of God' indicates that God is a body, similar to humans. And 'the finger of God' is an attribute of a member, for fingers are members of the body. And in the Torah there are attributes of meanings befitting the majesty of God, including attributes: power, will, knowledge, hearing, sight, life, pre-eternity, duration, and so on all attributes that indicate that He is a holy essence free from every deficiency and absolved from every defect, such as His saying: 'The LORD, the LORD, a God merciful and gracious, slow to anger, and abounding in steadfast love and faithfulness, keeping steadfast love to thousands, forgiving iniquity and transgression and sin, but who will by no means clear the guilty' [Exodus 34:6-7]. 2 - In the Gospel we find similar to what is in the Torah regarding attributes of members and attributes of meanings. For attributes of members, Jesus - peace be upon him - says: 'Do not swear at all: neither by heaven, for it is God's throne; nor by the earth, for it is His footstool' [Matthew 5:34-35]. So the apparent text indicates that God sits on a throne in heaven, and His feet are on the earth. And sitting necessitates being a body, and the feet are among the attributes of members. And regarding attributes of meanings, we find many verses describing God as the Torah described Him with power, will, etc. Among that is Jesus's saying - peace be upon him - that God - Mighty and Majestic - gave him authority over all flesh to give eternal life to all whom God wants to give: 'And this is eternal life, that they may know You, the only true God, and Jesus Christ whom You have sent. I have glorified You on the earth.' [John 17:2-4]. So Jesus's acknowledgement - peace be upon him - that God gave him authority indicates that He is powerful, and his acknowledgement that he glorified God and magnified Him indicates that God is characterized with every perfection and transcendent above every deficiency. - In the Noble Quran, regarding attributes of members: 'The hand of Allah is above their hands' [Al-Fath 10]; and regarding attributes of meanings: 'And We had certainly destroyed their towns and their people. So those are their houses, desolate because of their wrongdoing. Indeed in that is a sign for a people who know' [An-Naml 51]. So in this text is the affirmation of complete power, and that He is just, and that He knows that, and bears witness to what happened from those knowledgeable of historical realities.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage classifies the divine attributes found in scriptures into two categories: attributes of members (صفات الأعضاء) and attributes of meanings (صفات المعاني). The author first notes that the reader of Torah, Gospel, and Quran will find both types. Then he provides examples. From the Torah: the phrase 'finger of God' is an attribute of a member, implying a body, while verses describing God as merciful, gracious, etc., are attributes of meanings. From the Gospel: Jesus' words about heaven as God's throne and earth as His footstool are attributes of members, while Jesus' prayer in John 17 affirms God's power and glory, attributes of meanings. From the Quran: 'the hand of Allah' is an attribute of a member, while the verse about destroying towns shows God's power and justice, attributes of meanings. The author does not yet resolve the apparent contradiction but merely identifies the presence of both types of verses in each scripture. The purpose is to set up the problem that will be addressed in the next lesson.

Key Terms
صفات الأعضاء
صفات المعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all biblical verses are anthropomorphic and that the Bible only describes God in human-like terms. The author shows that both the Torah and Gospel also contain verses describing God's attributes of meaning (e.g., mercy, power, knowledge).
Correction: The author demonstrates that each scripture contains both types: anthropomorphic passages (like 'finger of God' or 'God's throne') and passages that describe God's non-bodily perfections (like mercy and power). The presence of attributes of meaning shows that anthropomorphic descriptions are not literal.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is an example of an attribute of a member (صفة عضو) from the Torah?
A) 'The LORD is merciful and gracious.'
B) 'This is the finger of God.'
C) 'God is powerful.'
D) 'God knows all things.'

Key Takeaway
Both anthropomorphic and non-anthropomorphic attributes appear in the Torah, Gospel, and Quran; understanding this distinction is essential for properly interpreting scriptural descriptions of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'the hand of God' in the Quran or other scriptures, do you catch yourself forming a mental image of a hand? How will you actively remind yourself that God is unlike anything, and turn your focus to the attribute of power instead?
?
The lesson shows that both anthropomorphic and abstract descriptions of God appear in the Torah, Gospel, and Quran. Does this challenge any assumptions you held about the nature of revelation? How will you use this distinction to read scriptural descriptions of God more carefully in your personal study?
?
If you have ever felt uncomfortable with verses that seem to describe God in human terms, how does knowing that the same scriptures also include verses negating likeness help you gain confidence in tawheed? What concrete step will you take this week to align your understanding of God with the principle that 'nothing is like Him'?

The Muslim Creed Has Three Scriptural Proofs
Author asserts scriptural basis for Islamic creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Muslim creed of Tawhid and Tanzih is supported by proofs from the Quran, Torah, and Gospels.

Core Idea
The Muslim creed has evidence from all three Abrahamic scriptures.

The author begins by asserting that the beliefs of Tawhid (monotheism) and Tanzih (divine transcendence) are not baseless. They are supported by the Quran, the Torah, and the Gospels. He clarifies that by 'Gospel' he means the four canonical Gospels. He also reminds us that he previously presented Surah al-Ikhlas as a Quranic proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the first sentence that states the Muslim creed has proofs from three scriptures.
Note the author's careful definition of 'Gospel'.
2
Understand the Quranic reference
The author refers to Surah al-Ikhlas as a previously mentioned proof. Reflect on why this surah is a concise proof of Tawhid and Tanzih.
If you are not familiar with the surah, it declares God's absolute oneness and transcendence.
3
Prepare for detailed citations
This lesson sets the stage for the detailed Torah and Gospel citations that follow. Recognise the author's method of citing textual evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : إن عقيدة المسلمين في التوحيد وفي التنزيه . عليها أدلة من القرآن الكريم ، ومن التوراة ، ومن الإنجيل . وأعني بالإنجيل : الأناجيل الأربعة : متى ، ومرقس ، ولوقا ، ويوحنا .
۱ ـ وقد ذكرنا من قبل صورة من المقرآن الكريم تدل على التوحيد والتنزيه . وهي سورة الإخلاص
•
- والتوراة صرحت بوحدانية الله تعالى ، وصرحت بالتنزيه أيضا . وهذه نصوص منها : (أ) في الاصحاح السادس من سفر التثنية : اسمع يا إسرائيل. الرب إلهنا رب واحد . فتحب الرب إلهك من كل قلبك ، ومن كل نفك ، ومن كل قوتك الخ : انت ٦ : ٤ - ٥] وهذا النص يدل على التوحيد و الرب إلهنا رب واحد . .
(ب) وفي الأصحاح الثالث والثلاثين من سفر التثنية : وليس مثل الله ، [تث ٣٣ - ٢٦] وهذا النص بدل على التنزيه ، وعدم مشابهة الله للحوادث .
(ج) ودلت التوراة على أن الله تعالى لا يقدر أحد أن يراه ، وموسى عبده وكليمه لم يقدر أن يراه نفي الأصحاح الثالث والثلاثين من سفر الخروج أن موسى قال الله : ( أرني مجدك ، فقال له : ولا تقدر أن ترى وجهي . لأن الإنسان لا يراني ويعيش . [خر ٣٣ : ٢٠] .
(د) ودلت التوراة على أن الله تعالى كلم موسى وكلم بني إسرائيل وأنهم سمعوا الصوت ولم يروا ذاته المقدسة . ففي الأصحاح الرابع من التثنية : « فكلمكم الرب من وسط النار ، وأنتم سامعون صوت كلام ، ولكن لم تروا صورة بل صوتا ، [تث : ٤ : ١٢] .
(هـ) وتبين التوراة أن الله تعالى فعل هذا ، ليتقوا الله ويخافوه . ففي الأصحاح الرابع من سفر التثنية : « إنك قد رأيت هذا . لتعلم أن الرب هو الإله . ليس آخر سواه ، [نٹ ٤ : ٣٥] .
- وفي الأناجيل الأربعة المقدسة اليوم لدى النصارى مثل ما في التوراة عن الموحدانية والتنزيه .
Secondly: The Muslim creed concerning Tawḥīd and Tanzīh has proofs from the Noble Quran, and from the Torah, and from the Gospel. And by the Gospel I mean the four Gospels: Matthew, Mark, Luke, and John.
1) We have previously mentioned a section from the Noble Quran indicating Tawḥīd and Tanzīh, and it is Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ.
- And the Torah explicitly stated the oneness of God Most High, and explicitly stated Tanzīh as well. And these are texts from it: (a) In chapter six of Deuteronomy: 'Hear, O Israel: The Lord our God is one Lord. And you shall love the Lord your God with all your heart, and with all your soul, and with all your strength' etc. [Deut 6:4-5]. This text indicates Tawḥīd and 'the Lord our God is one Lord'.
(b) And in chapter thirty-three of Deuteronomy: 'There is none like God' [Deut 33:26]. This text indicates Tanzīh and the incomparability of God to occurrences.
(c) And the Torah indicated that God Most High cannot be seen by anyone, and Moses His servant and interlocutor could not see Him, in chapter thirty-three of Exodus when Moses said to God: 'Show me Your glory', and He said to him: 'You cannot see My face, for no man sees Me and lives' [Exod 33:20].
(d) And the Torah indicated that God Most High spoke to Moses and spoke to the Children of Israel and they heard the voice but did not see His Holy Essence. In chapter four of Deuteronomy: 'The Lord spoke to you from the midst of the fire; you heard the sound of words, but saw no form; only a voice' [Deut 4:12].
(e) And the Torah shows that God Most High did this so that they would be pious and fear God. In chapter four of Deuteronomy: 'You have been shown this, that you may know that the Lord is God; there is no other besides Him' [Deut 4:35].
- And in the four Gospels, holy today among Christians, is like what is in the Torah concerning monotheism and Tanzīh.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a positive presentation of evidence for Islamic tawḥīd (monotheism) and tanzīh (divine transcendence) from three scriptures: the Quran, the Torah, and the Gospels. The author first refers to Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ as a concise Quranic proof. He then cites five passages from the Torah. (a) Deuteronomy 6:4-5 affirms God's oneness. (b) Deuteronomy 33:26 affirms God's incomparability. (c) Exodus 33:20 shows that God cannot be seen, affirming His transcendence. (d) Deuteronomy 4:12 indicates that the Israelites heard God's voice but saw no form. (e) Deuteronomy 4:35 states that God alone is God, with no other. Finally, the author claims that the four canonical Gospels contain similar monotheistic and transcendent teachings, but he does not quote them here, merely asserting their agreement.

Key Terms
التوحيد
التنزيه
سورة الإخلاص
التوراة
الإنجيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which scriptures does the author claim support the Muslim creed?
A) Quran and Torah only
B) Quran, Torah, and Gospels
C) Quran and Gospels only
D) Torah and Gospels only

Key Takeaway
The Muslim creed is not based solely on the Quran; it finds support in the Torah and Gospels as well.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that the message of Tawḥīd has been consistent from Adam to Muhammad, how does that change the way you view a Jew or Christian who sincerely follows their scripture's teachings on monotheism? What concrete step will you take this week to acknowledge that common ground?
?
The author cites specific Gospels as evidence. Have you ever read the Gospels yourself to see what they teach about God's oneness? If not, what is holding you back, and what will you do about it starting today?
?
If the Muslim creed is attested in previous scriptures, why do so many Muslims ignore this fact? What responsibility does that place on you when you speak about Islam to others? Will you commit to learning more about the Torah and Gospel to better fulfill that responsibility?

The Torah Testifies to God's Oneness
Author cites Deuteronomy 6:4-5 as proof of Tawḥīd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Torah explicitly declares God's oneness in Deuteronomy 6:4-5.

Core Idea
The Torah commands love for the one Lord.

The author now presents his first Torah citation. Deuteronomy 6:4-5 is the classic Jewish Shema, affirming that the Lord is one and commanding love for Him with all one's being. This is a clear proof of Tawḥīd.

The Torah Affirms God's Incomparability

Core Idea
There is none like God.

The Torah on God's Unseenability
Author cites Exodus 33:20 to prove God cannot be seen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Exodus 33:20 proves that God cannot be seen in this world.

Core Idea
No one can see God and live.

The author cites Exodus 33:20, where God tells Moses that no one can see His face and live. This is a powerful proof of Tanzih, showing that God is beyond physical perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative
Read the Arabic description of Moses asking to see God's glory and God's response.
The author summarizes the story before quoting the verse.
2
Examine the verse
The key phrase: 'You cannot see My face, for no man sees Me and lives.'
This emphasizes God's transcendence.
3
Understand the implication
This verse shows that God is not visible to human eyes, reinforcing His absolute otherness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : إن عقيدة المسلمين في التوحيد وفي التنزيه . عليها أدلة من القرآن الكريم ، ومن التوراة ، ومن الإنجيل . وأعني بالإنجيل : الأناجيل الأربعة : متى ، ومرقس ، ولوقا ، ويوحنا .
۱ ـ وقد ذكرنا من قبل صورة من المقرآن الكريم تدل على التوحيد والتنزيه . وهي سورة الإخلاص
•
- والتوراة صرحت بوحدانية الله تعالى ، وصرحت بالتنزيه أيضا . وهذه نصوص منها : (أ) في الاصحاح السادس من سفر التثنية : اسمع يا إسرائيل. الرب إلهنا رب واحد . فتحب الرب إلهك من كل قلبك ، ومن كل نفك ، ومن كل قوتك الخ : انت ٦ : ٤ - ٥] وهذا النص يدل على التوحيد و الرب إلهنا رب واحد . .
(ب) وفي الأصحاح الثالث والثلاثين من سفر التثنية : وليس مثل الله ، [تث ٣٣ - ٢٦] وهذا النص بدل على التنزيه ، وعدم مشابهة الله للحوادث .
(ج) ودلت التوراة على أن الله تعالى لا يقدر أحد أن يراه ، وموسى عبده وكليمه لم يقدر أن يراه نفي الأصحاح الثالث والثلاثين من سفر الخروج أن موسى قال الله : ( أرني مجدك ، فقال له : ولا تقدر أن ترى وجهي . لأن الإنسان لا يراني ويعيش . [خر ٣٣ : ٢٠] .
(د) ودلت التوراة على أن الله تعالى كلم موسى وكلم بني إسرائيل وأنهم سمعوا الصوت ولم يروا ذاته المقدسة . ففي الأصحاح الرابع من التثنية : « فكلمكم الرب من وسط النار ، وأنتم سامعون صوت كلام ، ولكن لم تروا صورة بل صوتا ، [تث : ٤ : ١٢] .
(هـ) وتبين التوراة أن الله تعالى فعل هذا ، ليتقوا الله ويخافوه . ففي الأصحاح الرابع من سفر التثنية : « إنك قد رأيت هذا . لتعلم أن الرب هو الإله . ليس آخر سواه ، [نٹ ٤ : ٣٥] .
- وفي الأناجيل الأربعة المقدسة اليوم لدى النصارى مثل ما في التوراة عن الموحدانية والتنزيه .
Secondly: The Muslim creed concerning Tawḥīd and Tanzīh has proofs from the Noble Quran, and from the Torah, and from the Gospel. And by the Gospel I mean the four Gospels: Matthew, Mark, Luke, and John.
1) We have previously mentioned a section from the Noble Quran indicating Tawḥīd and Tanzīh, and it is Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ.
- And the Torah explicitly stated the oneness of God Most High, and explicitly stated Tanzīh as well. And these are texts from it: (a) In chapter six of Deuteronomy: 'Hear, O Israel: The Lord our God is one Lord. And you shall love the Lord your God with all your heart, and with all your soul, and with all your strength' etc. [Deut 6:4-5]. This text indicates Tawḥīd and 'the Lord our God is one Lord'.
(b) And in chapter thirty-three of Deuteronomy: 'There is none like God' [Deut 33:26]. This text indicates Tanzīh and the incomparability of God to occurrences.
(c) And the Torah indicated that God Most High cannot be seen by anyone, and Moses His servant and interlocutor could not see Him, in chapter thirty-three of Exodus when Moses said to God: 'Show me Your glory', and He said to him: 'You cannot see My face, for no man sees Me and lives' [Exod 33:20].
(d) And the Torah indicated that God Most High spoke to Moses and spoke to the Children of Israel and they heard the voice but did not see His Holy Essence. In chapter four of Deuteronomy: 'The Lord spoke to you from the midst of the fire; you heard the sound of words, but saw no form; only a voice' [Deut 4:12].
(e) And the Torah shows that God Most High did this so that they would be pious and fear God. In chapter four of Deuteronomy: 'You have been shown this, that you may know that the Lord is God; there is no other besides Him' [Deut 4:35].
- And in the four Gospels, holy today among Christians, is like what is in the Torah concerning monotheism and Tanzīh.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a positive presentation of evidence for Islamic tawḥīd (monotheism) and tanzīh (divine transcendence) from three scriptures: the Quran, the Torah, and the Gospels. The author first refers to Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ as a concise Quranic proof. He then cites five passages from the Torah. (a) Deuteronomy 6:4-5 affirms God's oneness. (b) Deuteronomy 33:26 affirms God's incomparability. (c) Exodus 33:20 shows that God cannot be seen, affirming His transcendence. (d) Deuteronomy 4:12 indicates that the Israelites heard God's voice but saw no form. (e) Deuteronomy 4:35 states that God alone is God, with no other. Finally, the author claims that the four canonical Gospels contain similar monotheistic and transcendent teachings, but he does not quote them here, merely asserting their agreement.

Key Terms
الرؤية
سفر الخروج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God can be seen physically in this world.
Correction: The Torah says no man can see God and live, affirming His transcendence.

Checkpoint
What does Exodus 33:20 say about seeing God?
A) Moses saw God
B) No man can see God and live
C) Only prophets can see God
D) Everyone will see God

Key Takeaway
The Torah affirms that God is invisible to human eyes, supporting divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that no one can see God and live, how would your daily pursuit of knowing Him change? Would you seek Him more through His words and signs rather than demanding visible proof?
?
Moses, the greatest prophet, was denied the vision of God in this world. When was the last time you felt a similar awe and longing for God's presence, and what did you do with that feeling?
?
This verse confronts you with your mortality: you cannot see God and live. Does your life reflect the urgency of preparing to meet Him after death, or have you become comfortable living as if God is distant and invisible?

The Torah on Hearing God Without Seeing
Author cites Deuteronomy 4:12 to show God's essence is unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Deuteronomy 4:12 shows that God's speech was heard but His form was not seen.

Core Idea
They heard a voice but saw no form.

The author cites Deuteronomy 4:12, where the Israelites heard God's voice from the fire but saw no form. This is another scriptural proof that God is not perceptible to sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the context
The author relates that God spoke to Moses and the Israelites, and they heard His voice but did not see His essence.
The verse is quoted: 'you heard the sound of words, but saw no form; only a voice'.
2
Analyze the verse
Note the distinction between hearing and seeing. The people heard God's speech but saw no physical form.
This reinforces Tanzih.
3
Consider the lesson
Understand that God can communicate without being visible, which preserves His transcendence.

The Torah's Purpose: Knowing the One God
Author cites Deuteronomy 4:35 as a conclusive proof of monotheism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Deuteronomy 4:35 concludes that the purpose of divine manifestations is to know that God is the only God.

Core Idea
Know that the Lord is God, there is no other.

The final Torah citation provides a conclusion: God showed these signs so that Israel would know that He alone is God. This reinforces the core message of Tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding verse
Read Deuteronomy 4:35: 'You have been shown this, that you may know that the Lord is God; there is no other besides Him.'
The author links this to the purpose of the previous signs.
2
Understand the purpose
The verse indicates that all divine actions aim to teach monotheism.
3
Reflect on the overarching theme
This verse ties together the previous proofs, showing that Tawhid is the central message of the Torah.

The Gospels Confirm the Same
Author claims Gospels agree with Torah on Tawhid and Tanzih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's claim that the four Gospels contain teachings on monotheism and transcendence similar to the Torah.

Core Idea
The Gospels also teach monotheism and transcendence.

The author concludes the section by stating that the four Gospels contain the same teachings about Tawhid and Tanzih as the Torah. Although he does not quote specific verses, he asserts their agreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim
Read the author's statement: 'And in the four Gospels, holy today among Christians, is like what is in the Torah concerning monotheism and Tanzih.'
Note that he does not provide citations.
2
Consider the implication
The author implies that the original Gospel message was monotheistic and transcendent.
This is a common argument in Islamic polemics.
3
Reflect on the method
Even without direct quotes, the author relies on the general content of the Gospels to support his case.

(ج) ودلت التوراة على أن الله تعالى لا يقدر أحد أن يراه ، وموسى عبده وكليمه لم يقدر أن يراه نفي الأصحاح الثالث والثلاثين من سفر الخروج أن موسى قال الله : ( أرني مجدك ، فقال له : ولا تقدر أن ترى وجهي . لأن الإنسان لا يراني ويعيش . [خر ٣٣ : ٢٠] .
(د) ودلت التوراة على أن الله تعالى كلم موسى وكلم بني إسرائيل وأنهم سمعوا الصوت ولم يروا ذاته المقدسة . ففي الأصحاح الرابع من التثنية : « فكلمكم الرب من وسط النار ، وأنتم سامعون صوت كلام ، ولكن لم تروا صورة بل صوتا ، [تث : ٤ : ١٢] .
(هـ) وتبين التوراة أن الله تعالى فعل هذا ، ليتقوا الله ويخافوه . ففي الأصحاح الرابع من سفر التثنية : « إنك قد رأيت هذا . لتعلم أن الرب هو الإله . ليس آخر سواه ، [نٹ ٤ : ٣٥] .
- وفي الأناجيل الأربعة المقدسة اليوم لدى النصارى مثل ما في التوراة عن الموحدانية والتنزيه .
Secondly: The Muslim creed concerning Tawḥīd and Tanzīh has proofs from the Noble Quran, and from the Torah, and from the Gospel. And by the Gospel I mean the four Gospels: Matthew, Mark, Luke, and John.
1) We have previously mentioned a section from the Noble Quran indicating Tawḥīd and Tanzīh, and it is Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ.
- And the Torah explicitly stated the oneness of God Most High, and explicitly stated Tanzīh as well. And these are texts from it: (a) In chapter six of Deuteronomy: 'Hear, O Israel: The Lord our God is one Lord. And you shall love the Lord your God with all your heart, and with all your soul, and with all your strength' etc. [Deut 6:4-5]. This text indicates Tawḥīd and 'the Lord our God is one Lord'.
(b) And in chapter thirty-three of Deuteronomy: 'There is none like God' [Deut 33:26]. This text indicates Tanzīh and the incomparability of God to occurrences.
(c) And the Torah indicated that God Most High cannot be seen by anyone, and Moses His servant and interlocutor could not see Him, in chapter thirty-three of Exodus when Moses said to God: 'Show me Your glory', and He said to him: 'You cannot see My face, for no man sees Me and lives' [Exod 33:20].
(d) And the Torah indicated that God Most High spoke to Moses and spoke to the Children of Israel and they heard the voice but did not see His Holy Essence. In chapter four of Deuteronomy: 'The Lord spoke to you from the midst of the fire; you heard the sound of words, but saw no form; only a voice' [Deut 4:12].
(e) And the Torah shows that God Most High did this so that they would be pious and fear God. In chapter four of Deuteronomy: 'You have been shown this, that you may know that the Lord is God; there is no other besides Him' [Deut 4:35].
- And in the four Gospels, holy today among Christians, is like what is in the Torah concerning monotheism and Tanzīh.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a positive presentation of evidence for Islamic tawḥīd (monotheism) and tanzīh (divine transcendence) from three scriptures: the Quran, the Torah, and the Gospels. The author first refers to Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ as a concise Quranic proof. He then cites five passages from the Torah. (a) Deuteronomy 6:4-5 affirms God's oneness. (b) Deuteronomy 33:26 affirms God's incomparability. (c) Exodus 33:20 shows that God cannot be seen, affirming His transcendence. (d) Deuteronomy 4:12 indicates that the Israelites heard God's voice but saw no form. (e) Deuteronomy 4:35 states that God alone is God, with no other. Finally, the author claims that the four canonical Gospels contain similar monotheistic and transcendent teachings, but he does not quote them here, merely asserting their agreement.

Key Terms
الإنجيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They are completely false
B) They contain the same teachings as the Torah on monotheism
C) They are not holy
D) They teach polytheism

Key Takeaway
The author claims the four Gospels support Tawhid and Tanzih, even without specific verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author makes a claim without citing specific verses. Are you comfortable accepting such a statement on authority? What does that reveal about your trust in Islamic scholarship? How can you deepen your own investigation to confirm or question this claim?

The Slap and Divine Command
Razi uses the child's slap to prove divine command and prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a child's instinctive demand for a reason for a slap proves that human actions are subject to divine commands and prohibitions.

Core Idea
The child's question 'why?' reveals that all actions require moral accountability.

In this passage, al-Razi presents a powerful everyday example to show that the very structure of human reasoning points to divine law. A child who is slapped does not accept it as meaningless—he demands a reason. This instinctive reaction, argues the author, demonstrates that pure reason judges actions to be subject to obligation (taklif). From this single case, he builds a general proof that all human acts, with their vast consequences, fall under divine command and prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English versions side by side to grasp the flow of the argument before analyzing it.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'فإنه' and 'ولما'.
2
Identify the key move
Notice how the child's spontaneous question 'why did you hit me?' is treated as evidence for a rational judgment, not a mere emotional outburst.
The author calls it 'pure reason' (صريح العقل), indicating its rational foundation.
3
Understand the generalization
Follow the logic: if a single slap cannot be free of obligation, then all actions with their many benefits and harms are even more deserving of moral evaluation.
The argument moves from a specific instance to a universal conclusion.
4
Reflect on the implication
Recognize that this proof establishes that God is described with command, prohibition, and obligation—a key divine attribute.
This is the second sought object mentioned in the lesson title.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما دلالة هذه اللطمة على المطلوب الثاني وهو كون الإله تعالى موصوفاً بالأمر والنهي والتكليف فنقول : إن ذلك الصبي إذا عرف أن ذلك الذي لطمه هو فلان ، فإنه في الحال يقول : لم ضربتني ؟ وبأي سبب آذيتني ؟ وهذا يدل على أن صريح عقله حكم بأن الخلق ما تركوا مهملين معطلين، بل التكاليف عليهم لازمة ، والمطالبات عليهم متوجهة ، ولما حكم صريح عقل ذلك الصبي بأن تلك اللطمة الواحدة لا يجوز خلوها عن التكليف ، والأمر والنهي ، فأفعال كل الخلائق مع كثرة ما فيها من المصالح والمفاسد ، أولى بأن لا يجوز خلوها من التكاليف.
And as for the indication of this slap to the second sought object, which is that God the Exalted is described with command, prohibition, and obligation, we say: That child, when he knows that the one who slapped him is so-and-so, immediately says: Why did you strike me? And for what reason did you harm me? This indicates that his pure reason has judged that creation is not left neglected and aimless, rather obligations upon them are necessary, and demands upon them are directed. Since the pure reason of that child has judged that that single slap cannot be free of obligation, command, and prohibition, then the actions of all creatures, with their abundance of benefits and harms, are more deserving that they cannot be free of obligations.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage explains how the slap proves that God has commands and prohibitions. Once the child identifies his aggressor, his immediate response is 'why did you hit me?' and 'for what reason did you harm me?' This shows that the child's innate reason assumes that actions are not arbitrary—they must have a reason and carry moral weight. The child's mind judges that even a single slap cannot be free of accountability (تكليف). If that is true for one slap, then all human actions, with their many beneficial and harmful consequences, are even more deserving of moral evaluation. Thus, the child's reaction implies that human actions are subject to divine commands and prohibitions, and that God is therefore a lawgiver.

Key Terms

أمر
نهي
صريح العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The child's question 'why did you hit me?' is a purely emotional reaction with no rational basis.
Correction: The author explicitly states that this response comes from 'pure reason' (ṣarīḥ al-ʿaql), meaning it is a rational judgment that actions require a reason and are morally accountable.

Checkpoint

A) The child is angry and seeking revenge.
B) Pure reason judges that actions have moral accountability.
C) The child is confused about who hit him.
D) The child wants to be compensated for harm.

Key Takeaway
A child's instinctive demand for a reason for a slap proves that all human actions are morally accountable and thus subject to divine commands and prohibitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act unjustly, do you immediately seek to justify yourself? That instinct proves you know your action is accountable to God. How will you change your response to your own wrongdoings starting today?
?
The child expects a reason for harm. Do you live as if your every action will be called into account? What one area of your life have you been treating as if it's free from divine command?
?
If even a slap demands a reason, how can you neglect the obligations God has placed on you? What is one command you are avoiding, and what will you do this week to fulfill it?

Necessity Cannot Be the Complete Quiddity: Three Sub-Proofs
Refutes the possibility that necessity is the complete quiddity using three sub-proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three sub-arguments proving that necessity (wujub) cannot be identical to the quiddity (essence) of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
If necessity were the essence, speech about it would be meaningless and the known would be unknown.

Ibn al-Ṭaqṭaqāʾī (or the author) sets out to prove that necessity is not an existential attribute by eliminating all logical possibilities. The first possibility—that necessity is the complete quiddity itself—is refuted with three independent sub-proofs: linguistic, epistemic, and logical. Each shows that necessity cannot be what the essence is.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the trichotomy
The passage begins by stating three possibilities for necessity if it were a fixed thing: it could be the complete quiddity, a part of it, or external to it. This lesson covers only the first possibility.
The trichotomy is a standard method of logical elimination (taqsīm).
2
Examine the first sub-proof (linguistic)
The author compares two statements: 'The body is necessary by itself' vs. 'Necessary of necessity, necessary of necessity.' The former is informative, the latter is not, showing that necessity is not the same as the quiddity.
The repetition shows redundancy if necessity were the essence.
3
Examine the second sub-proof (epistemic)
The quiddity of the Necessary is unknown, but its attribute of necessity is known. If they were identical, they would share the same epistemic status. Their difference implies real distinction.
This argument relies on the principle that identical things are known to the same extent.
4
Examine the third sub-proof (logical)
Logicians treat necessity as a modality (kayfiyya) of predication, not as a quiddity. Since modalities apply to propositions, they cannot be the essence of the subject.
This connects necessity to the logical categories of judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو كان الوجوب أمرا ثابتا، لكان إما أن يكون تمام ماهية الواجب، أو جزءا من تلك الماهية، أو أمرا خارجا عنها والكل باطل. أما أنه يمتنع أن يكون تمام الماهية فلوجوه: الأول: إنا إذا قلنا: الجسم واجب الوجود لذاته، كان الكلام مفيدا ولو قلنا: واجب الوجوب واجب الوجوب لم يكن الكلام مفيدا فظهر الفرق. الثاني: إن ماهية واجب الوجود لذاته غير معلومة وكونه واجب الوجود لذاته معلوم، فوجب التغاير. الثالث: إن وجوب الوجود كيفية لانتساب الموضوع إلى المحمول. ولذلك قال أهل المنطق: الجهات الثلاثة: الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع. وإذا كذلك امتنع أن يكون الوجوب تمام الماهية. أما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الوجوب جزءا من الماهية، فلأن كل ما كان كذلك كان مركبا، وكل مركب فهو ممكن لذاته، وكل ممكن لذاته فإنما يجب بإيجاب غيره، فيلزم أن يكون الوجوب الذاتي ممكنا لذاته، واجبا بغيره، وهو محال. وأما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الوجوب خارجا عن الماهية، فلأن بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك، لم يكن...
The second: If necessity were a fixed thing, it would either be the complete quiddity of the necessary, or a part of that quiddity, or something external to it, and all are false. As for that it is impossible to be the complete quiddity, for several reasons: First: If we say 'The body is necessary of existence by itself', the speech is informative, but if we say 'Necessary of necessity, necessary of necessity', the speech would not be informative, so the difference appears. Second: The quiddity of the necessary of existence by itself is unknown, but its being necessary of existence by itself is known, so difference is necessary. Third: Necessity of existence is a modality (kayfiyya) of the relation of the subject to the predicate. For that reason the logicians said: The three modalities: necessity, possibility, and impossibility. And if so, it is impossible that necessity be the complete quiddity. As for that it is impossible that necessity be a part of the quiddity, because everything that is like that is composite, and every composite is possible by itself, and every possible by itself is only necessitated by the necessitation of another, so it would follow that self-necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) is possible by itself, and necessary by another, which is impossible. And as for that it is impossible that necessity be external to the quiddity, because on the estimation that it is so, it would not be...
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents a trichotomous classification of what necessity could be if it were a fixed, real thing: either it is the complete essence of the necessary (i.e., identical to the quiddity), or a part of that essence, or something external to it (an accident). All three possibilities are refuted. For the first (complete essence), three sub-arguments are given: (1) linguistic: 'necessary of necessity' is uninformative, unlike 'body is necessary'; (2) epistemic: the essence is unknown but the attribute is known; (3) logical: necessity is a modality of predication, not a quiddity. For the second (part of essence): if necessity were a part, the essence would be composite, and composites are contingent, requiring an external necessitator, which contradicts self-necessity. The third possibility (external accident) is cut off but presumably also leads to an absurdity. The overall structure is a thorough logical elimination.

Key Terms
تمام الماهية (tamam al-mahiyya)
الوجوب الذاتي (al-wujub al-dhati)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'necessary' is just another name for God's essence, so they are identical.
Correction: The passage shows that if they were identical, essential predication would be meaningless, the known and unknown would be conflated, and necessity would be misclassified as a modality.

Checkpoint
Which sub-proof argues that if necessity were the complete quiddity, then saying 'necessary of necessity' would be redundant?
A) The linguistic sub-proof
B) The epistemic sub-proof
C) The logical sub-proof
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Necessity cannot be identical to the quiddity, as shown by linguistic, epistemic, and logical considerations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You acknowledge that God's essence is unknown to you, yet you affirm that He is necessary of existence. Does this certainty of His necessity shape how you rely on Him in moments of utter helplessness, or do you still fear the unknown?
?
When you say 'God is necessary,' do you reduce it to a familiar phrase, or does it unsettle you that the One you worship is not fully known?
?
If necessity is only a modality, not His essence, then your knowledge of God is of a relation, not His innermost reality. Are you content to worship a God whose essence remains veiled, and does this humility penetrate your prayers?

Necessity Cannot Be Part of the Quiddity: The Composite Argument
Refutes the possibility that necessity is part of the quiddity via composition argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessity cannot be a part of the quiddity, because that would imply composition, contingency, and need for an external necessitator.

Core Idea
If necessity were part of the essence, the essence would be composite and thus contingent, contradicting self-necessity.

Having shown that necessity cannot be the whole quiddity, the author turns to the second possibility: that necessity is a part of the quiddity. This lesson unpacks the short but powerful refutation: every composite is contingent, and every contingent requires an external cause, which would make self-necessity dependent on another—an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second possibility
The passage states the argument concisely: if necessity were a part, the quiddity would be composite; every composite is possible (contingent); every possible is necessitated by another; so self-necessity would be contingent and dependent—absurd.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
2
Analyze the premise: composition implies contingency
The author asserts that anything composite is possible by itself. This rests on the idea that a composite has parts that can conceivably be separated, so its existence is not necessary in itself.
This is a widely accepted kalam principle.
3
Follow the chain of dependency
If the composite is contingent, it needs a cause to bring it into existence. That cause would be external, making the essence dependent on something else. But self-necessity (wujub dhati) means existence by itself, requiring no external cause.
Thus, the second possibility leads to a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو كان الوجوب أمرا ثابتا، لكان إما أن يكون تمام ماهية الواجب، أو جزءا من تلك الماهية، أو أمرا خارجا عنها والكل باطل. أما أنه يمتنع أن يكون تمام الماهية فلوجوه: الأول: إنا إذا قلنا: الجسم واجب الوجود لذاته، كان الكلام مفيدا ولو قلنا: واجب الوجوب واجب الوجوب لم يكن الكلام مفيدا فظهر الفرق. الثاني: إن ماهية واجب الوجود لذاته غير معلومة وكونه واجب الوجود لذاته معلوم، فوجب التغاير. الثالث: إن وجوب الوجود كيفية لانتساب الموضوع إلى المحمول. ولذلك قال أهل المنطق: الجهات الثلاثة: الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع. وإذا كذلك امتنع أن يكون الوجوب تمام الماهية. أما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الوجوب جزءا من الماهية، فلأن كل ما كان كذلك كان مركبا، وكل مركب فهو ممكن لذاته، وكل ممكن لذاته فإنما يجب بإيجاب غيره، فيلزم أن يكون الوجوب الذاتي ممكنا لذاته، واجبا بغيره، وهو محال. وأما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الوجوب خارجا عن الماهية، فلأن بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك، لم يكن...
The second: If necessity were a fixed thing, it would either be the complete quiddity of the necessary, or a part of that quiddity, or something external to it, and all are false. As for that it is impossible to be the complete quiddity, for several reasons: First: If we say 'The body is necessary of existence by itself', the speech is informative, but if we say 'Necessary of necessity, necessary of necessity', the speech would not be informative, so the difference appears. Second: The quiddity of the necessary of existence by itself is unknown, but its being necessary of existence by itself is known, so difference is necessary. Third: Necessity of existence is a modality (kayfiyya) of the relation of the subject to the predicate. For that reason the logicians said: The three modalities: necessity, possibility, and impossibility. And if so, it is impossible that necessity be the complete quiddity. As for that it is impossible that necessity be a part of the quiddity, because everything that is like that is composite, and every composite is possible by itself, and every possible by itself is only necessitated by the necessitation of another, so it would follow that self-necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) is possible by itself, and necessary by another, which is impossible. And as for that it is impossible that necessity be external to the quiddity, because on the estimation that it is so, it would not be...
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents a trichotomous classification of what necessity could be if it were a fixed, real thing: either it is the complete essence of the necessary (i.e., identical to the quiddity), or a part of that essence, or something external to it (an accident). All three possibilities are refuted. For the first (complete essence), three sub-arguments are given: (1) linguistic: 'necessary of necessity' is uninformative, unlike 'body is necessary'; (2) epistemic: the essence is unknown but the attribute is known; (3) logical: necessity is a modality of predication, not a quiddity. For the second (part of essence): if necessity were a part, the essence would be composite, and composites are contingent, requiring an external necessitator, which contradicts self-necessity. The third possibility (external accident) is cut off but presumably also leads to an absurdity. The overall structure is a thorough logical elimination.

Key Terms
جزء الماهية (juz' al-mahiyya)
المركب (al-murakkab)
الممكن لذاته (al-mumkin bi-dhatih)
الوجوب الذاتي (al-wujub al-dhati)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if necessity is part of the essence, the essence remains simple because the part is not separate.
Correction: The argument does not depend on physical separation but on conceptual composition. Any distinction within the essence (even mental) makes it composite, which implies contingency.

Checkpoint
What is the key principle that makes a composite contingent?
A) It is possible because it can be conceived not to exist
B) Its parts can be separated
C) It requires a cause to combine its parts
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Necessity cannot be part of the quiddity, as that would make the Necessary Being composite and contingent, contradicting its self-necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that the Necessary Being cannot have parts—not even necessity as a separate component. Do you ever mentally divide God's attributes, thinking mercy is one part and justice another? How can you train your heart this week to worship God as absolutely simple and one, without separating His attributes from His essence?
?
Everything composite depends on something else for its unity. You are composite—body and soul, many parts. Does this awareness make you feel your constant need for the Simple, Necessary Being? In what specific area of your life are you acting as if you are self-sufficient rather than contingent?
?
If necessity cannot be added to the essence, then God's essence is identical to His necessity. Your own 'essence' is not necessary; you could not exist. How does the total contingency of your existence press you to rely on God not just for your coming into being, but for every moment of your existence? What would change today if you truly lived as 'mumkin bi-dhatih'?

Necessity Cannot Be External to the Quiddity: The Incomplete Argument
Begins refuting the possibility that necessity is external to the quiddity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third possibility—that necessity is external to the quiddity—and why it is also impossible, based on the introductory reasoning.

Core Idea
If necessity were external, the Necessary Being would have an accident, making it dependent on something outside its essence.

The author completes the logical elimination by considering the third possibility: that necessity is something external to the quiddity—i.e., an accidental attribute. Although the passage is incomplete, the direction is clear: if necessity is an accident, then the essence would be contingent and need a substratum. This lesson explores the beginning of this refutation and what must follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening of the third possibility
The passage begins: 'As for that it is impossible that necessity be external to the quiddity, because on the estimation that it is so, it would not be...' The text cuts off, but the reasoning can be inferred from the context.
The original manuscript may have continued on the next page.
2
Consider why an external attribute would be problematic
An external attribute would be an accident (arad). An accident cannot subsist by itself; it requires a subject. If necessity is an accident of the essence, then the essence must exist prior to the attribute, making necessity non-essential and possibly contingent.
This aligns with the standard rejection of accidents in the Divine essence.
3
Connect to the overall elimination
By refuting all three possibilities, the author establishes that necessity is not an existential attribute at all. It is a rational judgment (hukm 'aqli) applied to the essence, not a real property.
This conclusion is key to the broader argument of the chapter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو كان الوجوب أمرا ثابتا، لكان إما أن يكون تمام ماهية الواجب، أو جزءا من تلك الماهية، أو أمرا خارجا عنها والكل باطل. أما أنه يمتنع أن يكون تمام الماهية فلوجوه: الأول: إنا إذا قلنا: الجسم واجب الوجود لذاته، كان الكلام مفيدا ولو قلنا: واجب الوجوب واجب الوجوب لم يكن الكلام مفيدا فظهر الفرق. الثاني: إن ماهية واجب الوجود لذاته غير معلومة وكونه واجب الوجود لذاته معلوم، فوجب التغاير. الثالث: إن وجوب الوجود كيفية لانتساب الموضوع إلى المحمول. ولذلك قال أهل المنطق: الجهات الثلاثة: الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع. وإذا كذلك امتنع أن يكون الوجوب تمام الماهية. أما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الوجوب جزءا من الماهية، فلأن كل ما كان كذلك كان مركبا، وكل مركب فهو ممكن لذاته، وكل ممكن لذاته فإنما يجب بإيجاب غيره، فيلزم أن يكون الوجوب الذاتي ممكنا لذاته، واجبا بغيره، وهو محال. وأما أنه يمتنع أن يكون الوجوب خارجا عن الماهية، فلأن بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك، لم يكن...
The second: If necessity were a fixed thing, it would either be the complete quiddity of the necessary, or a part of that quiddity, or something external to it, and all are false. As for that it is impossible to be the complete quiddity, for several reasons: First: If we say 'The body is necessary of existence by itself', the speech is informative, but if we say 'Necessary of necessity, necessary of necessity', the speech would not be informative, so the difference appears. Second: The quiddity of the necessary of existence by itself is unknown, but its being necessary of existence by itself is known, so difference is necessary. Third: Necessity of existence is a modality (kayfiyya) of the relation of the subject to the predicate. For that reason the logicians said: The three modalities: necessity, possibility, and impossibility. And if so, it is impossible that necessity be the complete quiddity. As for that it is impossible that necessity be a part of the quiddity, because everything that is like that is composite, and every composite is possible by itself, and every possible by itself is only necessitated by the necessitation of another, so it would follow that self-necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) is possible by itself, and necessary by another, which is impossible. And as for that it is impossible that necessity be external to the quiddity, because on the estimation that it is so, it would not be...
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents a trichotomous classification of what necessity could be if it were a fixed, real thing: either it is the complete essence of the necessary (i.e., identical to the quiddity), or a part of that essence, or something external to it (an accident). All three possibilities are refuted. For the first (complete essence), three sub-arguments are given: (1) linguistic: 'necessary of necessity' is uninformative, unlike 'body is necessary'; (2) epistemic: the essence is unknown but the attribute is known; (3) logical: necessity is a modality of predication, not a quiddity. For the second (part of essence): if necessity were a part, the essence would be composite, and composites are contingent, requiring an external necessitator, which contradicts self-necessity. The third possibility (external accident) is cut off but presumably also leads to an absurdity. The overall structure is a thorough logical elimination.

Key Terms
خارج الماهية (kharij al-mahiyya)
الوجوب الذاتي (al-wujub al-dhati)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might argue that necessity could be an accidental attribute added to the essence, like whiteness to a wall.
Correction: The passage (and broader theology) holds that God's attributes are not accidents; they are identical with His essence or are modes of relation. An external accident would imply composition and dependence.

Checkpoint
According to the overall argument, why is it impossible for necessity to be external to the quiddity?
A) Because it would make the essence composite
B) Because it would make necessity an accident dependent on the essence
C) Because the passage is incomplete and we cannot know
D) Because the logicians said so

Key Takeaway
The third possibility (necessity as external) is also impossible because it would make necessity an accident, contradicting the simplicity and self-sufficiency of the Necessary Being.

Divine Attributes

Al-Razi's Argument: Necessity Cannot Be an Attribute
Al-Razi argues that if wujub were an attribute, it would lead to regress and multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's proof that wujub (necessity) cannot be an attribute of the divine essence, as it leads to an infinite regress and multiplicity of necessities.

Core Idea
Necessity as an attribute leads to an impossible infinite regress.

Al-Razi presents a proof (burhan) to refute the idea that the necessity of existence (wujub) is a distinct attribute added to the divine essence. By analyzing the nature of attributes and their dependence on a subject, he shows that such a view leads to a logical absurdity: an infinite chain of causes and multiple necessities in one essence. This passage is a classic example of how kalam uses rational argument to defend the unity and simplicity of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure of the argument as it unfolds.
2
Identify the assumption being refuted
The argument begins by assuming that if wujub were an attribute, what would it be? Al-Razi first rejects that it could be a self-subsistent substance.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: attribute depends on subject → dependent thing is contingent → contingent requires a cause → that cause would be another wujub → leads to regress and multiplicity.
Pay attention to the terms: jawhar, sifa, mumkin, wajib bi-ghayrihi.
4
Understand the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that regress and multiplicity are impossible (muhal), therefore wujub cannot be a real attribute.
This proof protects the doctrine of divine simplicity (tawhid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جوهرا قائما بنفسه (۱) ، مباينا (۲) عن الذات الواجبة ، بل يكون صفة لتلك الذات ، والصفة مفتقرة إلى الموصوف . والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، واجب بغيره فيجب (۳) أن يحصل قبل هذا الوجوب وجوب آخر ، حتى يكون ذلك الوجوب السابق علة لوجوب هذا الوجوب (1) اللاحق ، فيلزم وقوع التسلسل ، ويلزم أن يحصل للذات الواحدة أعدادا من الوجوب . وكل ذلك محال .
It is not a substance subsisting by itself, distinct from the Necessary Essence, but rather it is an attribute of that essence, and the attribute is dependent on the described. What is dependent on another is contingent by itself, necessary through another. Then it must be that before this necessity there occurs another necessity, so that that prior necessity is the cause of the necessity of this posterior necessity, leading to an infinite regress, and leading to the occurrence of multiple necessities for one essence. All of that is impossible.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of a proof (burhan) that wujub (necessity) cannot be an existent attribute. The argument begins by assuming the opposite: if wujub were a self-subsistent substance (jawhar qa'im bi-nafsihi) separate from the Necessary Essence (al-dhat al-wajiba), it would be distinct and independent. But then it is shown that it must instead be an attribute (sifa) of that Essence. An attribute depends on its subject (al-mawsuf). Whatever depends on another is contingent (mumkin) by itself and necessary through another (wajib bi-ghayrihi). This leads to a problem: if the necessity of the attribute is contingent, it requires a prior necessity to cause it, which itself would require another, and so on ad infinitum (tasalsul). This infinite regress is impossible (muhal). Moreover, it would imply that one single essence (al-dhat al-wahida) has multiple instances of necessity (a'dad min al-wujub), which is also impossible. Thus the original assumption that wujub is an attribute leads to absurdity, proving that wujub cannot be an existent attribute.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
صفة (sifa)
ممكن (mumkin)
وجوب (wujub)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that wujub is a distinct attribute that adds something to the divine essence, like an accident to a substance.
Correction: Al-Razi's proof shows that any attribute dependent on the essence would be contingent, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, wujub cannot be a separate attribute; it is identical with the essence itself.

Checkpoint
What does the proof show would happen if wujub were an attribute?
A) It would be eternal and necessary.
B) It would lead to infinite regress and multiple necessities.
C) It would become a substance.
D) It would be identical with the essence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that treating wujub as an attribute leads to an impossible infinite regress and multiplicity, proving that necessity is not a distinct attribute but identical with the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's attributes, do you unconsciously treat them as separate qualities added to His essence? How does Al-Razi's logical unmasking of that error challenge your mental picture of God, and what will you do to correct it?
?
This argument insists that even one mistaken assumption about divine attributes leads to absurdity. Are you willing to scrutinize your own beliefs about Allah with the same relentless logic, even if it means abandoning comfortable but flawed notions?
?
Al-Razi proves that Allah's necessity is not an added feature but is Allah Himself. Does your heart truly rest in the belief that every aspect of your Lord is uncaused and independent, or do you secretly harbor traces of thinking that something about Him could be contingent? What step will you take today to deepen your tawheed?
