The Composite Nature of Wujub bil-Dhat
Al-Razi's initial argument for composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why wujub bil-dhat, if considered an existent attribute, would necessarily be composite, leading to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Wujub bil-dhat cannot be composite; composition implies contingency, contradicting necessity.

Al-Razi begins the third proof by arguing that wujub bil-dhat cannot be an existent attribute because any existent attribute is a quiddity. A quiddity with both a shared generic element and a specific difference is composite, and composition entails contingency. This lesson traces that initial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Razi uses a very dense, logical style.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the key terms: wujub bil-dhat, wujub bil-ghayr, mahiyya murakkaba. Understand their meanings.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Follow the logical deduction
Trace the logical steps: assumption of wujub bil-dhat as a positive attribute leads to it being a quiddity; quiddity is composite; composite is contingent; contingency contradicts necessity.
4
Ask why composition is impossible
Ask yourself why the Necessary Being cannot be composite. The commentary will help you understand the contradiction.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : في بيان أن الوجوب بالذات يمتنع أن يكون وصفا موجودا : وذلك لأن الوجوب بالذات ماهية مركبة. فهي ممكنة لذاتها . ينتج أن الوجوب لذاته [ ممكن لذاته ] (٥) ، وهو محال . إنما قلنا : إن الوجوب بالذات ماهية مركبة فلان الوجوب بالذات والوجوب بالغير متشاركان في المفهوم من كونه وجوبيا (۱) ويمتاز عن الآخر بكونه [ وجوبيا بالذات ، وكون الآخر) وجوبيا (۸) بالغير وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، فيلزم أن يكون الوجوب بالذات مركبا من هذين القيدين. فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الوجوب بالذات ، ممتاز عن الوجوب بالغير بقيد سلبي . وهو أنه لا يحصل معه القيد الزائد الحاصل في جانب الوجوب (۹) بالغير ؟ قلنا : لأن الوجوب الذاتي ، وهو القدر (١٠) المشترك بين الصورتين إما أن يكون واجبا لذاته وإما أن يكون ممكنا لذاته . فإن كان الأول (۱۱) امتنع أن يصير جزءا من ماهية الوجوب بالغير ، لأن ماهية الوجوب بالغير صفة عارضة لماهيات الممكنات، والعارض للشيء يكون عارضا لذلك الشيء بجميع أجزائه فلو كان أحد أجزائه هو الوجوب الذي هو واجب الوجود لذاته، لزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته مفتقرا إلى غيره وهو محال . وأما إن قلنا : إن مسمى الوجوب الذي هو القدر (۱۲) المشتريك بين الصورتين أمر ممكن لذاته ، ثم إنه جزء من قوام ماهية الوجوب الذاتي ، لزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته متقوما بالممكن لذاته ، وهو محال . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان الوجوب بالذات مفهوما ثبوتيا ، للزم كون هذه الماهية مركبة ولما ثبت أن هذا محال ، كان القول بكونه مفهوما ثبوتيا محال
The third proof: An exposition that necessity by essence (wujub bil-dhat) cannot be an existent attribute. This is because necessity by essence is a composite quiddity. Thus it is contingent by itself. This yields that necessity by itself is contingent by itself, which is impossible. We say that necessity by essence is a composite quiddity because necessity by essence and necessity through another share in the concept of being necessity, and are distinguished by one being necessity by essence and the other being necessity through another, and that by which they share is not that by which they differ, so it follows that necessity by essence is composed of these two determinations. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that necessity by essence is distinguished from necessity through another by a negative determination, i.e., that it does not occur with it the additional determination that occurs in the case of necessity through another? We say: Because essential necessity, which is the common factor between the two forms, is either necessary by itself or contingent by itself. If it were the first, it would be impossible for it to become part of the quiddity of necessity through another, because the quiddity of necessity through another is an accidental attribute of the quiddities of contingent things, and the accidental is accidental to the thing with all its parts. So if one of its parts were the necessity that is necessary of existence by itself, it would entail that the necessary by itself is dependent on another, which is impossible. And if we say that the meaning of necessity, which is the common factor between the two forms, is contingent by itself, and then it is part of the constitution of the quiddity of essential necessity, then it would entail that the necessary by itself is constituted by the contingent by itself, which is impossible. So it is established from what we have mentioned that if necessity by essence were a positive concept, this quiddity would necessarily be composite, and since this is impossible, the claim that it is a positive concept is impossible.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This is the third proof (al-wajh al-thalith) that wujub bil-dhat cannot be an existent attribute (wasf mawjud). The argument begins by asserting that if wujub bil-dhat were a positive concept, it would be a composite quiddity (mahiyya murakkaba). Anything composite is contingent (mumkin) by itself, which contradicts the very meaning of wujub bil-dhat (necessary by essence). The proof then shows why it would be composite: wujub bil-dhat and wujub bil-ghayr share the generic concept of wujub (necessity) and each has a specific difference (being by essence vs. through another). Since the shared part and distinguishing part are different, the composite nature is unavoidable. Al-Razi then considers and refutes an objection: could the distinction be by a negative determination (qayd salbi) instead? He shows that the common factor (al-qadr al-mushtarak) must itself be either necessary or contingent. If it is necessary, it cannot be a part of the quiddity of wujub bil-ghayr, which is accidental; if it is contingent, then the necessary by essence would be constituted by the contingent, another impossibility. Hence the concept of wujub bil-dhat as a positive attribute is impossible.

Key Terms
وجوب بالذات
وجوب بالغير
ماهية مركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think wujub bil-dhat is a simple positive attribute without parts.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that any positive attribute is a quiddity, and quiddities are composite due to shared generic concept and specific difference.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Razi argue that wujub bil-dhat would be a composite quiddity?
A) Because it is a simple positive attribute without parts.
B) Because it shares the concept of wujub with wujub bil-ghayr and has a specific difference.
C) Because it is the same as wujub bil-ghayr.
D) Because it is a negative concept.

Key Takeaway
Wujub bil-dhat cannot be a positive attribute because it would entail composition and therefore contingency, contradicting its necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If wujub bil-dhat cannot be composite, what does that mean for how you conceive of God? Are you tempted to picture God with parts or attributes that are separate from His essence? How does this argument challenge any mental images you may have formed?
?
This proof shows that any composition leads to contingency. In what areas of your life have you been relying on composite things—like wealth, status, or relationships—as if they were necessary? How does the truth of divine necessity expose the fragility of those dependencies?
?
Al-Razi’s argument demands precision in thinking about God. Are you willing to let go of comfortable but imprecise ideas about God’s attributes, even if it means rethinking your understanding of faith? What specific intellectual comfort might you need to abandon to affirm God’s absolute simplicity?

Refuting the Negative Determination Objection
Al-Razi's refutation of the negative distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a negative determination cannot save the composition problem, because the common factor is either necessary or contingent—both impossible.

Core Idea
A negative distinction does not avoid composition; the common factor still creates contradiction.

An opponent may propose that wujub bil-dhat is distinguished from wujub bil-ghayr not by a positive feature but by a negative one—simply lacking the additional determination. Al-Razi refutes this by analyzing the common factor (al-qadr al-mushtarak), showing it leads to impossibility in both cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the objection carefully: the opponent suggests a negative determination. Then read Al-Razi's refutation.
Notice that the refutation focuses on the common factor.
2
Examine the two possibilities
Identify the two cases: the common factor is either necessary by itself or contingent by itself. Understand why each leads to contradiction.
3
Understand the conclusion
Recognize that both cases are impossible, so the negative determination does not resolve the problem.
This completes the proof that wujub bil-dhat cannot be a positive concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : في بيان أن الوجوب بالذات يمتنع أن يكون وصفا موجودا : وذلك لأن الوجوب بالذات ماهية مركبة. فهي ممكنة لذاتها . ينتج أن الوجوب لذاته [ ممكن لذاته ] (٥) ، وهو محال . إنما قلنا : إن الوجوب بالذات ماهية مركبة فلان الوجوب بالذات والوجوب بالغير متشاركان في المفهوم من كونه وجوبيا (۱) ويمتاز عن الآخر بكونه [ وجوبيا بالذات ، وكون الآخر) وجوبيا (۸) بالغير وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، فيلزم أن يكون الوجوب بالذات مركبا من هذين القيدين. فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الوجوب بالذات ، ممتاز عن الوجوب بالغير بقيد سلبي . وهو أنه لا يحصل معه القيد الزائد الحاصل في جانب الوجوب (۹) بالغير ؟ قلنا : لأن الوجوب الذاتي ، وهو القدر (١٠) المشترك بين الصورتين إما أن يكون واجبا لذاته وإما أن يكون ممكنا لذاته . فإن كان الأول (۱۱) امتنع أن يصير جزءا من ماهية الوجوب بالغير ، لأن ماهية الوجوب بالغير صفة عارضة لماهيات الممكنات، والعارض للشيء يكون عارضا لذلك الشيء بجميع أجزائه فلو كان أحد أجزائه هو الوجوب الذي هو واجب الوجود لذاته، لزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته مفتقرا إلى غيره وهو محال . وأما إن قلنا : إن مسمى الوجوب الذي هو القدر (۱۲) المشتريك بين الصورتين أمر ممكن لذاته ، ثم إنه جزء من قوام ماهية الوجوب الذاتي ، لزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته متقوما بالممكن لذاته ، وهو محال . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه لو كان الوجوب بالذات مفهوما ثبوتيا ، للزم كون هذه الماهية مركبة ولما ثبت أن هذا محال ، كان القول بكونه مفهوما ثبوتيا محال
The third proof: An exposition that necessity by essence (wujub bil-dhat) cannot be an existent attribute. This is because necessity by essence is a composite quiddity. Thus it is contingent by itself. This yields that necessity by itself is contingent by itself, which is impossible. We say that necessity by essence is a composite quiddity because necessity by essence and necessity through another share in the concept of being necessity, and are distinguished by one being necessity by essence and the other being necessity through another, and that by which they share is not that by which they differ, so it follows that necessity by essence is composed of these two determinations. If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that necessity by essence is distinguished from necessity through another by a negative determination, i.e., that it does not occur with it the additional determination that occurs in the case of necessity through another? We say: Because essential necessity, which is the common factor between the two forms, is either necessary by itself or contingent by itself. If it were the first, it would be impossible for it to become part of the quiddity of necessity through another, because the quiddity of necessity through another is an accidental attribute of the quiddities of contingent things, and the accidental is accidental to the thing with all its parts. So if one of its parts were the necessity that is necessary of existence by itself, it would entail that the necessary by itself is dependent on another, which is impossible. And if we say that the meaning of necessity, which is the common factor between the two forms, is contingent by itself, and then it is part of the constitution of the quiddity of essential necessity, then it would entail that the necessary by itself is constituted by the contingent by itself, which is impossible. So it is established from what we have mentioned that if necessity by essence were a positive concept, this quiddity would necessarily be composite, and since this is impossible, the claim that it is a positive concept is impossible.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This is the third proof (al-wajh al-thalith) that wujub bil-dhat cannot be an existent attribute (wasf mawjud). The argument begins by asserting that if wujub bil-dhat were a positive concept, it would be a composite quiddity (mahiyya murakkaba). Anything composite is contingent (mumkin) by itself, which contradicts the very meaning of wujub bil-dhat (necessary by essence). The proof then shows why it would be composite: wujub bil-dhat and wujub bil-ghayr share the generic concept of wujub (necessity) and each has a specific difference (being by essence vs. through another). Since the shared part and distinguishing part are different, the composite nature is unavoidable. Al-Razi then considers and refutes an objection: could the distinction be by a negative determination (qayd salbi) instead? He shows that the common factor (al-qadr al-mushtarak) must itself be either necessary or contingent. If it is necessary, it cannot be a part of the quiddity of wujub bil-ghayr, which is accidental; if it is contingent, then the necessary by essence would be constituted by the contingent, another impossibility. Hence the concept of wujub bil-dhat as a positive attribute is impossible.

What are the two possibilities for the common factor (al-qadr al-mushtarak) in Al-Razi's refutation?
A) Necessary by essence or contingent by essence

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen al-Razi's argument that any positive concept of necessity in God leads to composition and impossibility. Are there areas in your own understanding of Allah's attributes where you unconsciously add human concepts or distinctions, thinking that negations or positive determinations can define Him? This lesson calls you to stop. What will you do today to surrender your intellect to the revealed names and attributes without philosophical elaboration?
?
Al-Razi forces you to admit that your mind cannot grasp Allah's necessity as a positive 'thing' without falling into contradiction. How does this humbling truth affect the way you speak about Allah? Will you let it transform your speech from philosophical speculation to humble affirmation of what He has said about Himself? If so, what is the first phrase you will change?
?
Consider the fact that even a 'negative distinction' fails to avoid composition. Does this not show that any attempt to rationalize Allah's attributes from your own reasoning leads to dead ends? Where in your heart do you still trust your own understanding over revelation? What step will you take this week to increase your reliance on the Qur'an and Sunnah in defining your Lord?

Wujub is Predicated of Non-Existence: Al-Razi's Fourth Proof
Al-Razi argues that wujub cannot be existent since it is predicated of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that necessity (wujub) cannot be a positive concept because it is predicated of non-existence and must itself be non-existent.

Core Idea
Necessity is not a positive attribute; it applies even to non-existence.

Al-Razi presents his fourth proof that wujub cannot be a positive, existent concept. The argument hinges on the logical principle that what is predicated of pure non-existence must itself be non-existent. This passage challenges the common assumption that necessity is always about existence, showing instead that we speak of things being 'necessary not to exist'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two-part structure: first, that necessity is predicated of non-existence; second, that what is predicated of non-existence must be non-existent.
The passage uses a reductio argument in the second part.
2
Identify the two main claims
The argument rests on two premises: (1) necessity is predicated of non-existence, and (2) whatever is predicated of non-existence is itself non-existent. Find where each is stated and supported.
The first is supported by the example of an impossible thing.
3
Understand the example
Al-Razi says: a thing that is impossible to exist is necessarily not to exist. So the concept 'necessary' is applied to non-existence. Reflect on this example.
4
Grasp the reductio ad absurdum
The second premise is justified by a reductio: if the predicate were existent, then pure non-existence would be qualified by an existent attribute, which is impossible. This shows the predicate must be non-existent.
This is a logical argument based on the nature of predication.
5
State the conclusion
From the two premises, Al-Razi concludes that necessity cannot be an existent attribute. Summarize the argument in your own words.
This supports the broader claim that wujub is not a positive concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : في بيان أن الوجوب الذاتي يمتنع أن يكون مفهوماً : هو أن مسمى الوجوب محمول على العدم [المحض ، والمحمول على العدم عدم . فالوجوب عدم . وإنما قلنا : إن الوجوب محمول على العدم] (٢) لأن الشيء الذي يصدق عليه [أنه يمتنع أن يوجد يصدق عليه ] (٢) لا محالة أنه واجب أن [لا] (4) يوجد . فههنا المفهوم من الوجوب محمول على اللاوجود . فثبت أن الوجوب محمول على العدم. وأما إن المحمول على العدم : عدم ، فلأنه لو كان وجودا لزم أن يكون العدم المحض (٥) موصوفا بالصفة الموجودة ، وأنه محال . فثبت أن مسمى الوجوب، لا يمكن أن يكون صفة موجودة .
The fourth proof: An exposition that essential necessity cannot be a concept: It is that the meaning of necessity is predicated of pure non-existence, and what is predicated of non-existence is non-existence. So necessity is non-existence. We say that necessity is predicated of non-existence because a thing of which it is true that it is impossible to exist is of necessity necessary not to exist. So here the concept of necessity is predicated of non-existence. Thus it is established that necessity is predicated of non-existence. As for the claim that what is predicated of non-existence is non-existence, it is because if it were existence, then pure non-existence would be qualified by an existent attribute, which is impossible. Thus it is established that the meaning of necessity cannot be an existent attribute.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This is the fourth proof (al-wajh al-rabi') that wujub (necessity) cannot be a positive concept (mafhum thubuti). The proof is based on predication. Al-Razi states that the term 'wajib' is often predicated of non-existence (al-'adam). For example, when we say something is impossible to exist (mumtani' al-wujud), it necessarily follows that it is wajib an la yujad (necessary not to exist). Thus the concept of wujub is applied to non-existence. Then he argues that whatever is predicated of non-existence must itself be non-existent (al-mahmul 'ala al-'adam 'adam). Why? Because if it were existent, then pure non-existence would be qualified by an existent attribute, which is a contradiction. Hence wujub cannot be an existent attribute (sifa mawjuda). This proof relies on the logical principle that a predicate must share the ontological status of its subject when the subject is pure non-existence.

Key Terms
عد (adam)
وجوب (wujub)
محمول (mahmul)
وجود (wujud)
ممتنع (mumtani')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity only applies to existing things.
Correction: Necessity can be predicated of non-existence, as in 'necessary not to exist'.

Checkpoint
What principle does Al-Razi use to argue that necessity must be non-existent?
A) Whatever is predicated of non-existence is non-existent.
B) Necessity is always positive.
C) Existence is always predicated of existence.
D) Necessity and existence are identical.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi proves that necessity cannot be a positive attribute because it is predicated of non-existence and must share the ontological status of its subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often plan your life with a sense of necessity—'this must happen,' 'that is inevitable.' But Al-Razi shows that necessity itself is non-existent. What does that reveal about the false certainty you place in your own designs? How will you confront the arrogance of assuming outcomes are fixed?
?
The phrase 'wujub is predicated of non-existence' means that the only real necessity is the impossibility of contradiction—a mental relation, not a concrete force. In your heart, where have you treated your own opinions or desires as necessary realities? What would it mean to dissolve that illusion and submit all necessity to Allah alone?

The Fifth Proof: Necessity Cannot Be an Existent Attribute
Al-Razi's fifth proof that wujub cannot be an existent attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessity (wujub) cannot be an existent attribute according to al-Razi's fifth proof.

Core Idea
Necessity precedes existence, so it cannot be an attribute of the thing.

In this passage, al-Razi presents his fifth proof that necessity (wujub) is not an attribute. He argues that since necessity is a precondition for existence, it must be prior. But if it were an attribute, it would have to exist before the thing itself, which is logically impossible. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the gist of the argument. Notice the logical flow from the premise to the conclusion.
Pay attention to the terms 'وجوب' and 'وجود'.
2
Identify the key premise
Al-Razi starts by stating that a thing does not exist unless it is necessary. This establishes that necessity precedes existence in rank.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
He then supposes that necessity were an attribute. That would mean the attribute exists before the thing itself, which is impossible because the thing's existence in itself is prior to any attribute from another.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that necessity cannot be an existent attribute. Consider how this argument fits into the larger discussion about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : في بيان أن الوجوب يمتنع أن يكون صفة موجودة : وذلك لأن الشيء ما لم يجب لم يوجد ، فوجوبه متقدم على وجوده ، فلو كان الوجوب صفة موجودة ] (٦) لزم أن يكون حصول تلك الصفة الموجودة للماهية سابقا على وجود تلك الماهية في نفسها . وهذا محال . لأن وجود الشيء في نفسه [ متقدم بالرتبة على وجود غيره له. فأما أن يقال : إن وجود غيره له ، متقدم بالرتبة على وجوده في نفسه. فهذا] (٧) محال لا يقبله العقل .
The fifth proof: An exposition that necessity (wujub) cannot be an existent attribute. This is because a thing does not exist unless it is necessary, so its necessity precedes its existence. If necessity were an existent attribute, then the occurrence of that existent attribute to the quiddity would be prior to the existence of that quiddity itself. This is impossible. Because the existence of a thing in itself is prior in rank to the existence of something else for it. As for saying that the existence of something else for it is prior in rank to its existence in itself, this is impossible, reason does not accept it.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This is the fifth proof (al-wajh al-khamis) that wujub cannot be an existent attribute. The reasoning: A thing's existence depends on its necessity (al-shay' ma lam yajib lam yujad). Therefore, the necessity of a thing is prior (mutaqaddim) to its existence. If necessity were an existent attribute, then it would have to be an attribute that occurs (husul) to the quiddity (mahiyya) before that quiddity exists in itself. This is impossible because the existence of a thing in itself is prior in rank (bi al-rutba) to any attribute that is for it from another. Granting that an attribute of something else could be prior to the existence of the thing itself is absurd and reason rejects it. So the temporal/ontological priority of necessity over existence shows that necessity cannot itself be an existent attribute.

Key Terms
وجوب (wujub)
وجود (wujud)
متقدم (mutaqaddim)
ماهية (mahiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that logical priority (necessity) is temporal priority, and thus an attribute could be temporally prior without contradiction.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that even logical priority of an attribute is impossible because the attribute must 'occur' to the quiddity, which presupposes the quiddity's existence.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Razi argue that wujub cannot be an existent attribute?
A) Because necessity is posterior to existence.
B) Because necessity would have to exist before the quiddity it qualifies.
C) Because existence is prior to necessity.
D) Because attributes are always non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Necessity cannot be an attribute because it is a condition for existence and thus prior to the thing itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi asserts that 'a thing does not exist unless it is necessary.' Do you live as if your own existence is contingent and dependent on Allah's necessity, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change today if you truly felt that you exist only because of an external necessity?
?
This lesson argues that necessity cannot be an attribute because an attribute cannot precede the existence of the thing it qualifies. When you think of Allah, do you subconsciously imagine 'necessary existence' as a quality He possesses? How does this proof challenge that mental image, and what does it mean to worship a being whose necessity is not an attribute but rather the very ground of all existence?

The Sixth Proof: Mental Construction of 'Wajib al-Wujud'
Al-Razi begins the sixth proof by analyzing the judgment 'wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi' as requiring mental composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's initial step in arguing that the judgment 'wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi' does not correspond to an actual attribute, but requires a mental process of composition.

Core Idea
The phrase 'necessary of existence' is a mental judgment, not a simple attribute.

Al-Razi begins his sixth proof by examining what happens in the mind when we say something is necessary of existence. He argues that this judgment does not yield a simple concept; it involves a complex mental act of positing a reality, then existence, then their relation. This lesson unpacks that first step.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening of Al-Razi's proof
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting how Al-Razi sets up the analysis of the judgment.
2
Identify the three mental steps
The passage lists three suppositions: a reality, existence, and the qualification. See how they build on each other.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why Al-Razi thinks this composition shows that 'necessary of existence' is not a real attribute but a mental construct.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : إذا قلنا في شيء : إنه واجب الوجود لذاته ، فهذا التصديق لا يحصل منه في العقل أمر [محصل] (۸) إلا إذا فرضنا حقيقة ، ثم فرضناها موصوفة بالوجود ، ثم فرضنا أن موصوفية تلك الحقيقة بذلك الوجود
The sixth proof: When we say of something that it is necessary of existence by itself, this judgment does not yield any definite thing [muḥaṣṣal] in the intellect unless we suppose a reality, then suppose it qualified by existence, then suppose that the qualification of that reality by that existence...
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This is the beginning of the sixth proof (al-wajh al-sadis) that wujub cannot be an existent attribute. Al-Razi begins by analyzing the judgment 'it is necessary of existence by itself' (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi). He argues that this judgment does not produce any determinate concept (amr muḥaṣṣal) in the mind except through a mental process: we first suppose a reality (haqiqa), then suppose it qualified by existence (mawsufa bi al-wujud), and then suppose that qualification of the reality by that existence. The argument is evidently unfolding but the preserved text breaks off. The complete argument would likely show that such mental composition does not correspond to an actual attribute inhering in the essence.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود لذاته (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi)
تصديق (tasdiq)
حقيقة (haqiqa)
موصوفية (mawsufiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Someone might assume that saying 'wajib al-wujud' directly implies an attribute of necessity inhering in the essence.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the judgment is a mental construct requiring composition; it does not correspond to a simple, real attribute.

Checkpoint

A) Supposing a reality, then existence, then their relation.
B) Simply affirming existence without any composition.
C) Denying any reality.
D) Affirming an attribute of necessity.

Key Takeaway
The judgment 'wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi' does not directly give an attribute; it requires mental composition of reality, existence, and qualification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is necessary of existence,' do you merely repeat the phrase, or do you actively engage your mind in the three-step process al-Razi demands? Which of those steps do you most often overlook, and how does that neglect affect the depth of your faith?

If the simplest statement about Allah requires such careful mental composition, are you willing to put in the effort to truly understand His attributes, or do you prefer the comfort of lazy repetition? How might this intellectual laziness manifest in your worship?
?
Al-Razi shows that even the 'first' attribute we affirm is mentally constructed. Does this humble your claim to have direct knowledge of Allah? Are you ready to accept that your understanding is always composite, always mediated by your finite mind, and to let that drive you to seek Him humbly rather than arrogantly?

The Fourth Proof: Wujub Cannot Be a Positive Concept
Sanusi's fourth proof that wujub cannot be a concept (mafhum).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof that wujub (necessity) cannot be a positive concept, because it is predicated of non-existence and predicates must share the ontological status of their subjects.

Core Idea
Wujub is predicated of non-existence, so it cannot be an existing attribute.

This lesson presents the fourth argument from the text demonstrating that wujub (necessity) cannot be a positive concept. The proof hinges on a logical principle: the predicate inherits the ontological status of the subject. Since wujub is predicated of non-existence (as in 'it is necessary that it does not exist'), wujub itself must be non-existence, not a positive attribute. This argument challenges any assumption that wujub is a positive attribute of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow settle before interpreting.
Notice the brackets indicate uncertain text; the core argument is clear.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that the term wujub is predicated of non-existence. Find the phrase that makes this claim.
Look for 'مسمى الوجوب محمول على العدم'.
3
Understand the reasoning
The proof uses a principle: what is predicated of non-existence must itself be non-existence. Grasp why this principle is necessary.
If it were existence, then non-existence would be described by an existing attribute—impossible.
4
See the consequence
Therefore, wujub cannot be an existing attribute. It is not a positive concept.
This conclusion is directly stated at the end.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters for theology: if wujub is not a positive attribute, then what does it mean to say God is necessary?
This connects to broader debates about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : في بيان أن الوجوب الذاتي يمتنع أن يكون مفهوماً : هو أن مسمى الوجوب محمول على العدم [المحض، والمحمول على العدم عدم . فالوجوب عدم . وإنما قلنا : إن الوجوب محمول على العدم] لأن الشيء الذي يصدق عليه [أنه يمتنع أن يوجد يصدق عليه] لا محالة أنه واجب أن [لا] يوجد . فههنا المفهوم من الوجوب محمول على اللاوجود . فثبت أن الوجوب محمول على العدم. وأما إن المحمول على العدم : عدم ، فلأنه لو كان وجودا لزم أن يكون العدم المحض موصوفا بالصفة الموجودة ، وأنه محال . فثبت أن مسمى الوجوب، لا يمكن أن يكون صفة موجودة .
The fourth aspect: in explaining that self-necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) is impossible that it be a concept (mafhum): it is that the denotation of necessity (wujub) is predicated of non-existence [pure, and what is predicated of non-existence is non-existence. So necessity is non-existence. And we only say that necessity is predicated of non-existence] because the thing of which it is true [that it is impossible that it exists is true of it] necessarily that it is necessary that [it does not] exist. So here the concept of necessity is predicated of non-existence. So it is established that necessity is predicated of non-existence. As for that what is predicated of non-existence is non-existence, because if it were existence, then pure non-existence would be described by an existing attribute, and that is impossible. So it is established that the denotation of necessity cannot be an existing attribute.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-wajh al-rabi') that wujub (necessity) cannot be a concept (mafhum) in the sense of a positive attribute. The argument proceeds as follows: (1) The term wujub is predicated of non-existence (al-'adam) because we say 'it is necessary that such-and-such does not exist'. (2) That which is predicated of non-existence must itself be non-existence; otherwise, non-existence would be described by an existing attribute, which is impossible. (3) Therefore, wujub cannot be an existing attribute (sifa mawjuda). This proof relies on the principle that predicates inherit the ontological status of their subjects.

Key Takeaway

The proof shows that wujub is predicated of non-existence — something 'negative'. Have you been treating God's attributes as if they were human-like concepts? Sanusi argues that if wujub were an existing attribute, non-existence would be qualified by existence — an absurdity. The Fifth Proof: Wujub Cannot Be an Existing Attribute
Fifth proof that wujub cannot be an existing attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why wujub (necessity) cannot be an existing attribute, based on the logical priority of necessity over existence.

Core Idea
Necessity must precede existence, so it cannot be an existing attribute itself.

This lesson presents the fifth rational proof from the classical kalam tradition, demonstrating that the attribute of necessity (wujub) cannot be a real, existent attribute. The argument relies on the simple but powerful observation that necessity must come before existence, making it impossible for necessity to be an existent thing itself. As you read the passage, notice how the proof unfolds from a basic premise about causality and priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the passage once in Arabic, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's structure become clear.
Notice the concise style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the key premise
Find the statement: 'a thing does not exist until its necessity obtains.' This is the starting point of the proof.
3
Understand the conditional
Follow the reasoning: if necessity were an existing attribute, then that attribute would have to exist before the essence it describes. Grasp why this is problematic.
4
Grasp the impossibility argument
The proof explains that a thing's own existence is prior in rank to any attribute's existence for it. Therefore, an attribute cannot exist before its subject.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that necessity cannot be an existing attribute. Think about what this means for the nature of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : في بيان أن الوجوب يمتنع أن يكون صفة موجودة : وذلك لأن الشيء ما لم يجب لم يوجد ، فوجوبه متقدم على وجوده ، فلو كان الوجوب صفة موجودة لزم أن يكون حصول تلك الصفة الموجودة للماهية سابقا على وجود تلك الماهية في نفسها . وهذا محال . لأن وجود الشيء في نفسه [ متقدم بالرتبة على وجود غيره له. فأما أن يقال : إن وجود غيره له ، متقدم بالرتبة على وجوده في نفسه. فهذا] محال لا يقبله العقل .
The fifth aspect: in explaining that necessity is impossible to be an existing attribute: that is because a thing, as long as it is not necessary, does not exist, so its necessity precedes its existence. Thus if necessity were an existing attribute, it would necessitate that the obtaining of that existing attribute for the essence be prior to the existence of that essence itself. And this is impossible. Because the existence of a thing in itself is prior in rank to the existence of something else for it. As for it being said that the existence of something else for it is prior in rank to its existence in itself, this is impossible, not accepted by the intellect.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof that wujub cannot be an existing attribute (sifa mawjuda). The reasoning is: (1) A thing does not exist until its wujub (necessity) obtains; thus wujub is temporally or ontologically prior to the thing's existence. (2) If wujub were an existing attribute, then that existing attribute would have to exist before the essence it qualifies. (3) But an attribute's existence depends on the existence of its subject (the essence); the subject's existence is prior in rank. (4) Therefore, wujub cannot be an existing attribute, or it would require an impossible priority.

Key Takeaway

The Sixth Proof: Necessity as Relational and Contingent
Sixth proof that wujub is relational and thus contingent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessity (wujub) cannot be an existing attribute because it is relational and thus contingent.

Core Idea
Necessity is a relational attribute dependent on its subject, making it contingent.

This passage presents the sixth proof from the author that the attribute of necessity (wujub) is not an inherent property but a relational one. By analyzing what it means to say something is 'necessary of existence by itself', the argument shows that necessity depends on a subject and a predicate, and anything dependent is contingent. This leads to a contradiction if necessity is taken as self-necessity, proving it cannot be an existing attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage aloud
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation to understand the argument in your own words.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if we say... then...'
2
Identify the three suppositions
The passage says that for the concept of 'necessary by itself' to form in the mind, we must suppose three things: an essence, its existence, and the attribute of necessity attaching to that existence. These are the building blocks of the argument.
These three suppositions are the key to seeing necessity as relational.
3
Understand necessity as a relational attribute
The passage argues that necessity is an attribute of the relation (nisba) between subject (essence) and predicate (existence). This means necessity is not a simple property but depends on the relation.
A relational attribute requires a subject and something related to it.
4
Trace the dependency chain
The attribute (necessity) depends on the relation, and the relation depends on the subject. Since the subject is something else (ghayr), necessity depends on another. Anything dependent on another is contingent (mumkin bi-dhatih).
The chain: necessity → relation → subject → other → contingency.
5
Grasp the contradiction
If necessity itself is contingent, then 'necessary by itself' would be contingent by itself, which is contradictory. Therefore, necessity cannot be an existing attribute.
The conclusion: wujub bi-dhatih cannot be a wasf mawjud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : إذا قلنا في شيء : إنه واجب الوجود لذاته ، فهذا التصديق لا يحصل منه في العقل أمر [محصل] إلا إذا فرضنا حقيقة ، ثم فرضناها موصوفة بالوجود ، ثم فرضنا أن موصوفية تلك الحقيقة بذلك الوجود موصوفية منعوثة بنعت الوجوب واللزوم وإذا كان كذلك ، فهذا الوجوب صفة من صفات النسبة الحاصلة بين الموضوع وبين المحمول ، وتلك النسبة صفة من صفات ذلك الموضوع . وصفة الشيء مفتقرة [إلى الموصوف، والمفتقر إلى الشيء ، أولى بالافتقار ، فيثبت أن الوجوب مفتقر] إلى الغير ، والمفتقر [إلى الغير] ، والمفتقر [إلى الغير] ممكن لذاته . فالوجوب بالذات ممكن بالذات، وذلك متناقض وباطل . فثبت أن الوجوب بالذات لا يمكن أن يكون وصفا موجودا .
The sixth aspect: if we say of something that it is necessary of existence by itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatih), then this assent does not result in the intellect an [established] thing unless we suppose an essence, then suppose it described by existence, then suppose that the being-described of that essence by that existence is a being-described adorned with the attribute of necessity (wujub) and persistence (luzum). And if that is the case, then this necessity (wujub) is an attribute of the relation (nisba) subsisting between the subject (mawdu') and the predicate (mahmul), and that relation is an attribute of that subject. And the attribute of a thing is dependent [upon the described (mawsuf), and the dependent (muftaqir) upon a thing is more deserving of dependence (iftiqar), so it is established that necessity is dependent] upon another (al-ghayr), and the dependent [upon another] is contingent by itself (mumkin bi-dhatih). So necessity by itself is contingent by itself, and that is contradictory and false. So it is established that necessity by itself cannot be an existing description (wasf mawjud).
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof that wujub cannot be an existing attribute. The argument: (1) When we say something is 'necessary of existence by itself', the mind forms a concept only by positing an essence, then its existence, then the attribute of necessity attaching to that existence. (2) This necessity is actually an attribute of the relation between the subject (essence) and the predicate (existence). (3) Any attribute depends on its subject; thus necessity depends on something else (the essence or the relation). (4) What depends on another is contingent (mumkin bi-dhatih). (5) Hence, if wujub were an attribute, it would be contingent, contradicting its definition as self-necessity. Therefore, wujub cannot be an existing attribute.

Key Terms
الوجوب بالذات
نسبة
موضوع
محمول
مفتقر
ممكن بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think necessity is a simple, non-relational property inherent in an essence.
Correction: The proof shows necessity is actually relational: it depends on a subject and a predicate, making it contingent rather than self-subsistent.

Checkpoint
What does the Sixth Proof conclude about wujub bi-dhatih (self-necessity)?
A) It is a simple, non-relational attribute
B) It is an existing attribute
C) It is contingent because it depends on another
D) It is the same as existence

Key Takeaway
If necessity were an existing attribute, it would be contingent because it depends on a subject, contradicting its definition as self-necessity.

?
This proof shows that any attribute of necessity we ascribe to ourselves or to created things is actually relational and dependent. How can you constantly affirm that they are contingent upon Allah? Commit to a daily dhikr that reinforces this dependence.
?

Three Opinions on Existence and the First Proof
Classification of three opinions on existence and first proof against homonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three major opinions on the meaning of existence in Islamic theology and the first rational proof against the view that 'existent' is purely homonymous.

Core Idea
Existence and non-existence are mutual opposites, so existence must be a single, univocal concept.

The author begins by classifying the three main positions on the meaning of the term 'existent' (wujud) when applied to God and creation. This classification is crucial because it sets the stage for the subsequent arguments. After outlining the positions and their proponents, he launches the first proof against the view that 'existent' is purely homonymous, drawing on the logical opposition between existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's classification come through clearly.
The passage starts abruptly with the first opinion; the full context is understood from the classification.
2
Identify the three opinions
List the three opinions on the meaning of 'existent' as presented by the author. Note the differences between them.
The first opinion is given only as a phrase; the second and third are explained more fully.
3
Note the proponents and exhaustiveness claim
Find which scholars or schools support each opinion and the author's statement that these three opinions are exhaustive.
The author himself supports the third opinion.
4
Analyze the first proof
Focus on the argument from the opposition of existence and non-existence. Understand why this refutes the first opinion (pure homonymy).
The proof relies on the intuition of reason that opposites must be single concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاشتراك اللفظي فقط . والثاني قول من يقول : لفظ الموجود يفيد مفهوما واحدا، إلا أنه في حق واجب الوجود لذاته، وجودا مجردا، أعني أنه وجود بشرط كونه غير عارض لشيء من الماهيات، بل يكون وجودا قائما بنفسه، وبهذا التقدير يكون وجود الله تعالى نفس حقيقته . والثالث : قول من يقول : إن الوجود صفة من صفات حقيقة الله تعالى، ونعت من نعوت ماهيته، وبهذا التقدير فوجود الله غير ماهيته . وهذه الأقوال الثلاثة قد ذهب إلى كل واحد منها عالم من الناس . فالأول : قول طائفة عظيمة من المتكلمين كأبي الحسن الأشعري ، وأبي الحسين البصري . والثاني : هو القول الذي اختاره أبو علي بن سينا في جميع [كتبه] . والثالث : قول طائفة عظيمة من المتكلمين ، وهو الذي نصرناه في أكثر كتبنا . ويجب على العاقل أن يتأمل في هذا التقسيم الذي ذكرناه حتى يعلم يقينا أن هذه الأقوال الثلاثة لا مزيد عليها البتة . ونقول : أما القول الأول وهو أن لفظ الموجود واقع على الواجب لذاته ، وعلى الممكن لذاته ، لا بحسب مفهوم واحد ، بل بحسب الاشتراك اللفظي فقط . فنقول : إنا قد دللنا في مسائل الوجود على فساده ، ولا بأس بإعادة بعض تلك الوجوه في هذا المقام ، فنقول : الذي يدل على فساده وجوه : الأول : إن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن الوجود لا يقابله إلا العدم ، وإن العدم لا يقابله إلا الوجود، فوجب أن يكون الوجود مفهوما واحدا ، كما أن العدم مفهوم واحد ، حتى يصح ذلك التقابل بينهما .
Pure homonymy. The second is the statement of one who says: the word 'existent' conveys a single concept, except that in relation to the Necessary in itself, it is a pure existence, meaning an existence conditioned by not being accidental to any essence, but rather an existence subsisting by itself, and by this estimation the existence of God Most High is identical to His essence. The third is the statement of one who says: existence is an attribute among the attributes of God Most High's reality, and a description among the descriptions of His quiddity, and by this estimation the existence of God is other than His quiddity. These three statements have been adopted by a scholar for each. The first: the statement of a great group of theologians like Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. The second: the statement chosen by Abu Ali ibn Sina in all his books. The third: the statement of a great group of theologians, and it is the one we have supported in most of our books. It is incumbent upon the intelligent person to ponder this division we have mentioned until he knows with certainty that these three statements are absolutely exhaustive. We say: As for the first statement, which is that the word 'existent' applies to the Necessary in itself and to the possible in itself, not according to a single concept, but according to pure homonymy. We say: we have already demonstrated its corruption in the treatises on existence, and there is no harm in repeating some of those arguments in this context. We say: what indicates its corruption are arguments: The first: the intuition of reason judges that existence is only opposed by non-existence, and non-existence is only opposed by existence, so it must be that existence is a single concept, just as non-existence is a single concept, so that the opposition between them is correct.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of three opinions regarding the meaning of the term 'existent' (wujud) and then begins the refutation of the first opinion with the first proof. The author first states the three positions: (1) the word 'existent' is purely homonymous (homonymy) when applied to God and creatures, meaning it has different meanings; (2) it has a single concept but when applied to God it means a pure existence identical to His essence, while for creatures it is accidental; (3) existence is an attribute additional to the essence, so God's existence is other than His essence. The author notes the proponents: the first is held by many theologians like al-Ash'ari and al-Basri; the second by Ibn Sina; the third by many theologians and by the author himself. He emphasizes that this division is exhaustive. Then he begins refuting the first opinion. He first states that he has already proven its corruption elsewhere, but will repeat some arguments. The first argument is from the opposition between existence and non-existence: the rational intuition that existence and non-existence are mutually exclusive opposites implies that each is a single concept. If existence had multiple meanings, its opposite would also have to be multiple, but non-existence is a single concept, so existence must be univocal.

Key Terms
الوجود
الاشتراك اللفظي
الواجب لذاته
الممكن لذاته
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the word 'existent' is purely equivocal (homonymous) when applied to God and creatures, meaning it has completely different meanings.
Correction: The author argues that existence and non-existence are logical opposites. Since non-existence is a single concept, existence must also be a single concept for the opposition to hold. This refutes the idea of pure homonymy.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why must existence be a single concept?
A) Because existence can be divided into necessary and possible.
B) Because existence is opposed only to non-existence, and non-existence is a single concept.
C) Because the intuition of reason says so without reason.
D) Because all scholars agree on it.

Key Takeaway
The logical opposition between existence and non-existence proves that existence is a single, univocal concept, refuting the view of pure homonymy.

Why Division Proves Existence Has One Meaning
Second proof: division of existence implies a common meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fact that existence can be divided into necessary, possible, substance, and accident shows that the word 'existence' has a single shared meaning.

Core Idea
A real division needs a common basis—so existence cannot be homonymous.

This lesson presents the second proof against the claim that 'existence' is a purely homonymous term. The argument is simple but powerful: if we can legitimately divide existence into categories like necessary and possible, then the term must have a single meaning that applies to all those categories. Without a common meaning, the division would make no sense. Let's examine this proof and see how it defends the univocity of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the author states the division, then the key conclusion about the basis of division.
The passage is very concise—every word carries weight.
2
Identify the divisions mentioned
List the categories into which existence is divided: necessary (واجب), possible (ممكن), substance (جوهر), and accident (عرض). Ask yourself: are these really different ways of existing?
This shows existence is not just one thing but applies to many.
3
Understand the logical implication
The passage says the basis of division (مورد التقسيم) is common to all divisions. That means the term 'existence' is the single thread running through all these categories. If it were homonymous, there would be no common basis.
Think of a real division: 'animal' divided into 'human' and 'horse'—'animal' has one meaning common to both.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some might think that having different types means the word is used in different senses. But the author shows the opposite: division actually requires a shared meaning.
This proof is a key tool in kalam against radical homonymy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الوجود يصح تقسيمه إلى الواجب ، وإلى الممكن والجوهر والعرض ، ومورد التقسيم مشترك بين كل الأقسام
The second: existence can be divided into the necessary, and into the possible, substance and accident, and the basis of the division is common to all the divisions.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This is the second proof against the claim that the word 'existence' is purely homonymous. The author argues that existence can be legitimately divided into categories such as necessary and possible, substance and accident. The fact that we can divide existence in this way presupposes that the term 'existence' has a single meaning that is common to all the divisions. If the word were homonymous, there would be no common basis for the division, and the division would not be valid. Therefore, existence must be a univocal term.

Key Terms

الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage shows the opposite: a valid division of a term presupposes that the term has a single meaning common to all its divisions. If it were homonymous, the division would be meaningless.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The ability to divide existence into categories proves that 'existence' has a single, shared meaning across all of them.

The Compatibility Proof for Univocity of Existence
Third proof: compatibility of existence with other attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the compatibility of existence with necessity, possibility, and other attributes demonstrates that existence has a single common meaning.

Core Idea
Existence means the same whether applied to God or atoms.

The third argument for the univocity of existence is deceptively simple: we can simultaneously believe something exists and believe it is necessary, possible, a substance, or an accident. If 'existence' changed meaning with each case, such combined beliefs would be impossible. This short passage packs a powerful logical punch.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence once, then its translation. Notice the structure: a premise, then a conclusion marked by 'فوجب' (so it must be).
2
Recognize the two parts of the argument
Identify the premise (no contradiction between belief in existence and other beliefs) and the conclusion (therefore existence contains a common element).
3
Understand why this proves univocity
Reflect: if existence had multiple unrelated meanings, then believing something exists would block certain additional beliefs. Since it doesn't, the concept of existence must be the same throughout.
This is a 'compatibility' argument based on the consistency of belief.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question. It will ask about the core reasoning of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن اعتقاد كونه موجودا ، لا ينافيه اعتقاد كونه واجبا أو ممكنا أو جوهرا أو عرضا ، فوجب أن يكون المفهوم من كونه موجودا فيه قدر مشترك بين الكل .
The third: Belief that something is existent does not contradict belief that it is necessary or possible or substance or accident, so it must be that the concept of its being existent contains a common element among all.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This third proof argues that the concept of existence is compatible with various additional determinations such as being necessary, possible, substance, or accident. The fact that we can simultaneously believe that something is existent and also believe it is necessary (or possible, etc.) shows that the concept of existence itself does not conflict with these determinations. If existence had multiple unrelated meanings, then the concept of existence would change with each determination, and such compatibility would not hold. Therefore, there must be a common element in the concept of existence that is shared by all things that are said to exist.

Key Terms
اعتقاد
وجوب
إمكان
جوهر
عرض
قدر مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is an attribute that changes meaning when applied to God versus creatures, so it cannot be univocal.
Correction: The compatibility proof shows that existence can be believed alongside necessity (divine) or possibility (creaturely), indicating the same concept is used. If meanings differed, such beliefs would conflict.

Checkpoint
How does the compatibility of existence with necessity and possibility prove univocity?
A) Because necessity and possibility are contradictory.
B) Because if existence had different meanings, it would conflict with necessity.
C) Because the belief of existence does not negate other beliefs, indicating a single concept.
D) Because all things are necessary or possible.

Key Takeaway
Existence is univocal because the belief in existence does not contradict belief in any other attribute, implying a shared concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Fourth Proof: Existence is Univocal
Analogy between existence and volume to show univocity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of existence (being realized) is univocal, based on an analogy with the concept of volume.

Core Idea
Existence means the same thing for all things, just as volume does.

This fourth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah uses a powerful analogy to show that existence is a univocal concept. Just as our rational intuition recognizes a single meaning of 'volume' across all spatial objects, so too it recognizes a single meaning of 'being realized' across all existents—whether necessary or possible, black or white. Let's examine this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the key analogy between existence and volume.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بديهة العقل' (intuition of reason).
2
Understand the analogy
The author compares existence (being realized) to volume. Volume is a single concept despite differences in density, temperature, etc. Similarly, existence is a single concept despite differences in essences.
3
Identify the rational intuition
Recognize that the argument relies on the innate judgment of reason (بديهة العقل) that these concepts are univocal.
This is a foundational point for the proof.
4
Reflect on the implication
If existence is univocal, then when we say 'Allah exists' and 'the world exists', 'exists' has the same meaning, differing only in the subject.
This supports the possibility of proving God's existence through shared attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن المراد من كونه موجودا، كونه محصلا في الاعتبار، محققا في الأعيان، وهذا المفهوم لا يختلف بأن يكون ذلك المحصل [المحقق] واجبا أو ممكنا. أو سوادا أو بياضا، كما أنه لما كان المعقول من الحجمية: التحيز والامتداد في الجهات، لم يختلف ذلك باختلاف كونه لطيفا أو كثيفا أو حارا أو باردا، وكما أن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن المفهوم من الحجمية أمر واحد، فكذلك حاكمة بأن المفهوم من الحصول والتحقق أمر واحد لا يختلف باختلاف الحقائق
The fourth: The intuition of reason judges that what is meant by something being existent is that it is realized in the mind and actualized in external entities, and this concept does not differ whether that which is realized is necessary or possible, or black or white. Just as the intelligible of volume is occupation of space and extension in directions, and that does not differ with differences of being fine or dense or hot or cold, and just as the intuition of reason judges that the concept of volume is one thing, so too it judges that the concept of being attained and realized is one thing that does not differ with differences of realities.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This fourth proof draws an analogy between the concept of existence (being realized) and the concept of volume. Just as the concept of volume—understood as occupying space and having extension—remains the same regardless of whether the object is fine, dense, hot, or cold, similarly, the concept of being realized (i.e., existence) remains the same whether the existent is necessary or possible, black or white. The rational intuition perceives existence as simply being present either mentally or externally, and this concept does not vary with the differences in the essences of things. Therefore, existence is univocal.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
محصل في الاعتبار
محقق في الأعيان
الحجمية
التحيز
الامتداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that existence has different meanings for different kinds of entities (e.g., necessary vs. possible).
Correction: The analogy with volume shows that existence, like volume, is a single concept that applies to all things without variation in meaning.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to argue for the univocity of existence?
A) The analogy between existence and non-existence.
B) The analogy between existence and volume.
C) The analogy between existence and color.
D) The analogy between existence and heat.

Key Takeaway
The concept of existence is univocal; rational intuition recognizes it as a single meaning across all existents, just as volume is one concept despite variations in physical properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is a single concept shared by all things—including you and your Creator—does that make you feel closer to God or more accountable? How does this truth change the way you view your own fleeting existence? Commit today to recite 'Al-Ḥayy al-Qayyūm' and reflect that your existence is a constant gift from the One whose existence is necessary.
?
The passage says existence is one concept whether something is necessary or possible. Do you live as though your existence is just as 'real' as God's, or do you acknowledge that your existence depends entirely on Him? What would it mean for your daily choices if you truly believed that existence is a loan, not a possession? Decide tonight to thank Allah for every breath as a renewed act of existence.

Author's Objection: Essence vs. Attributes
Author critiques misinterpretation of the unknowable essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of those who misinterpret the phrase 'the unknown essence' as referring to attributes, and why this interpretation is logically inconsistent.

Core Idea
The essence and attributes are distinct; you cannot substitute one for the other in theological arguments.

The author expresses astonishment at scholars who try to reconcile two incompatible doctrines by reinterpreting 'unknown essence' as 'unknown attributes.' This lesson unpacks his objection and his clear distinction between knowledge of the essence and knowledge of the attributes.

Guided Path
1

The passage cuts off mid-sentence. The full refutation is not present, but the direction of argument is clear.

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. The author refutes this by pointing out that knowledge of attributes is distinct from knowledge of the essence, and the philosophers explicitly said 'the inner reality of the essence' not 'attributes.' The text cuts off before completing the refutation.

Tenth Proof: Existence as Predicate in the Statement 'God is Necessarily Existent'
Razi's tenth proof: linguistic analysis of 'God is necessarily existent' shows existence is a predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the subject-predicate structure of the proposition 'God is necessarily existent' to argue that existence is additional to essence.

Core Idea
The sentence 'God is existent' already proves existence is not identical to God.

In this brief but powerful proof, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi points to the linguistic structure of a common theological statement. He argues that the very words 'God is necessarily existent' contain a subject ('God'), a predicate ('existent'), and an attribute ('necessary'). By recognizing that these elements are distinct, one can see that existence is a predicate added to the subject, not the subject itself. This simple observation forms the basis of his tenth proof for existence as an attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence from the source. Notice its brevity. The author states it as a proof without elaboration.
The Arabic is: الحجة العاشرة: لا شك أن قولنا: الله واجب الوجود، كلام حق وصدق. فقولنا: الله موضوع، وقولنا: موجود محمول، وقولنا: واجب صفة.
2
Identify the three components
Identify the subject (موضوع), predicate (محمول), and attribute (صفة) as Razi labels them. The subject is 'God', the predicate is 'existent', and the attribute is 'necessary'.
Notice that 'necessary' describes the predicate, not the subject.
3
Understand the logical implications
Razi uses the fact that the subject and predicate are linguistically distinct to argue that existence is not identical to the essence. If they were identical, the statement would be tautological.
This is a key point in the debate over existence being additional to essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة: لا شك أن قولنا: الله واجب الوجود، كلام حق وصدق. فقولنا: الله موضوع، وقولنا: موجود محمول، وقولنا: واجب صفة.
The tenth proof: There is no doubt that our saying 'God is necessarily existent' is true and correct speech. So our saying 'God' is a subject, our saying 'existent' is a predicate, and our saying 'necessary' is an attribute.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is the tenth and briefest proof that existence is additional to essence. The author simply points to the linguistic structure of the statement 'God is necessarily existent' (Allah wajib al-wujud). In this proposition, 'God' is the subject (mawdu'), 'existent' (mawjud) is the predicate (mahmul), and 'necessary' (wajib) is an attribute (sifa) of the predicate. Since the subject and predicate are distinct terms, this shows that existence is a predicate attributed to a subject (the quiddity of God), not identical to it. The proof relies on the obvious truth that in a subject-predicate statement, the subject is not the same as the predicate. Therefore, God's existence is additional to His essence.

Key Terms
موضوع
محمول
صفة
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this proof is merely grammatical wordplay without logical force.
Correction: Razi intends this as a logical argument: the linguistic distinction mirrors a metaphysical distinction between essence and existence. The proposition 'God is existent' is informative, not a tautology, showing existence is not identical to the divine essence.

Checkpoint
In Razi's tenth proof, what does the word 'necessary' (واجب) function as?
A) The subject
B) The predicate
C) An attribute
D) The copula

Key Takeaway
Razi's tenth proof uses the subject-predicate structure of the statement 'God is necessarily existent' to argue that existence is a predicate added to the divine essence, not identical to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that existence is an attribute added to God's essence, not identical to it, then how does that change the way you approach your own existence? Are you living as if existence is a mere fact, or as a gift that demands a response from you?
?
When you say 'God exists,' do you treat that statement as a mere grammatical truth or as a reality that must shape every moment of your life? What would it look like to live today as if the subject 'God' and the predicate 'exists' are not the same—meaning His existence is an added reality that requires your worship and obedience?

God's Essence Is Not Existence: The Main Argument
Proof that God's essence is other than existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the subject 'God' in the statement 'God exists' cannot be existence itself, and therefore existence is an attribute of a distinct essence.

Core Idea
The statement 'God exists' cannot be a tautology; God's essence is other than His existence.

In this passage, the author sets out to demonstrate that when we say 'God exists', the subject 'God' does not mean existence itself. He uses a logical analysis of subject-predicate relations to argue that existence must be an attribute of a distinct reality. This argument counters claims that God's essence is identical to His existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure of the argument as it unfolds.
The argument is technical; take your time.
2
Identify the two possibilities for the subject 'God'
The author states that the subject 'God' in the proposition must either be existence itself or something other than existence. Understand that this is the starting point of the proof.
The first possibility is rejected; the second leads to the desired conclusion.
3
Examine the refutation of the first possibility
The author gives two reasons why interpreting 'God' as existence fails. First, it makes the statement circular or redundant. Second, since existence has one nature, subject and predicate would be identical, making the predication arbitrary.
Pay attention to the examples of 'wall: wall' and 'triangle: triangle'.
4
Understand the conclusion that existence is an attribute
The author concludes that the subject 'God' must refer to a reality other than existence, and existence is an attribute of that reality. This is the main point of the lesson.
This conclusion is the 'desired conclusion' (وذلك هو المطلوب).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لانتساب الموضوع إلى المحمول، ونقول : المراد بالموضوع وهو قولنا الله ، إما أن يكون هو الوجود، أو أمرا مغايرا للوجود ، والأول باطل لوجوه : أحدها : أن على هذا التقدير يصير معنى الكلام أن الوجود واجب الوجود. ولكن هذا باطل ، لأن الوجود الذي جعل موضوعا، إما أن يكون هو الوجود الذي جعل محمولا أو غيره ، والأول باطل لأن جعل الشيء موضوعا لنفسه ومحمولا لنفسه ، كلام فاسد ، عار عن الفائدة. فإن من قال : الجدار : جدار . والمثلث : مثلث . كلامه كلام فاسد ، وإن كان الثاني لزم أن يكون الوجود الذي جعل موضوعا ، مغايرا للوجود الذي جعل محمولا ، فحينئذ يلزم من كونه موجودا : خبرين وأيضا : فلما كانت طبيعة الوجود واحدة ، كان الوجود الذي هو الموضوع مساويا في تمام الماهية للوجود الذي هو المحمول ، وحينئذ لم يكن أحدهما بأن يكون موصوفا والآخر بأن يكون صفة ، أولى من العكس . فيثبت بهذا البرهان : أنا إذا قلنا : الله يجب أن يكون موجودا ، يكن المشار إليه بقولنا نفس الوجودية ، بل حقيقة أخرى سوى الوجودية ، وقد صدق حكم العقل عليها بوجوب اتصافها بالموجودية ، وذلك يدل على أن وجود الله تعالى ليس أمرا قائما بنفسه بل هو صفة لماهية أخرى . وذلك هو المطلوب لم فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من قولنا : الله ، حقيقة لا ندري ماهيتها ولا تعينها ولكنا نعرف في الجملة كونها موجودة ؟ فنقول : هب أنا لا نعرف أن تلك الحقيقة ما هي ؟ إلا أنا نعرف منها أنها إما أن تكون هي الوجود ، أو حقيقة مغايرة للوجود فإن كانت هي الوجود ، لزمت المحالات . وإن كانت حقيقة مغايرة للوجود لزم كون وجود الله تعالى صفة لتلك الحقيقة . وذلك هو المطلوب. ولما كان هذا الكلام الذي يفيد مقصودنا متقرر ، سواء عقلنا خصوصية تلك الحقيقة ، أو لم تعقلها ، لم يكن عدم علمنا بتلك الحقيقة المخصوصة من حيث إنها هي قادحا في مطلوبنا
Regarding the attribution of the subject to the predicate, we say: the intended meaning of the subject, which is our saying 'God', is either existence or something other than existence. The first is false for several reasons: One: on this assumption the meaning of the statement becomes 'existence is necessarily existent'. But this is false, because the existence made the subject is either the same existence made the predicate or other. The first is false because making a thing subject to itself and predicate to itself is corrupt speech, devoid of benefit. For he who says 'the wall: wall' and 'the triangle: triangle' speaks corrupt speech. And if it is the second, then the existence made subject must be different from the existence made predicate; then it necessarily follows from its being existent: two [occurrences?]. Also: since the nature of existence is one, the existence which is the subject is equal in the whole essence to the existence which is the predicate, and then neither is more deserving to be described and the other to be a description than the opposite. Thus it is proven by this proof that when we say 'God must be existent', what is referred to by our saying is not existence itself, but another reality besides existence, and the judgment of reason has attested to it that it is necessarily qualified by existence. And that indicates that the existence of God Most High is not a self-subsistent thing but rather an attribute of another essence. And that is the desired conclusion. Then they said: Why is it not permissible that what is meant by our saying 'God' be a reality whose quiddity and specification we do not know, but we know in general that it is existent? We say: Grant that we do not know what that reality is; but we know of it that it is either existence or a reality other than existence. If it is existence, the impossibilities follow. And if it is a reality other than existence, then the existence of God Most High is an attribute of that reality. That is the desired conclusion. And since this speech which benefits our purpose is established, whether we understand the specificity of that reality or not, our lack of knowledge of that specific reality in itself does not detract from our objective.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that God's essence is not identical to existence, followed by an objection and its answer. The argument begins by stating that in the proposition 'God exists', the subject 'God' either means existence itself or something other than existence. The author proves the first option false with two reasons. First, if 'God' means existence, then 'God exists' becomes 'existence is necessarily existent', which is either circular (if the subject and predicate are the same existence) or redundant (if they are different existences, implying two existences). The second reason is that existence has a single nature, so if subject and predicate are both existence, they are identical in essence, making predication arbitrary. Therefore, 'God' must refer to a reality other than existence, and existence is an attribute of that reality. Then an objector suggests that perhaps we do not know what 'God' refers to, only that it exists. The author responds that even if we do not know its specific nature, we still know that it must be either existence or not; if existence, the previous impossibilities recur; if not, then existence is an attribute of that unknown reality, which is exactly what the argument intended to prove. Thus the conclusion holds regardless of our ignorance of the specific essence.

Key Terms
موضوع
محمول
وجود
ماهية
صفة
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument implies God does not exist.
Correction: The argument only proves that existence is an attribute of God's essence, not that He lacks existence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what problem arises if the subject 'God' is interpreted as existence itself?
A) It implies that God does not exist
B) It makes the statement 'God exists' redundant or circular
C) It denies God's attributes
D) It leads to polytheism

Key Takeaway
God's essence is not identical to His existence; rather, existence is an attribute of a distinct reality referred to as 'God'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God exists', do you ever catch yourself treating that statement as a mere fact, like a label, without letting it pierce your heart with the reality of a distinct, personal Being whose essence is beyond your comprehension? In what area of your life are you making your own tautologies — repeating comfortable truths about yourself that you never truly examine?
?
The argument shows that existence is an attribute of God's essence, not the essence itself. Have you ever reduced your concept of God to just 'existence' — a vague force or principle — rather than a personal, living reality with attributes that demand a response from you? How does that distinction change the urgency of your worship today?
?
This passage criticizes meaningless speech like 'the wall: wall'. What words do you speak about God — in prayer, in conversation — that have become empty repetitions, devoid of the awe that comes from knowing you speak of a reality beyond existence itself? Will you stop and truly mean every name and attribute you utter from now on?
