Answering the Objection: Ignorance of the Essence
Refutation of an objection based on ignorance of God's quiddity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that our lack of knowledge of God's essence invalidates the argument that His essence is not existence.

Core Idea
Even if we do not know God's specific reality, we still know it must be either existence or other; both lead to the same conclusion.

After establishing the main argument, the author anticipates an objection: perhaps we do not know what 'God' refers to, only that He exists. The author responds that even without knowing the specific quiddity, the logical alternatives remain and force the same conclusion. This strengthens the argument by showing it is independent of our epistemic limitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's statement: 'Why is it not permissible that what is meant by our saying 'God' be a reality whose quiddity and specification we do not know, but we know in general that it is existent?'
Note the concession: we know it exists but not its essence.
2
Understand the author's response
The author grants the concession but points out that we still know the reality is either existence or something else. Both options lead to the same conclusion: existence is an attribute.
The response uses a dilemma to show the conclusion follows regardless.
3
Appreciate the robustness of the argument
The author emphasizes that the argument does not depend on knowing the specific nature of God's essence; it works even with incomplete knowledge.
This shows the argument's strength against skeptical objections.

Correction: The author shows the argument works even without full knowledge because the logical alternatives are exhaustive.

God's Essence Is Not Existence Alone
The author presents the eleventh proof that God's essence is not mere existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eleventh proof from Al-Matalib al-'Aliyah that God's specific essence is not identical to the common concept of existence, and how the argument proceeds through dichotomies and elimination.

Core Idea
God's essence is not existence itself; existence is an attribute of a specific, positive essence.

This passage presents a sophisticated logical proof that God's essence is not the same as the common concept of existence. The author first establishes that God is a specific essence and that existence is a shared concept. He then systematically eliminates possibilities until only one remains: that God's essence includes a positive quality, with existence as its attribute. Follow the reasoning carefully to see how each option is ruled out.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the author's argument unfold without interrupting. Then re-read to identify the logical steps.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument starts with two undeniable premises: God is a specific essence distinct from all others, and existence is a common concept shared by all existents. Write these down.
3
Follow the first dichotomy
The author presents a dichotomy: God's essence is either the common concept of existence alone, or that concept plus an additional qualification. Notice how the first option is refuted by the fact that the common concept is predicated of many, but God's essence is unique.
This refutation is simple but crucial.
4
Analyze the qualification (privative vs positive)
The second option leads to a sub-dichotomy: the extra qualification is either privative (negative) or positive. The privative is ruled out because a privation cannot be part of an essence. Focus on why that is impossible.
The author uses a concise argument about part-whole relationship.
5
Understand the subject-predicate analysis and conclusion
The positive qualification is then analyzed in terms of subject and predicate. Two possibilities: either the specific quality is subject and existence predicate (desired), or existence is subject and specificity predicate (false for two logical reasons). Trace the two reasons and see how the conclusion follows.
The second reason involves a contradiction if existence becomes non-common.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر في إثبات هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : لا شك أن الحق تعالى ذات متعينة ممتازة بتعينها عن كل ما سواء من الذوات المعينة ، ولا شك أن الوجود من حيث إنه وجود حقيقة يشترك فيها الواجب والممكن والجوهر والعرض ، فنقول : ذاته المخصوصة إما أن يكون هو هذا المفهوم المشترك فيه بين كل الموجودات ، وإما أن يكون هو هذا المفهوم ، مع قيد زائد يقتضي تخصصه وتعينه . والأول باطل ، لأن هذا المفهوم المشترك يصدق عليه كونه مشتركا بين الكل ، وذاته المخصوصة لا يصدق عليها هذا المعنى . فبطل القول بأن ذاته المخصوصة هو هذا المفهوم المشترك بقي أن يقال : إنه هو هذا المفهوم مع قيد زائد . فنقول : ذلك القيد الزائد إما أن يكون سلبيا أو ثبوتيا ، والأول باطل ، لأنه سبحانه من حيث إنه هو ذلك المعين موجود ، وما لأجله التعين جزءا من المعين ، فلو كان ما لأجله حصل التعين قيدا سلبيا ، لزم أن يكون القيد السلبي جزءا من الماهية الثابتة ، وهو محال . وأما إن كان القيد الذي لأجله حصل التعين أمرا ثابتا فإما أن يكون ذلك الأمر المخصوص موضوعا والوجود المشترك فيه محمولا ، وإما أن يكون الأمر بالعكس منه . والأول هو المقصود . لأن على هذا التقدير تكون تلك الهوية المخصوصة المعينة موصوفة بالوجود، فيكون ذلك الوجود عارضا لماهيته ، والثاني باطل لوجهين : أحدهما : أنه ثبت في المنطق أن الأخص بالموضوعية والأعم أولى بالمحمولية . والثاني : وهو أنا لو جعلنا الوجود المطلق موضوعا ، وجعلنا تلك الخصوصية محمولة له ، لزم أن يقال : الوجود المشترك فيه هو تلك الخصوصية ، وحمل تلك الخصوصية عليه ، يمنع من كونه مشتركا فيه بينه وبين غيره ، فلزم أن يقال : الأمر المشترك فيه غير مشترك فيه ، وذلك محال . فثبت : أن القول بأن حقيقته وجود مجرد ، يفضي إلى أقسام باطلة فيكون القول به باطلا
The eleventh proof in establishing this desired conclusion: We say: There is no doubt that the Truth Most High is a specific essence distinguished by its specification from every other specific essence, and there is no doubt that existence, insofar as it is existence, is a reality in which the Necessary, the possible, substance, and accident share. So we say: His specific essence is either this common concept shared among all existents, or it is this concept together with an additional qualification that necessitates its specialization and specification. The first is false, because this common concept is predicated of being common to all, but His specific essence is not predicated of this meaning. Thus the statement that His specific essence is this common concept is false. It remains to be said that it is this concept with an additional qualification. We say: That additional qualification is either privative or positive. The first is false, because He, exalted, insofar as He is that specific one, is existent, and that for which specification occurs is part of the specified one. So if that for which specification occurs were a privative qualification, then the privative qualification would be part of the established essence, which is impossible. And if the qualification for which specification occurs is a positive thing, then either that specific thing is the subject and the common existence is the predicate, or the opposite. The first is the intended meaning, because on this assumption that specific specified identity is described by existence, so that existence is an accident of its essence. The second is false for two reasons: One: it is established in logic that the more specific is more deserving of subjecthood and the more general of predicatehood. The second: that if we make absolute existence the subject and make that specificity its predicate, then it would have to be said that common existence is that specificity, and predicating that specificity of it prevents it from being common between it and other things. Thus it would have to be said that the common thing is not common, which is impossible. So it is established that the statement that His essence is pure existence leads to false divisions, so the statement is false.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh proof that God's essence is not pure existence. The author begins by stating two premises: first, God is a specific essence distinct from all other essences; second, existence as such is a common concept shared by the Necessary, possible, substance, and accident. The argument then proceeds by dichotomy. God's specific essence is either the common concept of existence alone, or that concept plus an additional qualification. The first option is false because the common concept is predicated as common to all, while God's specific essence is not predicated in that way (it is unique). Thus the second option remains: God's essence is existence plus an additional qualification. The author then examines the nature of that qualification. It cannot be privative (negative) because then a privation would be part of God's essence, which is impossible. So it must be positive. The positive qualification is then analyzed in terms of subject-predicate relation. Either the specific quality is subject and existence is predicate (making existence an accident of the essence), or existence is subject and the specific quality is predicate. The first is what the author aims to prove. The second is false for two logical reasons: (1) the more specific should be subject and the more general predicate, but here the specific quality would be predicate; (2) if absolute existence is subject and the specific quality is predicate, then the predicate becomes identical to the subject, making existence no longer common, which is contradictory. Therefore, the claim that God's essence is existence alone is false, and it is established that existence is an attribute of God's specific essence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof denies that God exists, or that it reduces God's essence to a mere concept.
Correction: The proof does not deny that God exists; it denies that His essence is identical to the common concept of existence. It affirms that existence is a distinct attribute of a positive, specific essence.

Checkpoint

C) Existence is an attribute of God's essence.
D) God's specific essence is distinct from all other essences.

Key Takeaway
The eleventh proof establishes that God's specific essence is not the common concept of existence; rather, existence is a distinct attribute of a positive, specific essence.

God's Essence Is Distinct by Itself, Not by an Added Attribute
Introduces the fifth question and begins a proof against the majority view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two views on how God's essence is distinct from all other essences, and the beginnings of an argument against the majority view that difference requires an added attribute.

Core Idea
Difference by essence avoids infinite regress.

In this passage, the author begins a new discussion on a fundamental question: Is God's essence distinct from all contingent beings by itself, or by an added attribute? He first presents the majority view of the theologians, then states his own position. Before giving the full proof, he introduces a crucial premise and launches into the first argument, a dilemma that traps the majority view. Although the proof is cut off, the logical structure is already visible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the beginning of the passage carefully. The author presents the majority view (that essences are equal and become distinct by attributes) and his own view (that God's essence is distinct by itself). Mark these two positions clearly.
Notice how the author uses 'اعلم' to signal the exposition of the majority view.
2
Understand the premise
Before the proof, the author states a premise: a thing can differ from another by its very self, without needing an additional attribute. This is the key claim the proof will defend.
3
Trace the dilemma argument
The first proof is a dilemma. Follow the branches: if essences differ only by attributes, then those attributes must either differ from each other or not. If they don't differ, they cannot cause difference. If they do differ, then their difference must be either by other attributes (infinite regress) or by themselves. The proof stops at the latter branch.
This is a classic kalam reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the author's own view avoids the infinite regress. If the essence itself is different by its own self, no further attribute is needed to explain difference. This is the point of the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة
في
بيان آننه سبحانه يخالف جملة المكنات ، لذاته المخصوصة، لا لصفة زائدة على الذات
اعلم . أن جمهور المتكلمين يقولون : الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات وإنما يمتاز بعضها عن بعض بصفات قائمة بها ولما اعتقدوا ذلك ، قالوا : إنه سبحانه وتعالى ذات ، وإن الجسم أيضا ذات ، وامتياز ذاته عن ذوات الأجسام ، لا بد وأن يكون لأجل صفة اختص بها ذات الله تعالى ، ولأجل تلك الصفة امتازت ذاته عن سائر الذوات . والذي نقول به : أن ذاته سبحانه وتعالى ذات مفردة منزهة عن جميع جهات التركيب وأن تلك الذات لعينها ولذاتها، مخالفة لسائر الذوات، وقبل الخوض في الدليل لا بد من تقديم مقدمة وهي في بيان أن الشيء قد يخالف غيره لعينه ولذاته ، من غير حاجة إلى صفة زائدة ] (۱) والذي يدل على صحة ما ذكرناه وجوه :
الأول : إنا لو فرضنا كون الذوات متساوية في الذاتية ، وفرضنا أنه إنما يخالف بعضها بعضا لأجل الصفات القائمة بها، فتلك الصفات إما أن يكون بعضها مخالف للبعض ، أو لا يكون . والثاني باطل لأن تلك الصفات إذا لم تكن متخالفة ، استحال ضرورة أن تكون تلك الذوات متخالفة لأجلها ، وإن كانت تلك الصفات متخالفة فاختلافها إما أن يكون لصفات أخرى ، وحينئذ يلزم منه التسلسل ، وهو محال . وإن كان اختلافها لأنفسها ولأعيانها ، فحينئذ
The Fifth Question
Concerning the explanation that He, glorified and exalted, differs from all contingent beings by His specific essence, not by an attribute additional to the essence.
Know that the majority of theologians say: essences are equal in being essences, and they only become distinct from each other by attributes subsisting in them. And since they believed that, they said: He, glorified and exalted, is an essence, and the body is also an essence, and the distinction of His essence from the essences of bodies must be due to an attribute particular to the essence of God Almighty, and due to that attribute His essence became distinct from all other essences. What we say is: that His essence, glorified and exalted, is a singular essence free from all aspects of composition, and that essence by its very self and essence is different from all other essences. Before delving into the proof, a premise must be presented, which is an explanation that a thing can differ from another by its very self and essence, without needing an additional attribute. That which indicates the soundness of what we have mentioned are aspects:
The first: if we suppose that essences are equal in essentiality, and we suppose that they only differ from each other due to attributes subsisting in them, then those attributes are either some of them different from others, or not. The second is false because if those attributes are not different, it is necessarily impossible for those essences to become different due to them. And if those attributes are different, then their difference is either due to other attributes, in which case an infinite regress would result – which is impossible – or their difference is due to themselves and their own entities, then in that case...
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage begins a new question (the fifth) addressing whether God's essence is distinct from all contingent beings by itself or by an added attribute. The author first states the view of the majority of theologians: all essences are equal in being essences, and they become distinct only by attributes subsisting in them. Hence, they say God is an essence, a body is also an essence, and God's essence is distinct only by a particular attribute. The author's own position is that God's essence is singular, free from composition, and by its very self it is different from all other essences. Before providing the proof, the author introduces a premise: that a thing can differ from another by its own self, without an added attribute. Then the first proof is presented as a dilemma: if essences are equal and differ only by attributes, then those attributes must either differ from each other or not. If they do not differ, they cannot cause the essences to differ. If they do differ, then their difference must be either due to other attributes (leading to infinite regress, which is impossible) or due to themselves, in which case they differ by themselves—which supports the author's claim. The proof is cut off mid-sentence at the end of the page, but the argument is clear up to that point.

تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that for one thing to be different from another, there must always be an added attribute that causes the difference.
Correction: The author argues that this leads to an infinite regress unless the difference belongs to the thing itself. Hence, it is possible and necessary for God's essence to be distinct by itself.

Checkpoint

A) If attributes are not different from each other, they cannot make essences different.
B) God's essence is composed of parts.
C) The majority agree with the author's view.
D) Infinite regress is possible.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that if difference required an added attribute, then either the attributes cause no difference or an infinite regress ensues; therefore, difference can be intrinsic to the essence.

?
The proof shows that assuming difference by added attributes leads to infinite regress. In your own life, where do you rely on 'added attributes'—such as status, achievements, or religious practices—to define your identity before God, rather than resting in your fundamental contingency as His creature? What would it look like to let go of those and stand before Him as your bare essence?
?
The argument forces you to accept that God's essence is absolutely unique and incomparable. The Preamble: How a Thing Differs by Its Own Essence
Sanusi introduces the fifth issue and states the preamble that difference can be by essence alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether God's essence differs from contingent things by its own essence or by an additional attribute, and the necessity of a preamble establishing that difference by essence alone is possible.

Core Idea
Difference does not always require an extra attribute; essence alone can be the cause of distinction.

Sanusi begins the fifth issue by presenting two rival positions: the majority view that essences are distinguished only by attributes, and his own Ash'ari view that God's essence is unique by itself. Before providing the proof, he insists on a preamble to show that a thing can be different from another by its very essence. This lesson examines that foundational claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very clear contrast between the majority view and his own view.
2
Identify the two views
Locate the statement of the majority theologians (جمهور المتكلمين) and the author's own statement (والذي نقول به). Compare how each explains the distinction of essences.
The majority say essences are equal and distinguished by attributes; the author says difference can be by essence alone.
3
Understand the purpose of the preamble
Read the sentence that begins 'وقبل الخوض في الدليل لا بد من تقديم مقدمة'. Ask yourself why Sanusi thinks a preamble is necessary before giving the proof.
The preamble will show that it is possible for a thing to differ by its own essence, which is needed to support the author's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة في بيان أنه سبحانه يخالف جملة المكنات، لذاته المخصوصة، لا لصفة زائدة على الذات. اعلم أن جمهور المتكلمين يقولون: الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات وإنما يمتاز بعضها عن بعض بصفات قائمة بها، ولما اعتقدوا ذلك، قالوا: إنه سبحانه وتعالى ذات، وإن الجسم أيضا ذات، وامتياز ذاته عن ذوات الأجسام لا بد وأن يكون لأجل صفة اختص بها ذات الله تعالى، ولأجل تلك الصفة امتازت ذاته عن سائر الذوات. والذي نقول به: أن ذاته سبحانه وتعالى ذات مفردة منزهة عن جميع جهات التركيب، وأن تلك الذات لعينها ولذاتها مخالفة لسائر الذوات. وقبل الخوض في الدليل لا بد من تقديم مقدمة وهي في بيان أن الشيء قد يخالف غيره لعينه ولذاته، من غير حاجة إلى صفة زائدة.
The fifth issue: Concerning the explanation that He, glorified and exalted, differs from all contingent things by His specific essence, not by an attribute additional to the essence. Know that the majority of theologians say: essences are equal in being essences, and they are only distinguished from one another by attributes subsisting in them. When they held that, they said: He, glorified and exalted, is an essence, and the body is also an essence, and the distinction of His essence from the essences of bodies must be due to an attribute with which the essence of God, exalted, is specified, and due to that attribute His essence is distinguished from all other essences. But what we say is: that His essence, glorified and exalted, is a single essence, transcendent from all aspects of composition, and that that essence, by its very identity and essence, is different from all other essences. And before entering into the proof, it is necessary to present a preamble, which is an explanation that a thing may differ from another by its very identity and essence, without need for an additional attribute.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a preamble (مقدمة) that sets up the fifth issue: that God's essence differs from all contingent things by its own specific essence, not by an added attribute. The author first presents the view of the majority of theologians (جمهور المتكلمين) who hold that essences are equal in their essentiality and are distinguished only by attributes. Accordingly, they claim that God is an essence and bodies are essences, and the distinction of God's essence from that of bodies must be due to an attribute that God's essence possesses. The author then states the position he and his school hold: that God's essence is a single essence, transcendent from all composition, and that it is different from all other essences by its very identity and essence, not by an additional attribute. He then states that before the proof, a preamble is needed to establish that it is possible for a thing to differ from another by its own essence without needing an attribute. This preamble is necessary to show that difference by essence alone is possible.

Second Proof: The Argument from Essence and Attribute
Sanusi proves that the difference between essence and attribute is by themselves, not by an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the difference between Allah's essence and His attributes cannot be due to another attribute but must be by their own realities.

Core Idea
The difference between essence and attribute is intrinsic, not derived from another attribute.

In this second proof, Sanusi examines the relationship between Allah's essence (dhat) and His attributes (sifat). He argues that they must be distinct, and then shows that their distinction cannot be explained by an additional attribute, because that would lead to circularity. The conclusion is that their difference is by their very quiddities. This establishes an important principle for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the logical connectors: 'فثبت', 'فنقول', 'لأنا بينا'.
2
Understand the necessity of difference
Focus on the first part: why essence and attribute must be different. If they were identical in quiddity, the assignment of one as essence and the other as attribute would be arbitrary.
This is a reductio argument.
3
Examine the two possibilities for the cause of difference
The passage then asks whether the difference is due to an attribute or not. Understand the dichotomy.
4
See why the attribute-based explanation fails
The attribute explanation leads to circularity (dawr) because the attribution itself presupposes difference. This is the key move.
Circularity is a logical impossibility.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The only remaining option is that the difference is by their own realities (li-‘ayn haqiqatihima). This is what the proof sought to establish.
This conclusion supports the broader claim about attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في صحة هذا المطلوب: أن نقول: الذات والصفة لا بد وأن يكون كل واحدة منهما مخالفة للأخرى، إذ لو كانتا متساويتين في تمام الماهية، فحينئذ لم يكن كون إحداهما ذاتا والأخرى صفة أولى من العكس. فثبت أن الذات والصفة يجب كون كل واحدة منهما مخالفة للأخرى. فنقول: تلك المخالفة الحاصلة بينهما إما أن تكون لأجل صفة أو لا لأجل صفة؟ والأول باطل. لأنا بينا أن كون الذات موصوفة بالصفة مشروط يكون كل واحد منهما مخالفا للآخر. فلو عللنا كون كل واحدة منهما مخالفة للأخرى بقيام الصفة بها، لزم الدور وهو محال. فثبت: أن كون الذات والصفة مختلفين ليس معللا لشيء من الصفات، بل هو لعين حقيقتهما، ولنفس ماهيتهما. وذلك هو المطلوب.
The second proof for the correctness of this claim: we say: essence (dhat) and attribute (sifa) must be each one of them different from the other, because if they were equal in the entirety of quiddity (māhiyya), then it would not be more appropriate for one to be essence and the other to be attribute than the reverse. So it is established that essence and attribute must be each one different from the other. So we say: that difference occurring between them is either due to an attribute or not due to an attribute? The first is false, because we have shown that the essence being qualified by the attribute is conditioned upon each one being different from the other. So if we explain the difference of each from the other by the subsistence of an attribute in it, circularity (dawr) would follow, which is impossible. So it is established: that the difference of essence and attribute is not caused by any attribute, but rather by their identical reality and their very quiddity. That is what we sought.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is the second proof (الحجة الثانية). It proceeds by analysing the relation between essence and attribute. First, it establishes that essence and attribute must be different from each other, because if they were identical in quiddity, the distinction would be arbitrary. Then it asks whether the difference is due to an attribute or not. The first option is rejected because it leads to circularity: the essence's being qualified by an attribute already presupposes difference; explaining difference by the attribute would be a circular dependence. Therefore, the difference is by their own realities and quiddities. This supports the general claim.

Key Terms

الصفة
الماهية
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the difference between Allah's essence and His attributes is due to some other attribute that distinguishes them.
Correction: The proof shows that this leads to circularity; the difference is intrinsic to their realities.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible that the difference between essence and attribute is due to an attribute?
A) Because it would require infinite regress
B) Because it would lead to circularity
C) Because they are the same
D) Because attributes are not real

Key Takeaway
The difference between essence and attribute is not caused by any attribute but is inherent in their own realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof rejects circular reasoning. In your own faith, are there beliefs or practices where you rely on circular justifications (e.g., 'I pray because I am a Muslim') without grounding them in the intrinsic truths of tawḥīd? What would it take to break that cycle and anchor your actions directly in the reality of God's attributes?

The Second Proof: Difference Between Essence and Attribute is Intrinsic
Sanusi gives the second proof that the difference between essence and attribute is intrinsic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that the difference between essence and attribute is intrinsic, not due to an additional attribute.

Core Idea
The difference between essence and attribute is intrinsic, not caused by another attribute.

Sanusi presents the second proof for why essence and attribute must be fundamentally different. He argues that if the difference were due to an attribute, it would cause circular reasoning. Therefore, the distinction is by their very realities. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understanding the premise
Recognize that essence and attribute must be different from each other; otherwise the distinction would be arbitrary.
The proof starts from this necessary difference.
2
The question about the cause
Sanusi asks whether this difference is due to an attribute or not.
Two options are presented.
3
Rejection of attribute cause
See why attributing the difference to an attribute leads to circularity: the qualification already presupposes difference.
Circularity is impossible.
4
Conclusion: intrinsic difference
The difference is by the very reality and quiddity of each, not by an external cause.
This is the required conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في صحة هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : الذات والصفة لا بد وأن يكون كل واحدة منهما مخالفة للأخرى ، إذ لو كانتا متساويتين في تمام الماهية ، فحينئذ لم يكن كون إحداهما ذاتا والأخرى صفة أولى من العكس . فثبت أن الذات والصفة يجب كون كل واحدة منهما مخالفة للأخرى . فنقول : تلك المخالفة الحاصلة بينهما إما أن تكون لأجل صفة أو لا لأجل صفة؟ والأول باطل . لأنا بينا أن كون الذات موصوفة بالصفة مشروط يكون كل واحد منهما مخالفا للآخر . فلو عللنا كون كل واحدة منهما مخالفة للأخرى بقيام الصفة بها ، لزم الدور وهو محال . فثبت : أن كون الذات والصفة مختلفين ليس معللا لشيء من الصفات، بل هو لعين حقيقتهما ، ولنفس ماهيتهما . وذلك هو المطلوب
The second proof for the validity of this claim: that we say: The essence and the attribute must each be different from the other, for if they were equal in complete quiddity, then it would not be more appropriate for one to be an essence and the other an attribute than the reverse. So it is established that essence and attribute must each be different from the other. We say: That difference occurring between them is either due to an attribute or not due to an attribute? The first is invalid. Because we have shown that the essence being qualified by the attribute is conditioned upon each being different from the other. So if we were to attribute the fact that each is different from the other to the subsistence of the attribute in it, circularity would follow, which is impossible. So it is established that the being of essence and attribute as different is not caused by any attribute, but is by the very reality of both and by their own quiddity. And that is what was sought.

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that the difference between essence and attribute is intrinsic, not due to another attribute. The argument begins by stating the necessary difference between essence and attribute; if they were equal in quiddity, the distinction would be arbitrary. Since they are distinct, the difference must be real. The author then asks whether this difference is caused by an attribute. The first option is rejected because the qualification of the essence by an attribute already presupposes their difference, leading to circularity if the attribute is used to explain that difference. Therefore, the difference must be by the very reality and quiddity of each. This is the required conclusion.

The Third Proof: Similarity and Difference Are Intrinsic
Sanusi gives the third proof that similarity and difference are intrinsic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's third proof that similarity and difference between things arise from their essences themselves, not from extra attributes.

Core Idea
Similarity and difference are not added properties; they are in the very nature of things.

Sanusi continues his defense of essential attributes with a sharp logical analysis. He argues that when we say two things are similar in some respects and different in others, that is figurative language. In reality, every object is composed of parts, and similarity occurs between corresponding parts by their very essence, not by an extra property. This leads to the conclusion that similarity and difference are intrinsic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a condensed, precise style.
2
Identify the composition argument
Find the part where Sanusi says each thing is composed of two parts. Understand how this avoids contradiction.
3
Follow the conclusion
See how Sanusi deduces that similarity and difference are due to the essences themselves, not extra attributes.
This connects to the broader discussion on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في صحة هذا المطلوب : إنا إذا قلنا : إن هذا الشيء يماثل ذلك الشيء من بعض الوجوه ويخالفه من سائر الوجوه . فهذا كلام مجازي . وذلك لأن الوجه الذي لأجله حصلت المشاركة مغاير للوجه الذي لأجله حصلت المخالفة ، وإلا لزم اجتماع النقيضين في الشيء الواحد وهو محال . بل الحق أن كل واحد من هذين الشيئين مركب من جزءين. وأحد الجزءين من أحد الجانبين يماثل الجزء الآخر من الجانب الثاني مماثلة في حقيقتهما ومساواة في ماهيتهما والجزء الثاني من الجانب الأول يخالف الجزء الثاني من الجانب الثاني مخالفة تامة ، لنفس الحقيقتين . وإذا عرفت هذا فقد ظهر أن الشيئين اللذين تماثلا فإنما يتماثلان لتمام حقيقتهما ولنفس ماهيتهما ، لا لصفة زائدة ، وأن الشيئين اللذين يختلفان فإنما يختلفان لتمام حقيقتهما ولنفس ماهيتهما لا لصفة زائدة فظهر بما ذكرنا : أن التماثل أو الاختلاف لا يعقل أن يكون معللا بأمر زائد ، بل لا يحصلان إلا لنفس الماهية والحقيقة .
The third proof for the validity of this claim: that we say: If we say: 'This thing resembles that thing in some respects and differs from it in all other respects,' this is figurative speech. That is because the aspect by which similarity occurred is other than the aspect by which difference occurred, otherwise contradiction would follow in one thing, which is impossible. Rather, the truth is that each of these two things is composed of two parts. One of the two parts from one side resembles the other part from the other side in a complete resemblance in their reality and equality in their quiddity. And the second part from the first side differs from the second part from the other side in a complete difference, by the two realities themselves. And if you know this, it becomes apparent that the two things that resemble each other only resemble each other by the entirety of their reality and by their own quiddity, not by an extra attribute, and that the two things that differ only differ by the entirety of their reality and by their own quiddity, not by an extra attribute. So it has become apparent from what we have mentioned that resemblance or difference cannot be conceived to be caused by an extra thing, but they only occur by the very quiddity and reality.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, which analyzes statements about similarity and difference. The author argues that saying two things are similar in some respects and different in others is figurative; in reality, similarity and difference cannot come from the same aspect, as that would be contradictory. Instead, each thing is composed of parts: one part from the first is similar to a part from the second by their very realities, and another part from the first is different from the corresponding part from the second by their very realities. Hence, similarity and difference are not caused by extra attributes but by the things themselves. The conclusion is that these relations are intrinsic.

Key Terms
تماثل
اختلاف
وجه
مجازي
جزء
حقيقة
ماهية
صفة زائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similarity and difference can be partial due to the same aspect.
Correction: Sanusi shows that would cause contradiction; instead, composition avoids it.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why is it impossible for two things to be similar and different in the same respect?
A) Because it would cause circularity.
B) Because it would violate the law of non-contradiction.
C) Because essences are indivisible.
D) Because attributes are real.

Key Takeaway
Similarity and difference are not caused by extra attributes but by the very essences of things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the things you call 'similar' or 'different' in your daily life—your relationships, your actions, your beliefs. Does this proof challenge you to see those distinctions as rooted in the very nature of those things, not in accidental qualities? How would your judgments change if you took this insight seriously?

The Opponent's Argument: Why Distinction Seems to Require Attributes
Opponent's three proofs for attribute-necessity and partial reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's three arguments that essence differentiation requires attributes and the author's initial reply that the shared concept of essence may be negative.

Core Idea
Equal in essence does not force equal in reality; a negative concept can preserve distinction.

The author has argued that God's essence differs from all others by itself, not by an added attribute. Now the opponent pushes back with three logical proofs. Each proof tries to show that the very idea of distinction between essences must involve an attribute. As you read, notice how the opponent builds on the idea that essences share a single concept, and how the author begins his rebuttal by questioning the nature of that concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in.
2
Identify the three arguments
The opponent gives three numbered proofs. Underline the key claim of each: (1) from equality of essence concept, (2) from the nature of God's essence, (3) from our inability to define essences without attributes.
Notice that each argument ends with a conclusion that distinction must be by attribute.
3
Analyse the first argument's structure
The first argument has two 'maqam' (stations): first establishing that 'essence' is a single concept, second that equality in that concept forces distinction to come from a different concept. How does the opponent justify each?
The author's reply targets the first part by questioning whether the concept is positive or negative.
4
Study the author's reply (first argument)
The author concedes equality in concept but suggests the concept might be negative: 'something not subordinate to another'. Think about how a negative concept could allow essences to differ without needing an attribute.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the full reply is not given here.
5
Reflect on the controversy
Consider the key point: does sharing a concept imply sharing a reality? The opponent assumes yes, the author questions this. This is a foundational issue in how we understand essence and attribute.
This relates to the broader debate about divine attributes in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج المخالف على صحة قوله بوجوه:
الأول: إن الذوات بأسرها متساوية في كونها ذوات، فامتياز بعضها عن البعض لا بد وأن يكون لصفة. بيان المقام الأول: أنا نقول: الذات ما يكون مستقلاً بنفسه، والصفة لا تكون إلا بالغير. فيعقل من الذات هذا المفهوم الواحد، وذلك يدل على أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات. وبيان المقام الثاني: أنه لما وقع الاستواء في المفهوم من كونها ذوات وجب أن يحصل الامتياز بمفهوم غير ذلك المفهوم، ضرورة أن جهة المساواة غير جهة المخالفة.
والثاني: أن نقول: إن حقيقة ذات الله تعالى إما أن تكون هو أنه ذات فقط، أو تكون حقيقته أنه ذات ما معينة مخصوصة. فإن كان الأول لزم أن يكون كل ما يصدق عليه أنه ذات، يصدق عليه أنه هو الله سبحانه، وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ يكون المفهوم من ذلك التعين وتلك الخصوصية أمراً زائداً على المفهوم من كونه ذاتاً. وذلك يدل على أن امتياز ذاته تعالى عن سائر الذوات، لا بد وأن يكون بصفة.
والثالث: أن نقول: نحن نعلم بالضرورة أنه لا يمكننا تعريف ذات من الذوات المخالفة لسائر الذوات، إلا بأنه المختص بالصفة الفلانية، أو الموصوف بالأمر الفلاني، ولأجل اختصاصها بتلك الصفة، أو بذلك الأمر وقع الامتياز. وذلك يدل على مخالفة بعض الذوات لبعض، معللة بالصفات.
والجواب عن الأول: إنا لا ننازع في أن الذوات متساوية في المفهوم من كونها ذوات. لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك المفهوم مفهوماً سلبياً، ويكون معناه أنه أمر ما ليس تابعاً لغيره في الحصول والتحقق؟ وقد عرفت في كتب
The opponent argued for the validity of his statement with [several] aspects:
First: All essences are equal in being essences, so the distinction of some from others must be due to an attribute. Explanation of the first part: we say: the essence (dhāt) is what is independent by itself, while the attribute (ṣifa) is only by another. So this one concept is understood from essence, and that indicates that essences are equal in being essences. Explanation of the second part: since equality in the concept of being essences occurred, distinction must be by a concept other than that concept, necessarily because the aspect of equality is other than the aspect of difference.
Second: We say: the reality of the essence (dhāt) of Allah Most High is either that it is only an essence, or its reality is that it is a particular specified essence. If the first, then it would be necessary that everything of which it is true that it is an essence, it would be true that it is Allah the Glorified. If the second, then the concept from that specification and particularity is something additional to the concept of its being an essence. And that indicates that the distinction of His essence from other essences must be by an attribute.
Third: We say: we know necessarily that we cannot define an essence among essences that is different from other essences except by saying that it is the one specifically associated with such-an-attribute, or described by such-an-affair; and because of its specificity by that attribute or that affair, the distinction occurred. And that indicates that the difference of some essences from others is caused by attributes.
The answer to the first: We do not dispute that essences are equal in the concept of being essences. But why is it not permissible that that concept is a negative concept, and its meaning is that it is something not subordinate to another in obtaining and actualization? And you have known in books...
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents an opponent's objection to the author's earlier conclusion that God's essence differs from all other essences by itself, not by an added attribute. The opponent gives three arguments. The first argument: All essences share the common concept of being 'dhāt' (independent entity). Since they are equal in that concept, any distinction must come from something else, namely an attribute. The opponent splits this into two parts: first, establishing that 'dhāt' is a single concept; second, arguing that equality in that concept forces distinction to come from a different concept. The second argument: God's essence is either just 'an essence' (a mere universal) or a specific essence. If just an essence, then every essence would be God, which is false; if specific, then the specification is an additional concept, i.e., an attribute. The third argument: We can only define a distinct essence by its special attributes, proving that distinction depends on attributes. The author then begins his reply to the first argument only (the page ends here). He concedes that essences share the same concept of being 'dhāt', but questions whether that concept must be positive. He suggests it might be a negative concept: 'something not subordinate to another'. If so, then equality in a negative concept does not preclude differentiation by essence itself, without needing attributes. The answer is incomplete, but it opens a key move: undermining the opponent's assumption that the shared concept is a real positive commonality.

Key Terms
ذات (dhāt)
صفة (ṣifa)
مفهوم سلبي (negative concept)
امتياز (distinction)
مخالفة (difference)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that a shared concept (like 'essence') implies that all instances are identical in reality and any difference must come from an external addition.
Correction: A shared concept may be merely a negative or relational notion, which does not necessitate equality in the actual quiddity; thus essences can differ by themselves.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument of the opponent in this passage?
A) Essences are equal in being essences, so distinction must come from an attribute.
B) God's essence is unknowable.
C) Attributes are eternal like the essence.
D) The author's proof is circular.

Key Takeaway
Equality in a shared concept does not automatically require distinction by attribute if that concept is negative; the nature of the concept itself is a crucial point of contention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the concept of essence might be purely negative—simply 'not dependent'—does your worship of Allah rely more on what He is not (incomprehensible, unlimited) or on the attributes you affirm? Which gives you greater closeness, and why?
?
The opponent argued that to distinguish God, we need attributes. But can you personally conceive of God as pure existence without any positive descriptions? Try to hold that thought for one minute. Does it feel empty or liberating? What does your reaction reveal about your own need for attributes?
?
If Allah's essence is distinguished from all other essences by itself alone, not by any attribute, does that change how you think about the names and attributes you recite? Are they truly 'other' than His essence, or merely ways for you to grasp the ungraspable? Commit to one specific name this week and contemplate it as a window to the essence.

The Opponent's First Proof: Essences and Attributes
Opponent's first proof for distinction by attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the opponent's argument that essences require attributes for distinction.

Core Idea
Equality in concept demands a separate attribute for distinction.

The opponent begins by asserting that all essences share the same concept of being essences. Since they are equal in that concept, any difference must come from an external attribute. This lesson presents that argument in detail, including its two key stations.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the overall claim that distinction requires an attribute.
The argument has two clear stages (stations).
2
Analyze the first station
The first station defines essence (ذات) as independent and attribute (صفة) as dependent. From this, the opponent concludes all essences are equal in being essences.
Equality is based on a shared positive concept.
3
Analyze the second station
The second station argues that since equality is in the concept, distinction must be by a different concept, because equality and difference are different aspects.
The opponent assumes that the shared concept cannot be the source of difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن الذوات بأسرها متساوية في كونها ذوات ، فامتياز بعضها عن البعض لا بد وأن يكون لصفة . بيان المقام الأول : أنا نقول : الذات ما يكون مستقلا بنفسه ، والصفة لا تكون إلا بالخير . فيعقل من الذات هذا المفهوم الواحد ، وذلك يدل على أن الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات . وبيان المقام الثاني : أنه لما وقع الاستواء في المفهوم من كونها ذوات وجب أن يحصل الامتياز بمفهوم غير ذلك المفهوم ، ضرورة أن جهة المساواة غير جهة المخالفة . والجواب عن الأول : إنا لا ننازع في أن الذوات متساوية في المفهوم من كونها ذوات. لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك المفهوم مفهوما سلبيا ، ويكون معناه أنه أمر ما ليس تابعا لغيره في الحصول والتحقق ؟ وقد عرفت في كتب المنطق : أن الاستواء في المفهوم ، لا يوجب الاستواء في الماهية ، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يبعد استواء الذوات في هذا المفهوم السلبي ، مع أنه يكون كل واحد منهما ممتازا عن الآخر بتمام حقيقته . والذي يقوي هذا الذي ذكرناه : أن الدليل الذي ذكرتم في كون الذوات متساوية في المفهوم من كونها ذوات ، فهو أيضا بعينه يدل على أن الصفات متساوية في المفهوم من كونها صفات . وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن لا يحصل الاختلاف البتة ، لأن الاختلاف لو حصل الحصل إما في الذوات ، وإما في الصفات، وعلى التقديرين فالاختلاف مفقود ، فوجب أن لا يحصل الاختلاف البتة ، وحيث حصل ، علمنا أن ما ذكرتم من الدليل ساقط
The first: that the essences in their entirety are equal in being essences, so the distinction of some from others must be by an attribute. Explanation of the first station: we say: the essence is that which is independent by itself, and the attribute is only through another. So this single concept is understood from essence, and that indicates that essences are equal in being essences. And explanation of the second station: that when equality occurred in the concept of being essences, it is necessary that distinction occurs by a concept other than that concept, necessarily because the aspect of equality is not the aspect of difference. And the answer to the first: we do not dispute that essences are equal in the concept of being essences. But why is it not permissible that that concept be a negative concept, and its meaning be that it is something that is not dependent on another in existence and actuality? And you have known in the books of logic that equality in concept does not necessitate equality in quiddity. On this assumption, it is not far-fetched that essences are equal in this negative concept, while each of them is distinguished from the other by its complete reality. And what strengthens what we have mentioned: that the proof you mentioned concerning essences being equal in the concept of being essences — it also, in the same way, indicates that attributes are equal in the concept of being attributes. If that is the case, then difference must not occur at all, because if difference occurred, it would occur either in essences or in attributes; on both assumptions, difference is non-existent, so difference must not occur at all. And since it does occur, we know that the proof you mentioned is invalid.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's first proof that essences must be distinguished by attributes, followed by the author's response. The opponent argues: all essences share the concept 'essence'; to differentiate, an additional attribute is necessary. The response concedes equality in concept but argues that the shared concept might be a negative one (i.e., 'not dependent on another'), which does not preclude real difference in quiddity. The response further uses a tu quoque: the opponent's logic would also force attributes to be equal in concept, leading to the absurd conclusion that no difference can exist anywhere. Since differences are observed, the opponent's proof is invalid.

ذات
صفة
امتياز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the opponent's claim about all essences?
A) They are completely different in reality.
B) They are equal in the concept of being essences.
C) They are distinguished by their essence alone.
D) They have no common concept.

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that because all essences share the same concept, distinction requires an external attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all human beings are equal in being created essences, what attribute truly distinguishes you in the sight of Allah? Are you more than your physical existence? What attribute are you actively cultivating that sets you apart in faith and righteousness?
?
The opponent's argument states that distinction requires a different concept. Consider your own soul: are you defined by your essence (your mere existence) or by the attributes of your character and actions? What does your life reveal about which you prioritize?
?
The passage teaches that the aspect of equality is not the aspect of difference. In your daily life, do you treat people solely based on their shared humanity (equality) or do you recognize the distinct attributes that Allah has given each person? How can you balance seeing others as equal in essence yet unique in attributes, without falling into pride or discrimination?

Refuting the Proof: Negative Concepts and Logical Inconsistency
Response to the opponent's first proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the opponent's proof using the idea of a negative concept and a tu quoque argument.

Core Idea
The shared concept of essence may be negative, and the opponent's logic leads to absurdity.

Having presented the opponent's argument, the author now offers a two-part refutation. First, the common concept might be negative, implying nothing about real distinction. Second, the same logic would force attributes to be equal, preventing any difference at all—contradicting observable reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the response
Read the full response, noting the two distinct refutations: the negative concept and the tu quoque.
The response begins by conceding equality in concept.
2
Understand the negative concept refutation
The author suggests that the shared concept 'essence' could be a negative concept, meaning 'not dependent on another', which does not imply real identity.
This is supported by the logical principle that equality in concept does not entail equality in quiddity.
3
Examine the tu quoque refutation
The author shows that the opponent's own proof would also conclude that attributes are equal in concept, leading to the absurd result that no difference exists anywhere, which contradicts reality.
This demonstrates the proof is invalid.

Second and Third Proofs for Distinction by Attributes
Two further proofs for distinction by attributes and the joint answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's second and third proofs that distinction of God's essence requires an attribute, and the author's joint refutation that difference does not imply an extrinsic attribute.

Core Idea
Difference between essences does not automatically require an additional attribute.

The opponent continues to argue that God's essence must be distinguished by an attribute. The second proof presents a dilemma about the reality of God's essence, while the third proof appeals to our inability to define essences without attributes. Al-Sanusi responds with a joint refutation based on earlier proofs that difference can be intrinsic. Let's examine these arguments closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the two proofs and the answer.
Notice the structure: second proof, third proof, then a common answer.
2
Analyze the second proof
The second proof is a dilemma: either God's essence is just 'essence' (leading to pantheism) or it includes specification (implying an attribute). Understand the two horns.
The opponent concludes that specification must be an added attribute.
3
Analyze the third proof
The third proof argues that we can only identify an essence by its unique attribute, so distinction requires an attribute. See how this builds on the idea of definition.
Both proofs aim to show that attributes are needed for distinction.
4
Study the joint answer
The author refutes both by referring to earlier proofs that difference does not require an additional attribute. Understand why this refutation works.
The key is that distinction can be intrinsic to the essence itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أن نقول : إن حقيقة ذات الله تعالى إما أن تكون هو أنه ذات فقط ، أو تكون حقيقته أنه ذات ما معينة مخصوصة . فإن كان الأول لزم أن يكون كل ما يصدق عليه أنه ذات ، يصدق عليه أنه هو الله سبحانه ، وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ يكون المفهوم من ذلك التعين وتلك الخصوصية أمرا زائدا على المفهوم من كونه ذاتا . وذلك يدل على أن امتياز ذاته تعالى عن سائر الذوات ، لا بد وأن يكون بصفة . والثالث : أن نقول : نحن نعلم بالضرورة أنه لا يمكننا تعريف ذات من الدوات المخالفة لسائر الذوات ، إلا بأنه المختص بالصفة الفلانية ، أو الموصوف بالأمر الفلاني ، ولأجل اختصاصها بتلك الصفة ، أو بذلك الأمر وقع الامتياز . وذلك يدل على مخالفة بعض الذوات لبعض ، معللة بالصفات . والجواب عن الثاني : إنا بينا بالدلائل الثلاثة في مقدمة هذه المسألة : أنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء مخالفا لشيء آخر ، أن تكون تلك المخالفة واقعة لأجل صفة زائدة ، وإذا تقررت هذه المقدمة سقطت تلك الشبهة . وهذا هو الجواب بعينه من الشبهة الثالثة
And the second: that we say: the reality of the essence of God, may He be exalted, is either that it is only an essence, or its reality is that it is a particular specified essence. If it is the first, then it would be necessary that everything of which it is true that it is an essence, it is also true that it is God, may He be glorified. And if it is the second, then the concept of that specification and that particularity is something additional to the concept of its being an essence. And that indicates that the distinction of His essence, may He be exalted, from other essences must be by an attribute. And the third: that we say: we know by necessity that we cannot define an essence that is different from other essences except that it is the one specifically characterized by such-and-such an attribute, or described by such-and-such a thing, and because of its being specifically characterized by that attribute, or by that thing, distinction occurs. And that indicates that the difference of some essences from others is caused by attributes. And the answer to the second: we have shown by three proofs in the introduction of this issue that it does not follow from something being different from another thing that that difference occurs because of an additional attribute. And when this premise is established, that doubt is refuted. And this is the very answer to the third doubt.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage contains two more proofs from the opponent and the author's joint answer. The second proof considers God's essence: either it is just 'essence' (leading to pantheism) or it includes specification (implying an attribute). The opponent argues that specification is an additional attribute. The third proof argues that we can only identify an essence by its unique attribute, so distinction requires an attribute. The author's answer refers to earlier demonstrations (three proofs in the introduction of the issue) that a thing can differ from another without an extrinsic attribute. The same answer applies to both the second and third doubts.

تعين
خصوصية
مخالفة
صفة زائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's dilemma about God's essence is compelling and forces the conclusion that distinction requires an attribute.
Correction: The author denies that specification must be an extrinsic attribute; difference can be intrinsic to the essence itself.

Checkpoint
What is the common answer to both the second and third proofs?
A) Essences cannot be distinguished at all
B) Difference does not require an additional attribute
C) God's essence is unknowable
D) Attributes are eternal

Key Takeaway
The opponent's proofs fail because difference between essences need not be caused by an extrinsic attribute; it can be due to the essence itself.

Essences Share a Negative Attribute
Summary of the correct doctrine on essence and attribute distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's resolution of the debate on whether essences share a common attribute and how the concept of a negative necessary concomitant provides a solution.

Core Idea
Different essences can share a single negative attribute without contradiction.

This passage concludes the discussion on essences and attributes. The author clarifies that while each essence has its unique reality, they all share a necessary but privative property—being an essence. This subtle distinction resolves an apparent inconsistency in kalam reasoning about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: first the author's view, then the opponents' view.
The passage uses contrasting keywords like 'نقول' and 'يقولون'.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Find where the author states his position and where he presents the opponents' argument. Note the key difference: shared negative vs positive distinct attributes.
3
Understand 'negative necessary concomitant'
The phrase 'لازم سلبي' is crucial. Explain that it means a necessary property that is privative (like independence) rather than positive. Discuss why different realities can share this.
Contrast with the opponents' claim that equality prevents different concomitants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن حاصل الكلام في هذه المسألة أنا نقول : كل واحدة من الذوات تخالف غيرها، في كونها تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة ، إلا أن هذه الحقائق المختلفة ، يلزمها لازم واحد سلبي ، وهو كونها ذوات . والأشياء المختلفة لا يمتنع اشتراكها في لازم سلبي . أما الخصوم فإنهم يقولون : الذوات متساوية في كونها ذوات إلا أن كل واحدة منها يلزمها صفة معينة لأجلها ممتاز عن غيرها وذلك محال . لأن الأشياء المتساوية يمتنع أن يلزمها لوازم مختلفة . فهذا تمام هذا الكلام في هذا الباب وبالله التوفيق
And know that the gist of the discussion in this issue is that we say: each one of the essences differs from others in being that specific reality, except that these different realities are accompanied by a single negative necessary concomitant, which is their being essences. And different things are not impossible to share in a negative necessary concomitant. As for the opponents, they say: essences are equal in being essences, except that each one of them is accompanied by a specific attribute by which it is distinguished from others, and that is impossible, because equal things are impossible to be accompanied by different concomitants. This is the end of this discussion in this chapter, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage summarizes the author's position on the relationship between essences and attributes. The author states that essences are distinguished by their specific realities, but they all share a single negative necessary concomitant: being essences (i.e., not dependent entities). Different realities can share a negative attribute without contradiction. The opponents, however, claim that because essences are equal in being essences, any distinction must come from a positive attribute, but that leads to impossibility since equal things cannot have different attributes. The author's view avoids that problem by positing a negative common attribute.

Key Terms
لازم سلبي
حقيقة
حقائق مختلفة
ممتاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A negative attribute means nonexistence or absence of any attribute.
Correction: Here 'negative attribute' means a privative concept like independence or self-subsistence, not nonexistence. It is a real property but defined by negation (e.g., not dependent).

Checkpoint
According to the author, what do different essences share?
A) A positive attribute that distinguishes each
B) Their specific reality
C) A single negative necessary concomitant
D) Nothing at all

Key Takeaway
Different essences can share a single negative necessary concomitant (being essences) while differing in their specific realities, thus resolving the debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your identity is threatened when you share something in common with others? This lesson shows that sharing a negative attribute (like being a created being) does not erase your unique reality. Can you remember a time you resisted commonality out of fear of losing your individuality? What would it look like for you to embrace that shared attribute while still celebrating your uniqueness?
?
The opponents assumed that equal essences must have equal attributes. In your own life, do you sometimes assume that if someone shares a characteristic with you, you must be the same in other ways? How does this lesson expose that assumption as flawed?
?
If you were stripped of all your positive qualities—intelligence, wealth, appearance—what would remain? According to this lesson, you would still have the negative attribute of 'being an essence', a self. How does this affect your understanding of your own worth and your reliance on Allah, who alone is truly independent?

The Ash'ari Argument for God's Everlastingness
The author presents the Ash'ari argument for everlastingness via three divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that what is proven eternal cannot cease to exist, including the enumeration of three possible causes for non-existence and the reasoning that non-existence must be contingent.

Core Idea
An eternal being cannot cease to exist because all possible causes of non-existence are invalid.

Having established God's eternity, the Ash'ari theologians now argue that what is eternal cannot become non-existent. They systematically consider every possible way something could cease to exist after existing and then show each way is impossible for God. This passage lays out the three divisions that exhaust all possibilities and begins the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the context
The previous part of the text proved God's eternity (qidam). Here the author turns to proving His everlastingness (baqa'). Read the opening sentence that connects the two proofs.
2
Identify the three divisions
Look for the three possible causes of non-existence after existence: annihilation by an agent, occurrence of an opposite, or absence of a condition. Memorize them.
These are exhaustive according to the Ash'aris.
3
Understand the contingency of non-existence
The author argues that non-existence of an eternal being must be contingent, not necessary. Follow why necessary non-existence would imply perpetual non-existence, contradicting eternity.
4
Trace the preponderator argument
A contingent non-existence requires a preponderator (murajjih). See how the preponderator is either non-existent (absence of condition) or existent (choice or necessitation).
This links back to the three divisions.
5
Notice the structure of the proof
The author promises two clarifications: enumeration of divisions and invalidation of each. The text gives the full enumeration but only begins the invalidation. Be aware that the passage ends mid-sentence.
The invalidation will be detailed in later sections.

فهذا تمام الكلام في حكاية كلام المتكلمين في إثبات كونه تعالى قديما أزليا . وأما الوجه الذي تمسكوا به في إثبات كونه باقيا أبديا . فهو أنهم قالوا : ثبت بما ذكرنا كونه تعالى قديما أزليا . فنقول : ما ثبت قدمه ، فإنه يمتنع عدمه . واحتجوا على صحة هذه المقدمة بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : وهي الحجة التي عليها تعويل الأشعرية . أن قالوا : هذا القديم لو عدم بعد وجوده ، فإما أن يكون عدمه لإعدام معدم أو لطريان ضد ، أو لانتفاء شرط ، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة . فالقول بعدم القديم باطل فيفتقر ههنا إلى بيان أمرين : أحدهما : بيان حصر الأقسام ، والثاني : إفساد كل واحد منها . أما بيان الحصر فهو أنا نقول : كل شيء عدم بعد وجوده ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون ذلك العدم جائزا . إذ لو كان ذلك العدم واجبا لذاته ، لكان ذلك العدم دائما ، ولو كان العدم دائما ، لما كان (۲) الوجود أزليا ، وإذا كان ذلك العدم أمرا جائزا ، وجب أن يكون له مرجح ، وذلك المرجح إما أن يكون عدميا أو وجوديا ، فإن كان عدميا فهو أن يقال : إنه إنما عدم لأجل أنه عدم شيء. كان ذلك الشيء محتاجا في وجوده إليه ، وهذا هو الذي قلنا : إنه ينعدم لانتفاء شرط ، وإما إن كان المقتضى لذلك العدم أمراً وجودياً فتأثيره في ذلك الإعدام ، وأما أن يكون على سبيل الاختيار (۳) وهو الذي سميناه الإعدام بالفاعل ، أو على سبيل الإيجاب ، وهو الذي سميناه بالعدم لأجل طريان الضد ، فيثبت بما ذكرنا : بيان حصر هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ] (٤) وأما بيان فساد كل واحد من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة فهو أن نقول : إنما قلنا : إنه يستحيل أن يصير معدوما لأجل أن معدما أعدمه لوجهين :
This concludes the account of the theologians' speech in proving His being eternal and beginningless. As for the aspect they adhered to in proving His being everlasting and perpetual, it is that they said: It is established by what we have mentioned that God is eternal and beginningless. We say: What has been proven to be eternal, its non-existence is impossible. And they argued for the soundness of this premise with proofs. The first proof, which is the one upon which the Ash'aris rely, is that they said: This eternal being, if it should cease after its existence, then its non-existence would be either by the action of an annihilator, or by the occurrence of an opposite, or by the absence of a condition. And these three divisions are invalid. So the claim of non-existence of the eternal is invalid. So here one needs two clarifications: one, clarification of the enumeration of divisions; second, invalidating each of them. As for the clarification of the enumeration, we say: Everything that ceases after its existence, that non-existence must be contingent. For if that non-existence were necessary in itself, then that non-existence would be perpetual; and if non-existence were perpetual, then existence would not be beginningless. And when that non-existence is contingent, it must have a preponderator. That preponderator is either non-existent or existent. If it is non-existent, then it is said that it ceased because something ceased, which that thing was needed in its existence for it—this is what we called ceasing by absence of a condition. If the cause of that non-existence is an existent thing, then its effect in that annihilation is either by way of choice—which we called annihilation by an agent—or by way of necessitation—which we called ceasing due to occurrence of an opposite. Thus what we have mentioned establishes the clarification of these three divisions. And as for the clarification of the invalidity of each of these three divisions, we say: We only say that it is impossible for it to become non-existent because an annihilator would annihilate it, for two reasons:
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the proof for God's everlastingness (abadiyyah) based on the Ash'ari argument. The author first states that the theologians have proven God's eternity (qidam), and now they argue that what is proven eternal cannot cease to exist. They provide a first proof enumerating three possible causes for non-existence after existence: (1) annihilation by an agent, (2) occurrence of an opposite, (3) absence of a condition. The argument then explains that the non-existence of an eternal being must be contingent, not necessary, and thus requires a preponderator. The preponderator is either non-existent (absence of condition) or existent (choice or necessitation). The passage then says that all three divisions are invalid, but the text ends before detailing the invalidation of each, as the page ends mid-sentence. The author promises two clarifications: enumeration of divisions and refutation of each.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Ash'ari argument simply asserts impossibility without justification.
Correction: The argument systematically enumerates all possible causes of non-existence and then demonstrates each is impossible for the eternal being, as shown in the upcoming refutation.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari proof, what are the three possible causes for a thing's non-existence after it exists?
A) The thing's own choice, God's will, or natural decay.
B) Annihilation by an agent, occurrence of an opposite, or absence of a condition.
C) Time, space, or matter.
D) The thing's essence, its attributes, or its actions.

Key Takeaway
An eternal being cannot cease to exist because all possible causes of non-existence are logically invalid for it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that God's existence is so necessary that non-existence is impossible for Him. But your own existence is entirely contingent—you could have never been, and you will one day cease. Does the reality of God's eternal permanence make you feel secure, or does it expose the fragility of your life? What would you change today if you truly lived with that awareness?
?
The passage argues that an eternal being cannot be annihilated by anything. Reflect: are there things in your life—possessions, relationships, status—that you treat as if they were permanent, as if they could never cease? How does the absolute permanence of God challenge your attachment to these temporary things?
?
If you genuinely believed that God will remain forever while everything else passes away, what fear or worry in your life would be exposed as irrational? Will you take a concrete step this week to align your priorities with the only reality that truly lasts—God Himself?
